FAMILY  Lentariaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Lentaria pinicola  (Burt) R.H. Petersen^  J. Elisha Mitchell Sci. Soc. 1974;  Ramaria pinicola  (Burt) Corner (proposed current name);  Clavaria pinicola  Burt;  Clavaria stillingeri  Coker
ENGLISH NAMES  white-tipped coral
NOTES  Lentaria pinicola group produces small fruitbodies that are repeatedly branched, the branches light yellow to pale tan or bronze, any part turning darker brown with handling or aging, the tips pale yellow when young. A stem is sometimes present. It arises from a broad basal mat and a tangle of extensive white rhizomorphs, on rotting conifer wood, often clustered or in tufts. Spores are smooth, and there are monomitic hyphae in the basal mat with short sharp spines.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed September 4, 2017, gives Ramaria pinicola (Burt) Corner as the current name but MycoBank, accessed the same day gave both names as equivalent. RANGE Distribution includes central and western North America and the type collection is from ID, (Petersen). It is common in the woods of western WA (Marr). There are collections from BC (as Ramaria pinicola) at the University of British Columbia, and from WA, OR, and CA, (as Ramaria pinicola) at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  up to 4.5cm high, repeatedly branched, "axils somewhat flattened, narrowly rounded", "internodes 0.2-1.5cm long, diminishing abruptly, and often in only a single rank", tips very acute to almost flagelliform, round in cross-section, minute, (Petersen), 1.5-7.5cm high, tips pointed or toothed, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  leathery; paler than surface^, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  variable from light yellow to pale tan or bronze, any part bruising darker brown with handling or aging, tips pale yellow when young, aging the same color as the branches^, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  usually clustered and stem not visible, occasionally present, single, slender, similar in color to branches or browner; fruitbody arising from white rhizomorphs up to 0.2cm wide and surrounded by white cottony threads^, (Scates-Barnhart), stems almost absent, or fruitbodies from slender stems arising from broad basal mat and gregarious to cespitose, attached tenuously to rhizomorphic strands and basal mat; basal mat broad, byssoid to stringy, disappearing into a tangle of extensive white rhizomorphic strands permeating the woody substrate, (Petersen)
ODOR  earthy^ (Scates-Barnhart), faintly earthy (Marr)
TASTE  slightly bitter^ (Marr, Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  inedible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  usually on rotting coniferous wood, often smaller pieces, fall^, (Scates-Barnhart), type on rotten Pinus contorta, (Petersen), growing on small branches, twig litter or cones under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) and Douglas-fir, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.7-9.3 x 4.5-5.7 microns, elliptic to subovoid, smooth (without ornamentation), contents homogeneous, wall up to 0.4 microns wide, somewhat thinner at distal end, apiculus prominent, eccentric, and rounded-truncate^; hyphae of basal mat 2.5-5.5 microns wide, colorless, conspicuously clamped, thin-walled to somewhat thick-walled (up to 1 micron thick), often ornamented with small sharp cyanophilic scattered spines, inflated clamp connections common, hyphae of rhizomorphic strands also ornamented, (Petersen), 8-12 x 4-6 microns, clamp connections present, rhizomorphic strands monomitic, (Scates-Barnhart), spores 8-12 x 4-6 microns, average 9.2 x 4.8 microns, subcylindric to oval, smooth, slightly thick-walled, contents homogeneous or one or two droplets, wall and content cyanophilic; basidia 2-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 40-69 x 7-11 microns, clavate, clamped, contents granular and cyanophilic; clamp connections present; rhizomorphic strands monomitic, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'living on pine'
SIMILAR  Lentaria epichnoa has narrower spores. Lentaria byssiseda is a whitish to pinkish-tan species that often grows on wood, few-branched or many-branched, often with green-tinged branch tips, arising from a small or spreading white mycelial patch and often with rhizomorphs: spores measure 10-18 x 3-6 microns, (Corner(2)). It is mentioned by Arora for California, but Corner(3) synonymizes it with L. soluta and expresses doubts about the independence of L. soluta from L. surculus. See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria gracilis and Ramaria stricta var. stricta.
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Scates-Barnhart(1), Marr(1) (as Ramaria pinicola), Arora(1) (discussing Lentaria byssiseda), Corner(2) (discussing L. byssiseda), Corner(3) (discussing Lentaria soluta and L. byssiseda), Kornerup(2), Siegel(2)* (as Lentaria pinicola group)
15
FAMILY  Clavulinaceae, Order Cantharellales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Multiclavula mucida  (Fr.) R.H. Petersen^  Am. Midl. Nat. 77: 212. 1967;  Lentaria mucida  (Fr.) Corner  Ann. Bot. Mem. 1: 442. 1950;  Clavaria mucida  Fr.;  Clavaria alba  Lloyd;  Clavaria coronilla  G.W. Martin;  Lentaria coronilla  (G.W. Martin) Corner;  Multiclavula coronilla  (G.W. Martin) R.H. Petersen
ENGLISH NAMES  white green-algae coral, scum-lover
NOTES  Also listed in Clubs category. Multiclavula mucida produces gregarious, small, spindle-shaped to cylindric, whitish to pale yellowish waxy fruiting bodies that often age salmon to brick-red, on rotten logs that are covered with green algae.^ Corner(3) comments that structurally it is an unbranched Lentaria, but its association with algae appears to be with the phycophilous species of Clavulinopsis which Petersen placed in Multiclavula. It was found in Oregon by Doty according to Corner(2) as var. rosea (white below, rose in the middle, pale rose at the apex), although he says var. rosea is possibly a form of Clavulinopsis vernalis, and Petersen(27) synonymizes the variety with Multiclavula corynoides. RANGE Brodo(1) say that it is "an eastern to boreal species". Multiclavula mucida is widely distributed but uncommon in the USA, Canada, Panama, Columbia, Sweden, France, Russia (Siberia), Japan, and Australia, (Corner(2)). It has been found in Jamaica (Corner(3), who synonymizes Multiclavula coronilla (G.W. Martin) R.H. Petersen and Clavaria alba Lloyd with Lentaria mucida (Fr.) Corner). It is found in CA (Arora), and northern North America, especially in Northeast, (Lincoff). It is included in "Mushrooms of Idaho and the Pacific Northwest" by Tylutki(1) with a description that fits well for the species. The University of British Columbia has one collection determined by Paul Kroeger as Multiclavula mucida from BC.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  0.5-1.5cm high, 0.1-0.15cm wide, simple or rarely forked, cylindric to spindle-shaped, narrowing toward base; white to sometimes yellowish or cream^, (Lincoff), 0.5-1.5cm high, 0.1-0.2cm wide, cylindric or tapered; white to creamy, buff, or yellowish, but often aging salmon to brick-red; smooth, (Arora), 0.3-2cm high, 0.1cm thick, slender, mostly simple, but sometimes forked into 2-6 linear curved ascending branches, or incised or minutely cristate (crested) at the top, "solid, acute, rarely clavate-obtuse, cylindric fusiform, attenuate into a scarcely distinct stem"; delicate but tough, not breaking on bending, waxy-tough; (Corner(2)), spindle-shaped to cylindric or tine-like, usually unbranched; smooth or longitudinally wrinkled, (Bessette)
FLESH  tough, waxy, pliant; white^, (Arora), delicate but tough, not breaking on bending, waxy-tough, (Corner(2))
BRANCH COLOR  "white, yellowish, pale cream, or even pinkish, the apex sometimes becoming lateritious, brownish or blackish"^, (Corner(2)), white to creamy white or pale yellow, (Bessette)
STEM  indistinct^, (Lincoff), narrowing into a scarcely distinct stem, (Corner(2))
ODOR  none^ (Corner(2)), not distinctive (Miller)
TASTE  woody^ (Lincoff, Corner(2)), not distinctive (Miller)
EDIBILITY  inconsequential (Arora)
HABITAT  in groups but not clusters on wet algae-covered wood or occasionally on soil^, (Arora), on rotting wood, usually covered with green algae, sometimes also on algae-covered soil, (Lincoff), gregarious, often in large colonies, not bundled, on rotten wood, associated with a film of Chlorococcoid algae (in Corner(3) he corrects this to Coccomyxa) on the surface of the wood, in USA often on Nyssa, (Corner(2)), most often fruiting in late summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-7.5 x 1.8-3 microns, narrowly elliptic, smooth, colorless^, (Lincoff), spores 4.5-7.5 x 1.8-3 microns, narrowly elliptic or oblong elliptic, either without drops or with 1-2 drops; basidia 4-spored, 15-21 x 4-5 microns; cystidia none; hymenium extending over tip of the fruiting body; hyphae "3-6 microns wide, often dilated to 8 microns at the septa, scarcely inflated, clamped", "with few or no interweaving hyphae", (Corner(2)), "spores 5-7 x 2-3 microns; basidia 18-25 x 5.5-7 microns; hyphae 2-6 microns wide, the superficial, non-agglutinated stem hyphae 1-2 microns wide; mycelial hyphae round the algal cells 1.5-3 microns wide, with slightly thickened walls", (Corner(3)), spores 4.5-7.5 x 2-3 microns, oblong to elliptic, smooth; cystidia absent, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'moldy'
SIMILAR  Other Multiclavula species may be sparsely branched (see Clubs category). See also SIMILAR section of Lentaria epichnoa.
SOURCES  Corner(2), Corner(3), Petersen(27), Arora(1), Lincoff(2)*, Bessette(2)*, Miller(14)*, Petersen(4), Tylutki(1)*, Brodo(1)
16
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina abietina  (Pers.) Giachini^  Mycotaxon 115: 189. 2011;  Ramaria abietina  (Pers.: Fr.) Quel.  Fl. Myc. Franc. 467. 1888;  Ramaria ochraceovirens  (Jungh.) Donk;  Clavaria ochraceovirens  Jungh.
ENGLISH NAMES  green-staining coral
NOTES  Phaeoclavulina abietina has 1) small fruitbodies that are slender, profusely branched, and yellow ocher to greenish ocher, sometimes with some small branchlets deep blue-green, 2) stem and branches that quickly turn deep blue green when handled or confined in wax paper, 3) stem that is slender and distinct to nearly absent, 4) stem base with snow-white, cottony basal felt often including or ending in delicate snow-white rhizomorphs, the snow white color remaining on drying, and 5) microscopic characters that include relatively small spores with rounded spines, and clamp connections.^ Phaeoclavulina abietina is rare but scattered throughout conifer forests of temperate North America (Petersen). RANGE Collections were examined from WA, ID, NB, NS, ON, CA, CO, MI, MN, NY, Austria, Belgium, Czech Republic, Denmark, France, Germany, Italy, Sweden, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom, (Petersen). It is rare in the Pacific Northwest, but scattered through coniferous forests in WA, OR, and CA, (Castellano). There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia. It also occurs in Asia (Trudell).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh of stem positive reaction to ferric sulphate in water, negative for IKI^, (Castellano), branch sections green in ferric sulphate in water, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to 3.5cm wide and up to 7.5cm high, "copiously branched to congested-branched, in general outline obconical to spherical (without underground portion)"^, branches arising irregularly, often flattened, dichotomous to polychotomous, internodes short, axils narrowly rounded, tips minutely rounded, never terraced, (Petersen)
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  yellow-ocher to dull ocher when fresh or somewhat greenish ocher, quickly turning deep blue-green when handled or confined in wax paper, but often with some small branchlets deep blue-green when fresh, (color change begins within several minutes of picking and if confined in wax paper for some hours, almost the whole fruitbody changes color); axils often ochraceous tan with deposited spores; tips somewhat more yellow than branches when fresh, and usually changing little upon handling or confinement^, (Petersen)
STEM  "very variable, sometimes slender and distinct, often nearly lacking with branches arising at or below substrate level"; olive-ochraceous to dull ocher, or sometimes olivaceous, quickly turning deep blue-green when handled or confined, usually from the base progressively upward, but always first at the point of handling; arising from snow white cottony basal felt, often including or ending in delicate snow white rhizomorphic strands, the white color remaining on drying^, (Petersen), 0.5-1.5cm x 0.1-0.3cm, usually short and slender, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  mild, when dried of fenugreek, (Petersen), mild, of anise, (Castellano), earthy (Scates-Barnhart), strong (Miller)
TASTE  mild, agaricoid, sometimes mildly bitter^, (Petersen), bitter (Miller)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart), inedible (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
HABITAT  on conifer debris^ (Petersen, Castellano), May, and September through November, (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  ochraceous, but small greenish stains almost always present on paper where fruitbody rested^, (Petersen), light brown (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (5.5)7.0-9.0(11) x (3.3)3.7-4.5(4.8) microns, average 8.2 x 4.27 microns, sublacrymiform to broadly ovoid with curved apicular end; ornamentation of numerous, scattered, strongly cyanophilic warts or rounded spines less than 1 micron long, often about 0.5 microns long; contents homogeneous or with one droplet, very weakly cyanophilic with droplet acyanophilic, wall up to 0.5 microns thick and moderately cyanophilic, apiculus a thin-walled rounded nipple-like extension beyond the spore wall and acyanophilic^; basidia 2-(3)-4-spored, 40-46 x 6-6.5 microns, clavate, clamped, densely multiguttulate when mature, sterigmata stout, divergent; subhymenium extensive, hyphae 1.5-2.4 microns wide, thin-walled, clamped, colorless, "longitudinally oriented producing subbasidial cells as perpendicular branches"; hyphae of basal tomentum 1.4-2.6 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, conspicuously clamped, ampulliform clamp connections abundant, up to 14 microns broad, slightly thick-walled, especially in cross-walls, complicated stellate crystals often present in basal mat; hyphae of branch trama 2.6-14 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, adherent when dried, inconspicuously clamped, generally longitudinally oriented, ampulliform clamp connections occasional, up to 15 microns broad, medallion clamp connections common, gloeoplerous hyphal segments occasional, not delimited by septa, crystals elongate-rectangular, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning 'pertaining to Abies (fir)'
SIMILAR  Slender forms of Phaeoclavulina abietina could be confused with poorly developed greening fruitbodies of Phaeoclavulina mutabilis: 1) the stem and basal mycelium in P. mutabilis almost always show signs of brunnescent bruise marks, 2) the subordinate branches and tips in P. abietina are usually much more slender than in P. mutabilis, 3) the spores of P. abietina are somewhat longer on average, and more distinctly ornamented, and the tramal hyphae more discrete (while the tramal hyphae of P. mutabilis shed copious amorphous debris into the mounting medium), (Petersen). Phaeoclavulina glaucoaromatica has larger fruitbodies and spores, and the green color is limited to the stem, (Petersen). Phaeoclavulina eumorpha and Phaeoclavulina curta are similar but neither have greenish tones or stain blue-green, (Exeter). See also SIMILAR section of Phaeoclavulina argentea.
SOURCES  Petersen(14) (as Ramaria abietina), Castellano(2)* (as Ramaria abietina), Scates-Barnhart(1) (as Ramaria abietina), Miller(14)* (as Ramaria abietina), Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)* (as Ramaria abietina), Exeter(3)* (as Ramaria abietina), Trudell(4)* (as Ramaria abietina), Buczacki(1)* (as Ramaria abietina), Desjardin(6)*
17
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina argentea  (R.H. Petersen) Giachini^  Mycotaxon 115: 189. 2011;  == Ramaria argentea  R.H. Petersen  Bibl. Mycol. 79:111. 1981;  = Ramaria incognita  R.H. Petersen  Bibl. Mycol. 79: 111. 1981
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Phaeoclavulina argentea is small, slender, and golden to honey-colored, with whitish branch tips that do not turn green or blue when handled and remain white when dried. Spores are spiny. (Exeter(3)).^ Ramaria incognita was synonymized with Phaeoclavulina argentea in Giachini(3) in 2011 but this synonymy was not reflected in the online Species Fungorum, accessed April 2, 2018, or MycoBank, accessed the same day. Dried fruitbodies very light in weight with obvious brunnescent marks on the whitish stem surface and very minute congested tips that are often silvery or whitish, the stem disappearing "into copious amorphous, cottony white mycelium involving significant amounts of substrate", the basal mycelium often including some slender stringy rhizomorphs, (Petersen(14), who only examined dried specimens). RANGE It has been found in ID (Petersen(14)), and CA, CO, NM, UT, and Sweden, (Exeter(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  "rhizomorph surface quickly brown in 10% KOH, and slowly leaching a brown liquid"^, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to up to 5cm wide, up to 7cm high, repeatedly branched; branching system irregular, usually highly congested, major branches up to 0.5cm wide, "usually flattened or channeled, sometimes very palmate", secondary branches flattened somewhat, on drying often becoming subcartilaginous [somewhat cartilaginous]; tips "minute, acute, irregular and congested when young, becoming flattened and irregularly digitate to mitten-shaped in age"^, (Petersen)
FLESH  "off-white when fresh, becoming extremely fragile on drying; dried fruitbodies extremely light in weight"^, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches pallid olivaceous to "honey color", secondary branches "honey color", "antimony yellow", "yellow ocher", to moderately olivaceous tan, on drying often becoming dark brown; tips on drying usually becoming whitish or pewter-like [also referred to when dry as silvery or whitish]^, (Petersen)
STEM  "single in somewhat depauperate specimens, to usually complex, snow white when fresh, drying to white, bruising brown"; basal mycelium "white to snow white (white on drying), as delicate, fragile, ill-defined rhizomorphs" extending at least 0.5cm from the base of the fruitbody and usually considerably farther; rhizomorphic strands less than 0.1-0.2cm thick, "usually flattened, snow white, moderately soft, often reticulate, easily broken when dry, somewhat mealy"^, (Petersen)
ODOR  mild to none^ (Petersen)
TASTE  mild to none^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on humus and/or woody debris, under Abies (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), aspen, and Cupressus (cypress)^, (Petersen), clustered to gregarious among conifer debris in CA and ID, in fall, (Exeter)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.5-8.0 x 3.0-4.5 microns, elliptic to teardrop-shaped, with densely scattered, narrowly rounded, cyanophilic spines up to 1.2 microns long, "contents homogeneous to obscurely granular", wall slightly thickened proximally (up to 0.5 microns), thinner distally, hilar appendage obscure^; basidia 4-spored, 35-42 x 6-7.5 microns, clavate, with clamp connections, contents homogeneous becoming multiguttulate and golden by maturity, sterigmata short, spindly, effete basidia persisting as empty ghosts; hymenium thickening; subhymenium not extensive, hyphae 1.8-2.2 microns wide, discrete; tramal hyphae of fruitbody 2.2-13 microns wide, colorless, "thin-walled, clamped, easily separated, generally parallel", inflated clamp connections up to 15 microns wide, slightly thick-walled (wall up to 0.8 microns thick), without ornamentation; "hyphae of rhizomorphic strand surface pellicular, very narrow, adherent", outer tramal hyphae of rhizomorphic strand 1.5-2.2 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, long-celled, conspicuously clamped, inflated clamp connections up to 14 microns wide, somewhat thick-walled (wall up to 0.7 microns thick), without ornamentation, inner tramal hyphae of rhizomorphic strand 1.8-5.6 microns wide, otherwise like outer tramal hyphae, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'silvery', probably referring to the silvery or whitish color of the tips on drying
SIMILAR  Phaeoclavulina abietina stains blue-green and lacks the white tips, (Exeter(3)). Phaeoclavulina curta stains blue-green, has shorter spores, and lacks the white tips, (Exeter(3)). Phaeoclavulina eumorpha has yellowish rhizomorphs and lacks the white branch tips, (Exeter(3)).
SOURCES  Petersen(14) (colors individually enclosed in quotation marks from Ridgway(1)) (as Ramaria argentea), Exeter(3) (as Ramaria argentea), Giachini(3)
18
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina curta  (Fr.) Giachini^  Mycotaxon 115: 190, 2011;  Ramaria curta  (Fr.) Schild  Z. Mykol. 60(1): 125. 1994;  Ramaria myceliosa var. microspora  R.H. Petersen  Bibliotheca Mycologica  79: 157. 1981;  Ramaria pusilla  (Peck) Corner;  Clavaria pusilla  Peck;  Clavaria curta  Fr.;  Clavaria corrugata  P. Karst.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Phaeoclavulina curta is very small, creamy to yellowish, with intricate branches that have slightly paler tips, the spores very short and having short spines.^ It does not differ from Phaeoclavulina myceliosa microscopically except in spore dimensions, but the small size, irregular branching pattern, and yellow-ocher color tend to make it distinct. Ramaria myceliosa was synonymized with Phaeoclavulina curta in Giachini(3) in 2011, but this synonymy was not reflected in the online Species Fungorum, accessed September 30, 2018, or MycoBank, accessed the same day. Ramaria pusilla, given by Petersen as a synonym, is described by Corner as "wholly dull cream-white or pale yellowish, often with pinkish stains on the stem, or light olive-drab with pinkish purple stem, turning deep purplish pink on bruising, drying ochraceous-yellow or drab ... flesh white, deep purplish pink on bruising ... no smell or taste ... In humus under Pinus, Sequoia, and Tsuga". The description below is derived from Petersen except where noted. RANGE Collections are not always annotated separately from the type variety of Ramaria myceliosa (since it was regarded as variety microspora), but clearly it occurs in the Pacific Northwest, "Over the years, I have attempted to segregate collections of the small-spored taxon in the northwestern United States. Nomina herbaria have been used for an olive form ("R. olivaceo-pallida"), an erect, strict form ("R. idahoensis") and very small forms ("R. ultima"). ... Ultimately, however, intergrades between these forms intrude so often as to make their segregation awkward and perhaps invalid. I have restricted such distinction, therefore, to R. myceliosa f. [sic] microspora, more or less because it is most easily recognizable by its small size and irregular branching, although not geographically limited." R. idahoensis would obviously be found in ID, and Corner mentions collections of Ramaria pusilla from OR, ON, and NY. Exeter(3) gives the distribution as WA, OR, NY, Finland, and Sweden. There is a collection from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  branch sections green in ferric sulphate in water
FRUITING BODY  up to 1.5cm wide and up to 3cm high, repeatedly and irregularly branched^, major branches few, arising irregularly to polychotomously, up to 0.3cm thick, upper branches arising irregularly to dichotomously, flattened somewhat, axils narrow, acute, apices minute, acute
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  yellow ocher, tips pale when fresh, lighter than branches when dry
STEM  discrete (and then up to 2.0 x 0.4cm), or complex-cespitose, "covered at base with a felty to appressed mat of white mycelium (remaining almost white on drying), and involving a few very slender, stringy, white rhizomorphic strands, upward yellow-ocher"
ODOR  not recorded
TASTE  not recorded
EDIBILITY  inedible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  under pines (Scates-Barnhart), under Pinus, Sequoia and Tsuga, (Corner), terrestrial in Pacific coast and inland forests in fall, (Exeter)
SPORE DEPOSIT  pale tan
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.2-5.2 x 2.8-3.5 microns, average 4.4 x 2.97 microns, ovoid to broadly teardrop-shaped, ornamentation of very fine spines or verruculae [fine warts], sparsely scattered; contents homogeneous or with vacuolar structures (?nucleus, vacuole), wall thin to discernibly thick (up to 0.2 microns thick), apiculus prominent, eccentric, and more or less papillate^; basidia 4-spored, up to 30 microns long, clavate, clamped; hyphae of branch trama up to 5 microns wide, slightly thick-walled (wall up to 0.3 microns thick), not adherent, parallel, colorless, ampulliform clamp connections occasional, hyphae of rhizomorphs skeletalized generatives, up to 2.5 microns wide, tortuous, thick-walled (up to 0.5 microns thick), ampulliform clamp connections abundant, up to 12 microns broad, subspherical, smooth, (Petersen), lacks gloeoplerous hyphae in the rhizomorphic strands (Exeter)
NAME ORIGIN  'curta' means 'short'
SIMILAR  Phaeoclavulina myceliosa is larger, more delicately and regularly branched, less yellowish, and has larger spores, (Petersen, but note that Giachini(3) synonymized Ramaria myceliosa with Phaeoclavulina curta). Phaeoclavulina abietina has longer spores, often has greenish tint, and stains blue-green when handled, (Exeter). Phaeoclavulina eumorpha has longer spores (Exeter). See also SIMILAR section of Phaeoclavulina argentea.
SOURCES  Petersen(14) (as Ramaria myceliosa var. microspora), Corner(2) (as R. pusilla), Exeter(3) (as Ramaria curta) (as Ramaria curta), Scates-Barnhart(1) (as Ramaria curta), Buczacki(1)* (as Ramaria curta), Giachini(3)
19
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina eumorpha  (P. Karst.) Giachini^  Mycotaxon 115: 191, 2011;  == Ramaria eumorpha  (P. Karst.) Corner  Ann. Bot. Mem. 1: 575. 1950;  = Ramaria invalii  (Cotton & Wakef.) Donk;  Clavaria invalii  Cotton & Wakef.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Phaeoclavulina eumorpha include 1) small size and stocky stature in various shapes, 2) ochraceous branches that do not stain or bruise but darken when old and dry a dull rusty orange, 3) stem with basal felt or mycelial mat that is yellow to off-white, 4) flesh that is soft not brittle when fresh and dries friable, 5) spores with numerous short sharp spines, 6) monomitic rhizomorphs, and 7) a pale apricot or peachy liquid that leaches from mounts of dry branch pieces in 2% KOH.^ Ramaria invalii is a small olive-suffused form of P. eumorpha occurring under short-needled conifers (hemlock in the north, spruce and fir throughout the range). RANGE Phaeoclavulina eumorpha has been found in northern Europe and the cool parts of North America: collections were examined from WA, ID, NS, ON, PQ, CA, IN, MA, MI, MN, NC, NY, OH, TN, Austria, Czech Republic, Denmark, Finland, Germany, Latvia, Norway, Poland, Russia, Sweden, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom, (Petersen). The distribution includes WA, OR, ID, CA, IN, MA, MI, MN, NC, NY, TN, Canada, and Europe, (Exeter).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem context inamyloid, (Marr), rhizomorphs ephemerally bright yellow in 15% KOH, negative reaction to IKI, "when dry pieces of branches are placed in 2% KOH and crushed well for microscopic examination, a pale apricot or peachy pigment is leached into the liquid", branch sections green in ferric sulphate in water, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to 6cm wide and 8cm high, generally fusiform [spindle-shaped] to somewhat spherical in outline, branches congested, not uncommonly anastomosed, ascending^, up to 0.25cm thick, always with central branches longer (higher) than outer branches, axils narrowly rounded, tips 0.1-0.2cm long, awl-shaped, often curled when dry, (Petersen), 1.5-3.5cm wide, 2.5-7cm high, branching up to 5 times from stem, nodes mostly dichotomous, axils subacute to narrowly U-shaped and branches slightly divergent, internodes generally elongated, up to 1cm, branches slender, 0.1-0.3cm wide, single or forked near tips, tips acute, (Marr)
FLESH  fruitbody consistency coriaceous [leathery] when fresh; flesh in branches paler than surface, (Marr), in stem soft, not brittle, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  yellow-ocher when young, darker and duller when mature (clay-colored), tips buff yellow when young, aging cream buff to honey-colored^, (Scates-Barnhart), lower branches when young light ocher, when mature somewhat darker and duller; tips "buff yellow" when young, becoming "warm buff", "cream buff", "antimony yellow", "ochraceous buff", "light buff", "honey color" when old, when dried the fruitbodies very often dry to a dull rusty orange color, especially the upper branches and tips, a color only rarely found in other taxa, (Petersen), branches and tips 'golden yellow', not bruising or staining, (Marr)
STEM  ranging from distinct (up to 2cm long and 0.8cm wide) to (rarely) almost absent (branches occurring at substrate level, or more than one short stem arising in a densely cespitose habit); usually colored as branches or darker, but commonly with areas of dull pallid olive, basal mycelial tomentum off-white to pale yellow, extensive, as arachnoid [webby] or webbed plate-like sheets between slender (less than 0.1cm wide) yellowish rhizomorphic strands^, (Petersen), 1-3cm x 0.3cm wide, single, slender, with conspicuous 'yellowish white to pale yellow' basal tomental mat and mycelial strands, stem white in the region of basal tomentum but above the tomentum colored as branches, (Marr)
ODOR  "none or mild, fresh, perhaps farinaceous"^, (Petersen), faintly earthy (Marr)
TASTE  "musty, mildly bitter, perhaps of tallow", (Petersen), not distinctive (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  in needle duff under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) and other conifers as well as Fagus (beech) and Quercus (oak)^, (Scates-Barnhart), under conifers - Pinus (pine), Picea (spruce), Abies (true fir) - and hardwoods - Fagus, Quercus, (Petersen), in needle duff under Tsuga heterophylla, fall, (Marr) 
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow' (Marr), "ochraceous buff" ("yellow ocher", "antimony yellow"), (Petersen)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.3-10 x 3.3-4.8 microns, average 7.8 x 4.0 microns, with spines 0.25-1.5 microns tall^, (Scates-Barnhart), spores 6.3-10 x 3.3-4.8 microns, average 7.8 x 4.0 microns, broadly lacrymiform [teardrop-shaped] to broadly comma-shaped, roughened, with numerous short (up to 1 micron long) cyanophilic, conic, sharp spines scattered randomly over total spore surface, yellow-ocher under bright field, contents homogeneous or with a single dull droplet, wall thin to slightly thickened (up to 0.3 microns) irregularly in median regions; basidia (2-)4-spored, 30-45 x 7.5-9 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous when young, when old with many small, scattered dull droplets; clamp connections present; rhizomorphs monomitic, (Petersen), spores 6-8.5 x 4-6 microns, average 6.8 x 3.7 microns (not including spines), 7.6 x 4.9 microns (including spines), (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  'eumorpha' from 'eu-' meaning 'agreeable' or 'true, original, typical' and 'morpha' meaning 'shape'; 'invalii' after the town of Inval in Surrey, England, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Phaeoclavulina abietina has a characteristic blue-green bruising reaction, which allows it to be distinguished from this species either fresh or dried, (Petersen). Phaeoclavulina myceliosa  1) has a much branched, open, delicate form (P. eumorpha being more robust), 2) usually becomes more olive when old, and 3) has shorter spores, (Scates-Barnhart). Phaeoclavulina curta has shorter spores, (Exeter). See also SIMILAR section of Phaeoclavulina argentea.
SOURCES  Petersen(14) (as Ramaria eumorpha), Marr(1) (as R. invalii, color in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1)  (as Ramaria eumorpha), Lincoff(1)* (as R. invalii), Exeter(3)* (as Ramaria eumorpha), Buczacki(1)* (as Ramaria eumorpha)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina glaucoaromatica  (R.H. Petersen) Giachini^  [as 'glauco-aromatica'] Mycotaxon 115: 193. 2011;  Ramaria glaucoaromatica  R.H. Petersen  Bibliotheca Mycologica  79: 141. 1981
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Phaeoclavulina glaucoaromatica is immediately recognizable because of the intense blue-green stains at the base even before the fruitbody is picked, and within a few hours in wax paper or exposed, the entire fruitbody except the white stem base and the flesh becomes deep greenish olive. The branches are erect, and greenish ochraceous when fresh, the tips yellow to cream.^ Phaeoclavulina glaucoaromatica is rare. The description was derived from Petersen except where noted. RANGE Collections were examined from ID, AB, CO, NM, and UT, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  branch sections: deep olivaceous reaction to KOH or ferric sulphate in water
FRUITING BODY  up to 5cm wide, up to 15cm high, "copiously branched, the branches all erect and ascending giving the fruitbody a columnar appearance"^; major branches 2 to several, lobed in cross-section, ascending, rebranched into 3-6 ranks, axils acute to very narrowly acute, tips acute, often cusped, dichotomous to trichotomous
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  branches greenish ochraceous when fresh, becoming watery where handled and then changing to ochraceous olive, on drying changing to greenish olive; tips yellow to cream; the entire fruitbody except the white stem base and the flesh becoming deep greenish olive within a few hours on wax paper or exposed 
STEM  arising from a definite stem portion, some or most of which may be beneath the substrate level, stem up to 1.5cm wide, often swollen below substrate level, snow white and cottony below substrate level, (remaining snow white even when dried), subtended by delicate inconspicuous white rhizomorphic strands, upward ochraceous olive, staining in the field to intense blue-green (usually just above snow-white stem base), and slowly (in a few hours) changing to deep olive after picking or on exposure
ODOR  "none, on drying moderate to strong of fenugreek"
TASTE  mildly bitter
EDIBILITY  inedible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  on conifer duff
SPORE DEPOSIT  yellow-ocher
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.2-11.1 x (4.1)4.4-5.5 microns, average 9.5 x 4.6 microns, sublacrymiform [somewhat teardrop-shaped] to ovoid with subattenuate apicular end often curved somewhat, ornamentation of numerous, scattered, strongly cyanophilic warts or rounded spines up to 1 micron long but usually shorter, usually about 0.5-0.7 microns long, content homogeneous or with 1 or 2 droplets, the droplets brownish under phase contrast, colorless under bright field, wall up to 0.4 microns thick and moderately cyanophilic, apiculus a colorless, thin-walled acyanophilic nipple-like extension of the spore contents^; basidia (3-)4-spored, 55-65 x 6.8-7.2 microns, clavate, clamped, colorless when immature, becoming multiguttulate when mature, the droplets yellowish under bright field; clamp connections present, (Petersen), rhizomorphs presumably monomitic as this is a character of subgenus Echinoramaria
NAME ORIGIN  means 'glaucous' [in this case sea-blue-green] and 'aromatic' (fenugreek odor on drying)
SIMILAR  Phaeoclavulina abietina has a smaller fruitbody and smaller more delicate spores, and the stem base is smaller involving less basal tomentum, (Petersen). P. abietina is smaller and rhizomorphs are snow white, distinct, and felty, instead of delicate and inconspicuous, (Scates-Barnhart).
SOURCES  Petersen(14) (as Ramaria glaucoaromatica), Scates-Barnhart(1) (as Ramaria glaucoaromatica), Exeter(3) (as Ramaria glaucoaromatica)
21
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina mutabilis (Schild & R.H. Petersen) Giachini^ Mycotaxon 115: 194. 2011;  Ramaria mutabilis  Schild & R.H. Petersen  Bibliotheca Mycologica  79: 149. 1981
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Phaeoclavulina mutabilis include 1) medium size, 2) branches that are olive-brown to yellowish olive-brown, 3) fresh tips that are creamy yellow green to ochraceous olive, 4) brown to olive brown stem that is variable in size, (occasionally with greenish color), and stains slowly reddish brown when handled or picked, arising from mass of white mycelium and white rhizomorphs, 5) spiny spores and 6) clamp connections.^ The description is derived from Petersen(14). "It is evidently more common (except Europe) than R. abietina and together with R. eumorpha and R. flaccida, makes up the bulk of this subgenus [Echinoramaria with spiny spores] in North Temperate climates". The current name in the online Species Fungorum, accessed September 4, 2017, is Phaeoclavulina mutabilis (Schild & R.H. Petersen) Giachini, Mycotaxon 115: 194 (2011).  RANGE Distribution is from western and northern North America to central Europe and probably the Himalayas. Collections were examined from BC, WA, OR, ID, AZ, CA, CO, MI, MT, NY, UT, Italy, Switzerland, and India, Petersen(14).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  branch sections green in ferric sulphate in water^, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm wide, up to 16cm high, repeatedly branched, of very variable stature from fusiform to very broad ovate in profile, major branches 3 to many^, up to 0.5cm thick, ascending to erect, usually flattened laterally or longitudinally channeled or ridged, upper branches similar, axils rounded to lunate, tips minute, cusped to digitate, about 0.1cm wide and long
FLESH  branches extremely fragile when dried
BRANCH COLOR  dull olive-brown to yellowish-olive-brown; tips when young and fresh creamy yellow green to ochraceous olive, when old becoming duller in color
STEM  0.2-2cm long, from ill-defined in large specimens to well-defined in more slender fruitbodies, "usually channeled or ridged longitudinally as continuations of the major branches", white in lower part from basal mycelium, brown to olive brown in upper part, occasionally with definite greenish color especially in stout-stemmed fruitbodies, when handled or picked slowly bruising rubribrunnescent, base 0.3-3cm wide, always dissipating into a mass of snow white mycelium and rhizomorphs extending from scarcely a millimeter to several centimeters from the fruitbody and sometimes interconnecting several fruitbodies, "rhizomorphs and mycelium mealy, only occasionally discrete and rope-like, remaining off-white in drying, bruising brown in very limited areas"
ODOR  mild, in field notes reported as musty disagreeable, farinaceous, fragrant; when dried, either without odor or faintly pleasant, not of fenugreek
TASTE  variable, from musty to bitter or acrid [peppery]
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  under conifers, "Fruitings on wood or lignous duff seem to produce larger, more complex and congested fruitbodies."
SPORE DEPOSIT  citrine ochraceous ("antimony yellow", "honey yellow")
MICROSCOPIC  spores 5.5-7.5 x 3.3-4.1 microns, average 6.53 x 3.73 microns, ovoid to elliptic, slightly flattened adaxially, ornamentation of closely set, scattered sharp spines up to 0.6 microns long, contents homogeneous or (most commonly) with one to several droplets that are dark under phase contrast, wall thin, apiculus eccentric and papillate^; "hymenium irregularly unilateral, the sterile areas on inner or upper branch surfaces, especially above axils, but rarely extending upward to the next axil before occlusion by hymenium"; basidia 40-65 x 5-6 microns, narrowly clavate, clamped, colorless to pale yellow under phase contrast, somewhat refringent when young, colorless when old, not persistent after spore discharge; hymenium thickened; hyphae of branch trama loosely parallel, 1.5-7 microns wide, thin-walled, somewhat inflated, colorless, not adherent, with clamp connections; hyphae of rhizomorphs 1.5-3 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, ampulliform clamp connections up to 12 microns wide, onion-shaped to humped, thin-walled to slightly thick-walled, unornamented
NAME ORIGIN  means 'changeable', probably referring to the very variable stature
SIMILAR  Slender forms of Phaeoclavulina abietina could be confused with poorly developed greening fruitbodies of P. mutabilis: 1) the stem and basal mycelium in P. mutabilis almost always show signs of brunnescent bruise marks, 2) the subordinate branches and tips in P. abietina are usually much more slender than in P. mutabilis, 3) the spores of P. abietina are somewhat longer on average, and more distinctly ornamented, and the tramal hyphae more discrete (while the tramal hyphae of P. mutabilis shed copious amorphous debris into the mounting medium), (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(14), Exeter(3)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Phaeoclavulina myceliosa  (Peck) Fanchi & M. Marchetti^  Index Fungorum 373: 1. 2018;  Ramaria myceliosa  (Peck) Corner  var. myceliosa  Ann. Bot. Mem. 1: 607 1950;  Clavaria myceliosa  Peck
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Phaeoclavulina myceliosa  include 1) small size, 2) open to erect form, branching occurring from near the substrate level or from a slender stem, 3) light ocher branches when young, usually darker and more olive when old, 4) tips paler than branches especially when young, 5) plentiful off-white rhizomorphs, 6) usually bitter taste, 7) spores with small sharp spines, 8) extensive unilateral hymenium, and 9) clamp connections.^ Ramaria myceliosa was synonymized with Phaeoclavulina curta in Giachini(3) in 2011 but this synonymy was not reflected in the online Species Fungorum, accessed September 30, 2018, or MycoBank, accessed the same day.  RANGE Collections were examined from WA, OR, ID, MB, CA, MI, MN, NH, NY, Finland, Germany, Italy, India, and Sweden. There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  branch sections green in ferric sulphate in water^, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to 5cm high (and then rather strict), up to 3.5cm wide (and then rather divaricate), often much smaller, repeated branched, arbuscular to open and somewhat spherical in shape^, major branches somewhat flattened, up to 0.3cm wide, "arising in a range from open and somewhat divaricate, to erect with long internodes, to irregular and abruptly rebranching", secondary branches somewhat flattened, axils rounded, narrow to very broad, tips ranging from prolonged and awl-shaped through minute and acerose [needle-like], to subcristate [somewhat crested], (Petersen), 2-6(10)cm wide and high, abruptly and profusely branched from slender base, branches slender, spreading, (Arora)
FLESH  white^ (Corner), pliant, in stem thin, pliant, whitish, (Arora)
BRANCH COLOR  light ocher when young, usually darker when old and more olive; major branches tan-ocher when young, when older darker and more olivaceous, secondary branches colored the same, tips always paler than branches (pale yellowish tan when young, somewhat darker when old, eventually almost colored as branches)^, (Petersen), yellowish to tan, ochraceous, olive-ocher, cinnamon-buff, or dull orange, etc., tips the same color, (Arora)
STEM  ranging between two extremes of stature 1) discrete, slender, up to 1.5cm x 0.3cm, somewhat flattened to round in cross-section, channeled when dry, 2) obscure, with branching occurring virtually at substrate levels; always with copious long, slender, stringy, off-white, rhizomorphic strands^, (Petersen), slender, pliant, not very fleshy, same color as branches or paler, with abundant rhizomorphs attached to base and/or permeating surrounding humus, (Arora)
ODOR  mild, sometimes aromatic^, (Petersen)
TASTE  mildly astringent, bitter^, (Petersen), usually bitter (Arora)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on conifer litter^, (Petersen), in humus under conifers - Pseudotsuga (Douglas-fir), Tsuga (hemlock), Abies (true fir), Sequoia, (Corner), scattered to densely gregarious in duff under conifers, in parts of California often abundant under redwood in fall, winter, and early spring, (Arora)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 4.4-5.7 x 2.6-3.5 microns, average 4.75 x 2.88 microns, broadly elliptic to short-cylindric, roughened, ornamentation of small (up to 0.3 microns), sharp prickles sparsely scattered over whole wall area, contents homogeneous or with 1-2 small brownish droplets, wall not measurably thickened^; hymenium extensively unilateral, especially on outer branch surfaces (with sterile areas to more neutral brown after drying); basidia 4-spored, 27-33 x 3.5-4.5 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous; hyphae of stem and branch trama up to 6.5 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, generally parallel, ampulliform clamp connections common, elongate-pyriform; hyphae of rhizomorphic strands 1.5-2.5 microns wide, thin-walled, generally parallel, of 2 types, 1) colorless, with ampulliform clamp connections up to 10 microns wide, pyriform to broadly ovate, smooth, with wall up to 0.5 microns thick, 2) "gloeoplerous, with golden refringent contents distributed in many discrete segments", crystals less than 2 microns in diameter, amorphous to occasionally cruciform, (Petersen), spores 3.5-6 x 2-4 microns, elliptic to nearly round, minutely spiny, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with plentiful mycelium'
SIMILAR  Phaeoclavulina curta is smaller, more irregularly branched, more yellowish ocher, and has smaller spores, (Petersen(14) of R. curta as R. myceliosa var. microspora, but note that Giachini(3) synonymized Ramaria myceliosa var. myceliosa and var. microspora with Phaeoclavulina curta).
SOURCES  Petersen(14) (as Ramaria), Corner(2) (as Ramaria), Arora(1) (as Ramaria), Exeter(3) (as Ramaria), Desjardin(6)*, Giachini(3) (as Ramaria)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria acrisiccescens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Ramaria of Western Washington p.50 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  blah coral
NOTES  Ramaria acrisiccescens has 1) medium to large fruitbodies, usually taller than wide, the mature fruitbodies with nearly parallel very elongated branches, 2) branches that are pale yellowish brown to pale orangish brown, the lower branches turning brownish on bruising, 3) branch tips variously colored: like the branches, pallid, or with a pinkish or purplish cast when young, 4) a stem that is single or somewhat bundled, and white but with the superficial hyphae turning brown, 5) spores that are wide and warted, and 6) hyphae lacking clamp connections. Ramaria acrisiccescens is the only whitish Ramaria in Pacific Northwest without clamp connections (Trudell(4)). RANGE R. acrisiccescens is found at least in WA, (Marr). It is a common fall coral in the Pacific Northwest (Ammirati). There are collections from WA, OR, and CA at Oregon State University. There is one collection from BC at the University of British Columbia and it likely to be found in BC regardless of whether the unknown identifier was correct.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh of stem inamyloid^ (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter(3))
FRUITING BODY  5-29cm tall, 1.5-18cm across, generally taller than wide, stem single or subfasciculate, mature fruitbody with subparallel, very elongated branches^, branching up to 9 times from stem, dichotomous, axils acute to narrowly U-shaped; branches slender, 0.1-1.2cm wide, internodes frequently up to 3cm long, occasionally up to 7cm, bifid, antlered to plurinodulose near apices, apices mostly rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh, brittle and very chalky-friable when dried; brownish white^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  pale yellowish brown to pale orangish brown, lower branches turning brownish on bruising, tips variously colored: like branches, pallid, or with pinkish or purplish cast when young^, (Scates-Barnhart), slightly more brown than 'pale yellow' or 'pale orange', terminal branches the most pallid part of the fruitbody, occasionally very faint pinkish tint present; superficial hyphae of lower branches turning 'brown', (Marr), whitish to cream, (Trudell)
STEM  1.5-9cm x 1-3cm, often deeply buried, single or subfasciculate, steeply tapered, slender; white, superficial hyphae turning 'brown'; well defined white basal tomentum usually present^, (Marr)
ODOR  faintly musty sweet to bean-like^, (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive or slightly acid when fresh, drying with a pronounced bitterish acid taste^, (Marr), mild to +/- acrid [peppery] when fresh, cooking or drying with pronounced bitterish-acid taste, (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  mildly poisonous? (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  on ground in old mixed conifer forests^, (Scates-Barnhart), terrestrial under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'grayish yellow'
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-14 x 4-6 microns, average 10.1 x 4.9 microns, finely warty, hyphae without clamp connections^, (Exeter(1)), spores 8-14 x 4-6 microns, subcylindric to elliptic with prominent lateral apiculus, with distinct, lobed, cyanophilic warts; basidia 1-spored to 4-spored, mostly 4-spored, 40-90 x 7-13 microns, clavate, without basal clamp connection; subhymenial hyphae 2-5 microns wide, interwoven, thin-walled, without clamp connections; context hyphae subparallel to interwoven in stem, parallel in branches, hyphae walls thin to moderately thick, 0.25-2 microns, surface smooth to slightly fluted, non-inflating in the peripheral contextual regions, highly inflated nearer the center (3-24 microns wide), ampulliform swellings near septa 10-20 microns wide, walls of the vesicles moderately to conspicuously ornamented; clamp connections absent; gloeoplerous hyphae abundant in stem, less common in branches, localized bulbous regions enlarging up to 13 microns wide, except for these areas 2.5-5 microns wide, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Ramaria velocimutans is the species most likely to be confused in the field, but it has clamp connections, (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(1), Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Ammirati(1)*, Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria amyloidea  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Ramaria of Western Washington p.53. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  pale peach rusty-root
NOTES  Ramaria amyloidea has 1) a medium size, broad fruitbody with a single stout base, 2) branches that are light orange or with a tinge of light red, 3) branch tips that are the same color as the branches, yellower, or darker, 4) a pale brown band visible in the basal region of a radially sectioned stem, 5) flesh that when fresh is amyloid and instantly 'turquoise-green' on application of 10% ferric sulphate, and 6) relatively short, narrowly cylindric, nearly smooth spores.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA (Marr). Ramaria amyloidea is found at least in WA, OR, and CA, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh amyloid when fresh, in dried specimens instantly 'dark brown' with application of Melzer's reagent; flesh of fresh specimens instantly 'turquoise-green' on application of 10% ferric sulphate; the dark contextual band in the stem darkening on application of 20% KOH^, (Marr, colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), ferric sulphate in water instantly positive (turquoise green) on stem flesh; stem flesh instantly amyloid on both fresh (violet-brown) and dried (dark brown) specimens, (Exeter(3))
FRUITING BODY  7-15cm wide, 7-13cm high, base single and stout, lower branches often thick and upper sections short, giving an almost cauliflower-like appearance^, (Scates-Barnhart), mostly of medium size, branching from the base up to 8 times, lower nodes commonly polychotomous, axils frequently acute or turbinate, branches slightly to moderately divergent, lower branches sometimes connate, up to 4cm diameter, primary and secondary internodes lengthening up to 3cm, upper branches generally short, numerous and compacted on the primary branches, the more congested specimens cauliflower-like, pluridigitate or plurinodulose near the tips, tips rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  softly stringy; colored much like surface or slightly more reddish^, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy-fibrous when fresh, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light orange or with a tinge of light red; tips the same, yellower or darker^, (Scates-Barnhart), pale pink or orange with light red tinge, tips the same, yellow or darker, occasionally with small violet gray bruise spots, (Exeter(2))
STEM  2-6.5cm x 2-4cm, single, conic to cylindric, (Marr), equal or tapering, base white to cream, with brown patches, almost entirely brown when old; base surface not rough, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  slightly sweet^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) or Abies amabilis (Amabilis Fir)^, (Marr), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the surface of the ground, associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla, September and October, (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'apricot yellow'^ (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 3-4 microns, average 8.9 x 3.6 microns, in a single specimen a few spores smooth, but most very finely ornamented, warts cyanophilic, frequently linear^; basidia with strongly cyanophilic contents; clamp connections present, (Marr), basidia with clamp connections (Exeter)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'amyloid'
SIMILAR  Ramaria celerivirescens is similar in color and in having a band of brown in a radially sectioned stem base, the green reaction to ferric sulphate, and amyloid stem flesh, but R. celerivirescens differs in fruitbody form, in spore ornamentation and in lacking clamp connections, (Marr). Pacific Northwest species reacting with ferric sulphate 10% aqueous solution in the stem base include Ramaria testaceoflava (clamped basidia, average spore length 11.8 microns), R. amyloidea (clamped basidia, average spore length 8.9 microns, stem flesh amyloid), Ramaria velocimutans (clamped basidia, average spore length 9.0 microns, stem flesh inamyloid), Ramaria rubiginosa (clampless basidia, no rusty root), Ramaria celerivirescens (clampless basidia, rusty root, normal terminal branches), and Ramaria claviramulata (clampless basidia, rusty root, chubby terminal branches, branches turn reddish brown with 20% KOH), (Exeter). Ramaria leptoformosa might possibly be mistaken for this species, but the stem flesh is not amyloid, (Marr). There are two undescribed Ramaria species that have brownish stem flesh and clamp connections, and fruit in the fall, but do not show the instant color change in ferric salts, (Petersen(3)). Ramaria maculatipes, Ramaria rasilispora, and Ramaria rubricarnata are three other species with clamped hyphae that have amyloid stem flesh, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora, Ramaria rasilisporoides, and Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata.
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(2), Exeter(3)*, Castellano(1)*, Kornerup(2), Petersen(3) (discussing undescribed species)
25
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria apiculata  (Fr.) Donk  var. apiculata  (Fr.) Donk^  Biblthca Mycol. 21: 105. 1933;  Clavaria apiculata  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  green-tipped coral
NOTES  Ramaria apiculata var. apiculata has 1) medium size fruitbodies with usually erect somewhat slender branches that are dull buffy tan to pale orangish brown, bruising darker orange brown to vinaceous brown, 2) tips either colored as branches (and then the stem base or axils greenish), or yellow green to pale green, 3) stem distinct or branching apparently from base, 4) growth on well rotted wood, 5) spores with small, low warts often in lines or parts of spirals, 6) clamp connections, and 7) monomitic basal mat and rhizomorphs.^ RANGE Collections were examined from BC, WA, OR, ID, ON, AL, AZ, CA, MI, MN, NM, NY, VT, Mexico, Finland, Germany, Italy, and Sweden, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  spore-bearing surface in ferric sulphate in water dull slate green, in KOH browning; rhizomorphs turn yellow or brownish in 10% KOH^, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to 12cm wide, up to 17cm high, but usually narrower and smaller, stem single or branched from base^, fruitbody repeatedly branched, branches usually erect, somewhat slender, with long internodes in lower part, "but occasionally lax or spreading, and then often flattened somewhat", axils narrowly rounded, tips delicate, rounded at maturity, dichotomous to polychotomous, 0.1-0.4cm long, (Petersen)
FLESH  coriaceous [leathery] when fresh^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  dull buffy tan where fertile, darker where sterile, bruno-vinescent on bruising; tips either colored as branches (and then the stem base or axils greenish), or yellow-green to pale green; specimens showing greenish color at fruitbody base or in branch axils keep this color on drying even after many years, but when green color is restricted to branch tips, the color seems to be lost^, (Petersen), pale orangish brown, aging or bruising darker orangish brown, (Scates-Barnhart), branches 'reddish gray', soon maturing 'reddish brown', bruising the same color on handling, tips 'pale green' to 'malachite green', (Marr, colors from Kornerup(2))
STEM  "single and discrete or branched from the base so as to appear multiple or cespitose, often covered with felty basal mycelium up to 0.5cm, concolorous with branches, arising from basal tomentum; basal tomentum felty, with diffuse margin disappearing into substrate, or with margin ramifying as a tangle of slender, white, somewhat mealy, often webbed rhizomorphs", stem "white to cream color, or often some shade of green to pale blue-green"^, (Petersen)
ODOR  none^ (Petersen)
TASTE  mildly acrid [peppery] to bitter^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on well-rotted conifer wood or hardwood, including but not limited to Pinus ponderosa (Ponderosa Pine), Abies concolor (White Fir), Pinus strobus (Eastern White Pine), and Populus trichocarpa (Black Cottonwood), or rarely on buried wood and appearing terrestrial; often fruiting in late fall or after cold weather^, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores (8.0)8.5-11.0(11.8) x 4.1-5.2 microns, average 9.66 x 4.78 microns, cylindric to narrowly ovoid or elliptic, roughened in profile, ornamentation of very small, cyanophilic, densely scattered, low warts, hardly anastomosing, often in lines or portions of adaxial spirals, contents without droplets or with one obscure droplet, weakly cyanophilic, wall up to 0.3 microns thick, apiculus prominent, eccentric, truncate, thin-walled, and weakly cyanophilic^; hyphae of basal mat and rhizomorphic strands monomitic of two types 1) generative, 2.5-4.0 microns wide, thin-walled, conspicuously clamped, colorless, densely interwoven, inflated clamp connections up to 13 microns broad, thin-walled to somewhat thick-walled (wall up to 0.5 microns thick), usually delicately ornamented, usually clavate to elongate-clavate, rarely broadly clavate to subspherical, and 2) rare skeletalized generative segments, 3.2-4.5 microns wide, arising and ending in clamp connections, usually over 1000 microns long, thick-walled (wall up to 1.2 microns thick), refringent under phase contrast (these actually not observed in type variety but found in var. brunnea, so their apparent absence may be a coincidence of observation), (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Ramaria apiculata var. brunnea is at least as common as the green tinted variety (R. apiculata var. apiculata) but lacks the green coloration either when fresh or when dried, (Petersen). Ramaria stricta is definitively separated by its having a dimitic hyphal system in the basal mat, with narrow skeletal hyphae, (Petersen). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria tsugina.
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Corner(2), McKnight(1)*, Kornerup(2), Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*
26
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria apiculata  (Fr.) Donk  var. brunnea  R.H. Petersen^  Bibliotheca Mycologica 43: 43. 1975
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria apiculata var. brunnea differs from R. apiculata var. apiculata in the absence of the greenish tints, and is at least as common as the type variety.^ The description is derived from Petersen(11). One theory for the absence of green coloration in this variety and its presence in var. apiculata is that the green color is a response to cold temperatures. A form of this variety found in ID, MI, and ON, (f. compacta (Bourdot & Galzin) R.H. Petersen) "is characterized by the proliferation of enormous numbers of branches which become so compressed than they often anastomose. Colors are typical for the variety, without green pigmentation present on any specimen I have seen", (Petersen). RANGE R. apiculata var. brunnea has been found in BC, WA, ID, AB, MB, NS, ON, AL, AZ, CO, IN, IA, KY, MI, MN, MT, NC, NJ, NM, NY, OH, PA, TN, TX, UT, WI, Finland, Italy, Slovakia, Sweden, United Kingdom, also found GA, and Mexico, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  spore-bearing surface in ferric sulphate in water dull slate green, browning in KOH to deep copper color, sections of branches in KOH leach yellow into solution
FRUITING BODY  up to 5cm wide, up to 8cm high, repeatedly branched^, branches 0.2-0.4cm thick, often flattened or channeled, axils narrowly rounded to narrowly acute, often decurrent by a line, internodes somewhat long, and not abruptly shortening, tips either erect, somewhat elongated, strict, or flattened and then somewhat palmate and polychotomous
FLESH  in stem tough and fibrous
BRANCH COLOR  cinnamon color where fertile, and slowly bruno-rufescent where bruised, tips light cinnamon color when young, becoming darker, the palmate tips often becoming slightly olivine when dried
STEM  usually discrete, 0.3-0.7cm wide, often branched almost from base, dull brown, colored as branches, or occasionally covered with basal tomentum up to 0.5cm high, fruitbody "arising from a small patch of white, appressed, minutely felty to mealy basal tomentum" that extends from the fruitbody base and branches through the substrate, mycelium white and mealy, often with very slender (less than 0.1cm thick), mealy, ill-defined, white rhizomorphic strands
ODOR  none
TASTE  mildly acrid [peppery] or mildly astringent
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single, gregarious, or cespitose, common on rotting conifer wood, more rarely on hardwood; late summer through fall; for forma compacta, on very rotten wood or (preferably) sawdust heaps, the form being perhaps due to greater temperature generated by bacterial action or greater substrate surface area
SPORE DEPOSIT  dull ochraceous
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9.2-11.0(11.5) x 3.8-5.0 microns, average 9.79 x 4.51 microns, narrowly ovoid to elliptic, roughened in profile, ornamentation of scattered, small, strongly cyanophilic, non-anastomosing warts, contents homogeneous or with one obscure droplet, weakly cyanophilic, apiculus prominent, eccentric, truncate, and weakly cyanophilic^; basidia 4-spored, 35-50 x 7-7.5 microns, clavate, clamped, homogeneous to granular or minutely multiguttulate, sterigmata up to 6 microns long, slender, slightly bowed; hyphae of rhizomorphic strands monomitic, of two types, 1) generative, 2.5-4.5 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, with conspicuous clamp connections, inflated clamp connections up to 20 microns broad, thin-walled to somewhat thick-walled (wall up to 0.5 microns thick), clavate to elongate-clavate, usually delicately ornamented, and 2) skeletalized generative segments, 2.1-4.5 microns wide, thick-walled (wall up to 0.8 microns thick), usually over 1000 microns long, arising from and ending in a clamp connection, locally common to (usually) extremely rare or absent in many mounts; hyphae of basal tomentum of generative identical to above; hyphae of fruitbody base skeletalized (not skeletals), with clamp connections; hyphae of branch trama 2.5-7.5 microns, thin-walled, with conspicuous clamp connections, inflated clamp connections rare
NAME ORIGIN  'brunnea' means 'brown'
SIMILAR  Ramaria concolor is similar in stature, and to a lesser extent in color, but branching does not tend to be flattened, color is somewhat lighter, and rhizomorphic strands are not monomitic, (Petersen). Ramaria rubella is ruddy or ruddy purple, and rhizomorphic strands turn bright pinkish brown in 10% KOH, not weak brown, and it also has spores that are broadly ovoid rather than elliptic, (Petersen). Ramaria stricta has dimitic hyphae in the rhizomorphic strands, (Petersen). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria apiculata var. apiculata and Ramaria rubella forma rubella.
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria araiospora  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. araiospora^  Ramaria of Western Washington p.55 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  carmine coral, light red coral
NOTES  {See also Red Ramaria Table.} Ramaria araiospora var. araiospora has small to medium size, branches that are vivid red when young, fading to light red when old, and branch tips that become yellow to pale or deep orange.^ RANGE It has been found at least in WA (Marr), the Pacific Northwest and CA (Phillips). There are collections of Ramaria araiospora var. araiospora from BC at the University of British Columbia from WA and OR at the University of Washington. Ramaria araiospora var. araiospora is reported from ID by Andrew Parker, pers. comm.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), some collections are amyloid (Scates-Barnhart), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem context, (Exeter(3))
FRUITING BODY  2-8cm wide, 5-13cm high^, branches elongating when old to long intervals between forks, abortive branches often present, branching up to six times from the base, polychotomous to dichotomous, axils acute or turbinate and branches slightly to moderately divergent, mostly slender, 0.1-0.5cm wide, forked or finely divided near tips, tips acute or subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  softly stringy, colored like surface^, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy-fibrous when fresh, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  vivid red when young, fading to light red when old; tips becoming yellow to pale or deep orange^, (Scates-Barnhart), red fading to light red, tips yellow to pale deep orange, (Exeter(1))
STEM  2-3cm x 1.5cm, single, slightly bulbous, sometimes fasciculate, abortive branches often present, (Exeter(1)), base single, or semi-divided, bulbous; white to pale yellow, sometimes discoloring to pale brown; covered by soft fuzz, (Scates-Barnhart)^, base covered with a thin white tomentum (Marr)
ODOR  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  yes (Phillips, Lincoff)
HABITAT  on ground under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), fall^, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-13 x 3-4.5 microns, average 9.9 x 3.7 microns, finely warted with linear lobed warts^; basidia not consisting of numerous cyanophilous granules; clamp connections absent, (Exeter(1)), linear lobed warts illustrated with several thin narrowly branching lines on surface, basidia 1-4-spored, mostly 4-spored, not consisting of numerous cyanophilic granules, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'narrow-spored'
SIMILAR  Ramaria araiospora var. rubella is more magenta red with concolorous or paler but not yellow tips, (Marr). Ramaria stuntzii is bright scarlet when young, and is easily distinguished by its robust habitat and amyloid flesh, (Marr). R. stuntzii has shorter spores (7-10 x 3-5 microns), "a more massive base, reddish orange upper stipe, branches with red tips, and amyloid flesh", (Trudell). Ramaria cyaneigranosa has "overall pinkish to salmon color", wider wartier spores (8-15 x 4-6 microns), "and basidia with granules that appear blue in cotton blue stain", (Trudell). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cyaneigranosa var. cyaneigranosa.
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(1), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Trudell(4), Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria araiospora  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. rubella  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Ramaria of Western Washington p.57. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  crimson coral
NOTES  {See also Red Ramaria Table.} Ramaria araiospora var. rubella has a small to medium fruitbody, with crimson to magenta branches when young and when mature. It differs from the type variety only in color, being slightly more bluish red, and the tips not becoming yellow, (Marr).^ RANGE Ramaria araiospora var. rubella is found at least in WA (Marr). There are collections of Ramaria araiospora var. rubella from WA and OR at the University of Washington. It has been reported also from BC by O. Ceska and S. Berch.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid^ (Marr, on basis of stated similarity of characters to type variety), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem context, (Exeter(3))
FRUITING BODY  approximately 2-8cm wide and 5-13cm tall^, (Marr, on basis of stated similarity of all characters but color to type variety)
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  crimson to magenta (slightly bluish red) when young and when mature^, (Scates-Barnhart), 'red to "bluish red or crimson"', tips red, (Exeter(1))
STEM  2-3 x 1.5cm, "single, slightly bulbous, sometimes fasciculate, abortive branches often present"^, (Exeter(1), on basis of stated similarity to type variety)
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  
SPORE DEPOSIT  yellowish
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-14 x 3-5 microns, average 9.8 x 3.6 microns, finely warted with linear lobed warts^, basidia not having numerous cyanophilous granules; clamp connections absent, (Exeter(1)), basidial clamp connections absent, (Exeter(3))
NAME ORIGIN  'rubella' means 'red'
SIMILAR  For a comparison with other red Ramarias, see Ramaria araiospora var. araiospora. See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cyaneigranosa var. cyaneigranosa.
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(1), Exeter(3)*, Phillips(1)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria armeniaca  R.H. Petersen & Scates^  Mycotaxon 33: 111. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria armeniaca include medium size, branches and tips brightly colored, ranging from pastel orange to bright apricot, coarse tips, massive stem with homogeneous white flesh, spring fruiting, smooth or almost smooth spores, and absent clamp connections.^ The description is derived from Petersen(3) except where noted. RANGE It has been found in northern ID (Petersen), and OR (Exeter).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ferric chloride in water stem flesh negative, branch sections slowly positive, (Petersen), stem flesh inamyloid, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 8cm wide and up to 12cm high, somewhat circular to broadly obovate in outline, base single, major branches 3 to several^, hardly circular in cross-section, branches in 2-4 ranks, round in cross-section, internode length diminishing gradually upward at maturity, axils lunate in lower part, narrowly rounded in upper part, tips coarse, crowded when young, bluntly minutely knobby when mature
FLESH  in branches firm, white; in stem watery but not slippery, homogeneous, white, with no sign of brown patch or band
BRANCH COLOR  major branches off-white in lower part, shading to orange in upper part, branches pallid pastel orange in lower part, pallid orange to 'melon color' (Kornerup(2) color) in upper part, tips orange to 'deep orange' (Kornerup(2) color), color of branches and tips also given as "orange to bright apricot", and color of fruitbody as "ranging from pastel orange to bright orange"
STEM  up to 4cm long and up to 6cm wide, large to massive, single, broadly narrowing downward to a point, usually without abortive branchlets; white to off-white, unchanging on handling or bruising; smooth
ODOR  negligible
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  unknown
HABITAT  not given, but fruiting in spring
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.6-11.5(12.6) x 3.6-4.3 microns, average 10.3 x 3.9 microns, narrowly elliptic to subcylindric, obscurely undulate in profile, smooth or merely a suggestion of very low warts, uninucleate, 1 to 3 droplets, the droplets dark yellow and refringent, wall up to 0.3 microns thick, hilar appendix small and inconspicuous^; basidia 4-spored, 50-70 x 7-9 microns, clavate, without clamp connection, contents multiguttulate when mature; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means 'pertaining to apricot'
SIMILAR  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia fruits in the fall, and flesh of the stem and lower branches is gelatinous, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Exeter(3)*, Kornerup(2)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria aurantiisiccescens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Ramaria of Western Washington p.57. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria aurantiisiccescens has 1) small to medium fruitbodies with slender bright orange branches (most intense at tips) that do not stain red-brown or vinaceous, 2) a yellow belly-band just above the ground level, 3) fleshy-fibrous flesh, 4) a base that is single to nearly compound, white underground and colored in upper part like the lower branches, 5) thin white basal tomentum, and 6) microscopic characters including spores averaging 10.8 x 4.0 microns, with fine lobed warts, and absent clamp connections.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA (Marr). It has been found at least in WA, OR, and CA, (Castellano). There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid^ (Marr)
FRUITING BODY  2-8cm wide, 8-10cm high, small to medium^, base single to nearly compound, branching 4-7 times from the base, polychotomous or dichotomous, connation [growing together] of branches sometimes occurring in lower internodes, axils acute to rounded and branches slightly divergent, internodes elongated up to 5cm in length, branches generally slender, 0.2-1cm wide, bifid to finely divided near tips, tips subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  softly stringy; colored much like surface^, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy-fibrous when fresh (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light orange, growing lighter downward to the yellow belly band just above ground level; tips intense deep orange; lower branches sometimes stained caramel brown^, (Scates-Barnhart), lowest branches pale yellow to yellow, shading upwards into pale orange or orange-yellow, the tips nearly dark orange, (Castellano)
STEM  single to nearly compound, variable in size and shape, 1-4cm x 1-2cm, underground part white, upper base colored as lower branches; base and lower branches sometimes with pale superficial stains of 'caramel-brown' (Kornerup(2) color); a thin white basal tomentum present^, (Marr), base white, base and lower branches sometimes stained caramel brown, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  slightly sweet^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock)^, (Marr), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the ground, associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla, October, (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  rarely making good spore prints^, (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.5-14 x 3-5 microns, average 10.8 x 4.0 microns, cylindric to somewhat pip-shaped (broadly rounded apex and tapering at apicular end), ornamented with fine, lobed, cyanophilic warts^; basidia 1-4-spored, clavate, without basal clamp connection, some sterigmata conspicuously thick and long, mostly 4-7 microns, up to 22 microns; clamp connections absent, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia is orange but has a gelatinous consistency, (Marr). Ramaria sandaracina is orange but has clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria longispora is orange but has longer, more coarsely warted spores, (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1)*, Kornerup(2), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria botrytis  (Pers. ex Fr.) Ricken  var. aurantiiramosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 41. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria botrytis var. aurantiiramosa differs from Ramaria botrytis var. botrytis by having orange upper branches.^ RANGE It is found at least in WA (Marr).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh weakly amyloid after a half hour (Scates-Barnhart), stem flesh negative with ferric sulphate in water, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  medium size, 6-17cm wide, 8-15cm high^, stem massive, single or 2 or 3, primary branches few, mostly 2 or 3, short to moderately elongate, thick, up to 3cm wide, upper branch systems compacted on primary branch or stem, 3cm or less in length, pluridigitate near tips, tips subacute, rounded, or nodulose, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; stem flesh white^, (Marr)

ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  terrestrial, one of the two collections under Abies (true fir)^, (Marr), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the surface, associated with Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir) and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-16 x 4-6 microns, average 13.5 x 4.7 microns, with a suprahilar depression and a dorsal and ventral convexity, striae steeply oblique and cyanophilic; hyphae of stem thin to moderately thick-walled, 0.25-2 microns; other microscopic characters similar to R. botrytis var. botrytis^, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  'aurantiiramosa' means 'with orange branches'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubripermanens has smaller spores (Marr).
