FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria botrytis  (Pers. ex Fr.) Ricken  var. botrytis^  Vademecum. 253. 1918;  == Clavaria botrytis  Pers.
ENGLISH NAMES  wine-tipped coral, clustered coral, cauliflower coral, pink-tipped coral mushroom
NOTES  Ramaria botrytis var. botrytis has its stem and lower branches whitish, with stains yellowish to light brown. The tips and a short distance down branches are colored reddish to purplish. The stem is single and massive with the branch pattern cauliflower-like (dense terminal branching on a few thick lower branches). Stem flesh is weakly amyloid. Spores are striate and clamp connections are present.^ RANGE It is found at least in WA, (Marr), from New England to North Carolina, west to southern CA and BC, (Lincoff(2)), and Europe and North Africa, (Courtecuisse).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh weakly amyloid, reaction frequently requiring 30 minutes or more, (Marr), stem flesh negative with ferric sulphate in water (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  4-12cm wide, 7-12cm high, (one collection 19cm x 19cm), nearly as broad as tall, single stem, lower branches usually few^, upper branch system short, about 1-1.5cm long, and densely clustered on lower branches, but sometimes elongating up to 10cm, branching 5-7 times, polychotomous, axils acute to U-shaped, slightly divergent, branches generally slender, 0.1-1.5cm wide, pluridigitate near tips, tips subacute to rounded or nodulose, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous to punky-firm when fresh, (Marr), white (Lincoff(2))
BRANCH COLOR  white, with reddish or purplish tips^, (Lincoff(2)), tips and a short distance down branches grayish magenta, when young pale red to light lilac, branches much paler; sometimes bruising yellow or tannish, (Scates-Barnhart), lower branches opaque white with stains or bruises varying from 'pastel yellow' to 'light brown', upper branches a shade or two lighter than 'flesh color' and with a faint violaceous tint, tips and a short distance downward 'reddish white' to 'grayish ruby or magenta', (Marr(1) who also refer to the "the orange, reddish or purplish colored terminal branches" as one of the distinctive features of R. botrytis, the colors in single quotes being from Kornerup(2))
STEM  2-5cm x 2-3cm, single, cylindric or conic, massive, occasional abortive or primordial branches; opaque white with stains or bruises varying from 'pastel yellow' to 'light brown'^, (Marr), 
ODOR  faintly sweet^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  yes, choice, (Lincoff(2))
HABITAT  terrestrial under conifers^, (Marr), on the ground under conifers, especially Picea (spruce) and Abies (fir), August to October, (Lincoff(2))
SPORE DEPOSIT  ocher to pale orange^ (Lincoff(2)), light orange-brown (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-17 x 4-6 microns, average 13.8 x 4.7 microns, subcylindric, mummy-shaped, or occasionally sigmoid, striate ornamentation steeply spiraled to nearly longitudinal, both striae and general wall cyanophilic^; basidia mostly 4-spored (occasionally 2-spored), 59-82 x 8-11 microns, clavate, clamped; hyphae with clamp connections, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  from Greek, meaning "like a bunch of grapes"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubripermanens also has reddish terminal branches, stout form, and striate spores, but the spores are much shorter, 8-13 x 3.5-4.5 microns, (Marr). Ramaria botrytoides has smaller spores with obscure lines of warts. See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria rubrievanescens.
SOURCES  Marr(1), Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Exeter(3)*, Miller(14)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Scates-Barnhart(1), Kornerup(2), Bacon(1)*, AroraPocket*, Buczacki(1)*
33
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria botrytoides  (Peck) Corner^  Ann. Bot. Mem. 1: 562-563. 1950;  Clavaria botrytoides  Peck
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria botrytoides has 1) whitish branches with red or pink tips, 2) base developing beneath soil level, producing branches at or very near the ground level, 3) spores with longitudinal rows of low obscure cyanophilic warts, 4) rare clamp connections, and 5) rare to occasionally oleaginous hyphae in the stem context.^ Petersen(24) (1967) says of Ramaria conjuncta (Peck) Corner (described in Corner(2)), "There can be little doubt that this is the same as C. botrytoides Pk." RANGE Ramaria botrytoides is common throughout the US according to Corner, widespread but not particularly common and occurring in California according to Arora, not included in Marr and Stuntz: Ramaria in Western Washington (Marr(1)), but collected from OR with confirmation by R.H. Petersen (R. Exeter, pers. comm.). There is a collection by O Ceska from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh non-amyloid; ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  3-6cm wide, 5-7cm high, [measurements may be of dried collection], stem producing branches at or very near ground level^, lowest branches vestigial or primordial, semiaborted, major branches 0.2-0.4cm thick, open, somewhat spreading, with primary internodes about 1cm long, branching pattern dichotomous or trichotomous, "open, with internode distances decreasing markedly and branch axils open-acute to narrowly acute and then decurrent by a line", branch tips "finely dissected, minutely digitate, usually double dichotomous, with little terracing", (Petersen), 3-15cm wide, 7-16cm high, massive, branching at the ground from a short rooting base, branches curving upright, rather elongate, rugose [wrinkled], much branched distally, (Corner)
FLESH  firm, turgid, brittle; colored as surface^, (Corner)
BRANCH COLOR  branches whitish, tips red or pink when young, colored as branches when old^, (Petersen), pallid cream, pinkish toward the rose-pink tips, becoming tan or brownish tan with dull brick-brown tips when old, (Corner)
STEM  2.5-4cm long, developing beneath soil level, producing branches at or very near the ground level^, (Petersen), fruitbody branching at the ground from a short, white, rooting base set with numerous abortive pink-tipped branches, (Corner)
ODOR  similar to taste^ (Corner)
TASTE  like green pea-hulls^ (Corner), exceedingly acrid [peppery] taste can linger in mouth for up to 12 hours, (but in his key taste said to be bitter to very bitter), (Arora)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on the ground in woods^ (Corner), on clay soil, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.4-10.1 x 3.6-4.6 microns, short-cylindric to subovate, cyanophilic ornamentation of longitudinal rows of low obscure warts, spores pale yellow in mass, with obscure dark vacuolar structures under phase contrast, (spores pale brownish in Melzer's reagent), slightly thick-walled, apiculus indistinct and lateral^; basidia 4-spored, 42-52 microns long, clavate, simple-septate, homogeneous in content, sterigmata up to 6 microns long, slender, slightly incurved; basidioles simple-septate; hymenium thickening through hyphal branching from or in juxtaposition to the basidial septum; stem context hyphae of 3 types: 1) "very rare thin-walled, oleaginous, uninflated hyphae", 2.4-5 microns wide, 2) somewhat tortuous, colorless, thin-walled, sometimes clamped hyphae, homogeneous in content, 2.1-2.5 microns wide, and 3) rather straight, thin-walled, colorless, simple-septate hyphae 4.0-6.5 microns wide, "with numerous small dark smudges visible under phase contrast, and with pronounced, somewhat thick-walled, minutely punctate swellings up to 15 microns diam, distal to septa", all context hyphae "subparallel with some interweaving, but arranged in no regular tissues"; branch context hyphae 4.0-6.5 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, simple-septate, interweaving, swollen to about 10 microns distal to septa; stem "covered with effete hymenial cells the more turgid of which are refractile, but mostly sterile", (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'like [Ramaria] botrytis'
SIMILAR  Ramaria botrytis has larger spores with distinctly striate cyanophilic ornamentation (Arora). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria botrytis var. botrytis.
SOURCES  Petersen(24), Corner(2), Arora(1), Marr(1) (does not include), Exeter(3)*
34
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria caulifloriformis  (Leathers) Corner^  Beih. Nova Hedwigia 33: 268. 1970;  == Clavaria caulifloriformis  Leathers;  Ramaria cartilaginea  Marr & D.E. Stuntz
ENGLISH NAMES  devil's flesh
NOTES  Ramaria caulifloriformis has small to medium, cauliflower-shaped to fan-shaped fruitbodies with pale buff to pale pinkish buff branches, the tips of which often turn dark brown where exposed, large stem with abortive branchlets, short wide spores with mostly discrete warts, and clamp connections.^ Some authors recognize Ramaria cartilaginea Marr & D.E. Stuntz as a separate species, based on a different color (branches are pale yellow turning more tannish yellow during maturation - especially near tips, the tips colored as branches when young and contrasting slightly when old): it is microscopically very close to R. caulifloriformis. RANGE Ramaria caulifloriformis has been found in ID, OR, CA, and MI, (Petersen). There is a collection from BC (as Ramaria cartilaginea) at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  IKI reaction on stem flesh equivocal; KOH browning on hymenium; ferric chloride in water positive, probably with branch sections^, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  4.5-10cm wide, 3-13cm high, small to medium, broad, +/- fan-shaped, cauliflower-like when young^, (Scates-Barnhart), up to 16cm x 15cm, circular to spheropedunculate in outline, major branches numerous, crowded, hardly circular in cross-section, branches in 3-6 ranks, lobed in section, internode length diminishing upward gradually when mature, axils rounded, tips double-dichotomous when young, becoming cusped or knobby by maturity, (Petersen)
FLESH  brittle like cartilage to gelatinous; colored +/- like surface^, (Scates-Barnhart), in major branches solid and white, in branches "pale ochraceous buff" (Ridgway color); in stem solid to heterogeneous, moist but not slippery or soapy, not hygrophanous, white to off-white, not showing a brown band or patch, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  light brownish yellow when young, becoming more tan when old and finally brownish orange, tips the same^, (Scates-Barnhart), pallid buff to pallid pinkish buff; tips pallid pinkish buff, often turning dark brown where exposed or dried in the field, all upper parts slowly "cinnamon" (Ridgway color) when old, sometimes "reluctantly vinescent", (Petersen), pale pinkish, cinnamon to pallid pinkish buff, tips pink buff, cinnamon buff, often turning dark brown where exposed or dried in the field, all parts slowly "cinnamon" when old, (Exeter(1))
STEM  up to 4cm x 5cm, single to falsely fasciculate [bundled], rounded, involving substrate when picked, numerous abortive branchlets and clusters, especially high on stem; off-white where protected, hardly brunnescent^; tomentose between substrate fragments, mycelial at base, (Petersen), base white, a band of light yellow above the base, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  negligible to mildly bean-like or weakly agaricoid, (Petersen), slight or musty-sweet (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  negligible to faintly bean-like or slowly musty, (Petersen), mild (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  yes (Petersen)
HABITAT  usually fall, but in California in spring, (Petersen), on ground under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) or Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), fall, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.3-10.4 x 4.7-5.8 microns, average 9.3 x 5.1 microns, short-cylindric to broadly elliptic, distinctly roughened in profile, small warts, mostly discrete, some lobed in outline^, contents deep yellow, usually multigranular or with 2-4 amorphous droplets, wall up to 0.2 microns thick; basidia 4-spored, 45-60 x 7-8.5 microns, clavate, clamped, multiguttulate to multigranular when mature, strongly cyanophilic; clamp connections present, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'in cauliflower form'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(1), Exeter(3)* (both R. cartilaginea and R. caulifloriformis)
35
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria celerivirescens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 61. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  gold rusty-root
NOTES  Ramaria celerivirescens has branches that are light pinkish orange with yellow tips, stem that is single and whitish with patches of brown or reddish brown, stem flesh with a brown band, a slow amyloid reaction in the stem flesh, a rapid green reaction of flesh to ferric sulphate, spores with coarse irregularly shaped warts, and absent clamp connections.^ RANGE A collection was examined from WA (Marr). It has been found at least in WA, OR, and CA, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh amyloid when fresh, the reaction usually slow; flesh of fresh specimens instantly turning 'dark green' on application of 10% ferric sulphate; the brown band in flesh darkening with 20% KOH^, (Marr), ferric sulphate in water instantly dark green on stem flesh (Exeter(3))
FRUITING BODY  usually medium size, 3-10cm wide, 6-18cm high^, branching up to 10 times from single stem, "polychotomous or dichotomous, axils acute to turbinate and branches slightly divaricate", lower branches with internodes up to 5cm long and up to 1.3cm wide, "upper branches usually much less elongated and more slender", 0.1-0.5cm wide, bifid or multifid near tips, tips subacute to rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  in branches fleshy-fibrous when fresh, nearly the same color as or slightly more reddish than surface; stem base in radial section showing a band of pale 'camel brown', white above^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  yellow-orange (with yellow tips when very young)^, (Scates-Barnhart), light pinkish orange with yellow tips, 'pale to light orange', with tips 'light to sunflower yellow', (Marr) 
STEM  2-7cm x 1-3cm, single, cylindric or tapered; white or 'yellowish white', covered with subareolate patches [cracked somewhat like dried mud] of 'brown to reddish brown' superficial hyphae; rough^, (Marr), single, narrowing to base or equal, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  +/- unpleasant^, (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  mild or bitter^, (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  unknown, (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October and November^, (Marr), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the surface, associated with Abies spp. (true fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'grayish yellowish orange'^, (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 4-6 microns, average 9.5 x 4.6 microns, subcylindric, coarse irregularly shaped cyanophilic warts, prominent lateral apiculus up to 2 x 2 microns^; basidia 2-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 41-70 x 7-11 microns, clavate, without clamp connections, sometimes the basidial cell giving rise to lateral branches; clamp connections absent, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'quickly turning green', probably referring to the rapid green reaction of flesh to ferric sulphate
SIMILAR  Ramaria coulterae also show the brown patch in stem base flesh, but the reaction to ferric sulphate in water is only weakly positive, (Petersen(3)). Ramaria formosa has a similar color, but discolors with handling and has clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria amyloidea is similar in color and in having a band of brown in the radially sectioned stem base, blue-green reaction to ferric sulphate, and amyloid stem flesh, but R. amyloidea is different in fruitbody form, in spore ornamentation and in having clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria velocimutans also has a band of brown in the radially sectioned stem base, and reacts quickly to ferric sulphate, but is larger and has whitish fruitbodies, (Marr). Pacific Northwest species reacting with ferric sulphate 10% aqueous solution in the stem base include Ramaria testaceoflava (clamped basidia, average spore length 11.8 microns), Ramaria amyloidea (clamped basidia, average spore length 8.9 microns, stem flesh amyloid), Ramaria velocimutans (clamped basidia, average spore length 9.0 microns, stem flesh inamyloid), Ramaria rubiginosa (clampless basidia, no rusty root), R. celerivirescens (clampless basidia, rusty root, normal terminal branches), and Ramaria claviramulata (clampless basidia, rusty root, chubby terminal branches, branches turn reddish brown with 20% KOH), (Exeter(2)).
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in single quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1)*, Exeter(2), Exeter(3)*, Petersen(3) (discussing Ramaria coulterae)
36
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria claviramulata  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 63. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria claviramulata has 1) beige branches, 2) chubby terminal branches with tips rich yellow when young but colored as branches when old, 3) immediate dark green reaction of stem flesh with 10% ferric sulphate, 4) the spore-bearing surface turning mahogany with 20% KOH, 5) spores with a large apiculus, and with low meandering longitudinal ridges and isolated warts (in aniline blue), and 6) thick-walled stem trama hyphae.^ The inflation of branches, noted by Marr & Stuntz in the type and in a few branches of one fruitbody in the California collection, is also found in R. obtusissima, as well as other taxa (usually when old): it may be a pathological condition, rather than being caused only by age, (Petersen). RANGE Collections were examined from WA and CA, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh instantly dark green with ferric sulphate in water, KOH 'oxide red' to orange, IKI branch sections sometimes weakly positive^, (Petersen), both fresh and dried specimens reacting with 20% KOH, dried specimens turning 'oxide red', stem flesh inamyloid, (Marr), fertile surfaces turning mahogany on application of 20% KOH, (Scates-Barnhart)
FRUITING BODY  3.5-4.5cm wide, 5-9cm high, ovate to obovate in outline, stem single to fasciculate [bundled]^, major branches several, not round in cross-section, ascending, not curved, superficially rugulose [finely wrinkled], branches in 4-6 ranks, of two types 1) more or less round in cross-section, dichotomous, up to 0.4cm wide, internodes long, up to 3cm in lower part, diminishing gradually at maturity, and 2) inflated up to 1.3cm wide, rugulose, axils rounded, tips cusped to antler-like or molar-like on inflated branches, (Petersen), branches generally not diminishing greatly in width upwards, some terminal branches distinctively enlarged, up to 1.5cm wide, resembling some of the irregular clubs of Clavariadelphus, (Marr)
FLESH  in branches soft, punky; in stem homogeneous, moist (but not slippery or gelatinous), ivory to tan, (Petersen), fleshy-fibrous when fresh, brownish white (Marr), base flesh with "rusty root" (with band of brown context hyphae), (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  pastel salmon-ocher to 'greyish orange'; tips rich yellow when young, colored as branches when old^, (Petersen), beige ('grayish orange'), tips mostly the same color, (Marr)
STEM  single to fasciculate, 1.4cm long and 0.5-2cm wide, moderately rounded to tapered, slender, without abortive branchlets; "cream colored to brownish white, strongly rubri-brunnescent, sometimes in subareolate regions of darker brown"^, (Petersen), brownish white, sometimes with subareolate regions [cracked somewhat like dried mud] of superficial hyphae of darker brown, (Marr)
ODOR  musty, krauty, medicinal^, (Petersen), musty (Marr)
TASTE  bitter^ (Petersen, Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), fall^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.6-11.9 x 4.3-6.1 microns, average 10.03 x 5.31 microns, cylindric to elliptic, "flattened adaxially, smooth to very obscurely undulate in profile", "ornamentation of very low, lobed, meandering ridges and a few isolated warts longitudinally oriented", appearing in aniline blue "as though paint has been dropped on the spore apex and had dripped down the length of the spore wall", but "in optical section under phase contrast, most spores appear almost smooth, with some subtly undulate"; contents yellow to ocher, refringent (all of the cytoplasm, not just the droplet, an unusual feature for Ramaria), without droplets or with one droplet, the droplet deeply ochraceous, wall up to 0.3 microns thick, apiculus very prominent, conic, appearing thick-walled under phase contrast, and appearing "glassy, under both phase contrast and bright field microscopy, as though constructed of solid wall material"^; lacking clamp connections; stem trama hyphae 5-23 microns wide and extremely thick-walled (1-5 microns thick), (Petersen), basidia 2-4-spored, 65-75 x 8-8.5 microns, clavate, without basal clamp connection; spores of type embedded in thickened hymenium and subhymenium, which combined are 135-211 microns thick, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with club-shaped small branches'
SIMILAR  The fast green reaction with 10% ferric sulphate also occurs with Ramaria celerivirescens, (Marr). Pacific Northwest species reacting with ferric sulphate 10% aqueous solution in the stem base include Ramaria testaceoflava (clamped basidia, average spore length 11.8 microns), Ramaria amyloidea (clamped basidia, average spore length 8.9 microns, stem flesh amyloid), Ramaria velocimutans (clamped basidia, average spore length 9.0 microns, stem flesh inamyloid), Ramaria rubiginosa (clampless basidia, no rusty root), Ramaria celerivirescens (clampless basidia, rusty root, normal terminal branches), and R. claviramulata (clampless basidia, rusty root, chubby terminal branches, branches turn reddish brown with 20% KOH), (Exeter).
SOURCES  Petersen(2), Marr(1) (colors in single quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(2)
37
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria concolor  (Corner) R.H. Petersen  forma concolor^  Bibliotheca 43: 54. 1975;  Ramaria stricta  var. concolor  Corner
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria concolor forma concolor include 1) small to medium size, 2) lower branches dull ochraceous to tan, becoming darker when old, 3) upper branches and tips pale tan or ocher-tan, 4) any part of fruitbody bruising brownish with a slight violet tint, 5) often branching from the base, 6) branches normally erect and crowded, (but in forma marrii more open and lax), arising from a mycelial mat on wood with associated white rhizomorphs, 7) spores with ornamentation of inconspicuous low warts or ridges, 8) clamp connections, and 9) two or three types of hyphae in the rhizomorphs.^ In forma marrii, branches are open, lax, curved ascending (as opposed to erect, often crowded, and neither lax or nor open). In forma fumida (Ontario and New York) stem and lower branches are deep chocolate brown. RANGE Collections of forma concolor were examined from BC, ID, NS, ON, CT, IN, MA, MD, ME, MI, NC, NH, NY, OH, PA, TN, VT, and VA, (Petersen). It is common in eastern North America, occasional to rare in Pacific coastal regions from MT westward, and rare in northern Europe, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  spore-bearing surface in ferric sulphate in water slowly slate green, yellow-brown to copper-brown in KOH
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm wide, up to 14cm high, major branches several, up to 0.8cm thick, "usually divergent secondarily by the force of the copious branches above, but in one form lax-ascending"^, "branches numerous, erect, strict to lax and open in one form", axils rounded, often somewhat teardrop-shaped, internodes up to 1.5cm long in lower part, gradually shortening, upper branches and tips erect, rounded in cross-section, strict, often somewhat prolonged, dichotomous to polychotomous, (Petersen), 3-7cm wide, 5-10cm high, habit generally open and branches moderately divaricate, axils obtuse, branches slender and internodes of mature specimens elongated, single or bifid near the tips, tips acute, (Marr)
FLESH  consistency rubbery when fresh^ (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  branches dull ochraceous to tan, becoming darker when old, axils in one form [tsugina] green to pallid blue-green, upper branches and tips some shade of pale tan or ocher-tan, but not yellow or greenish yellow, (Petersen), any part of the fruitbody bruising brownish with a slight violet tint, (Marr)
STEM  "variable, up to 1.2cm thick, often branched almost from the base", often channeled or lobed in cross-section; "in most varieties cinnamon when fresh and young", becoming somewhat violaceous or ruddy when old; "arising from a small, white, tomentose to felty basal mycelial mat", usually quickly becoming involved in a copious tangle of stout (up to 0.25cm thick), "white, ropy rhizomorphic strands permeating the substrate to an indefinite distance"^, (Petersen), 2cm x 0.6cm, single, slender, or several primary axes arising from the wood, arising from conspicuous rhizomorphic strands and a white spreading mycelial mat, (Marr)
ODOR  strong, of anise or aromatic, (Petersen), sweet (Marr)
TASTE  mildly bitter to bitter, sometimes mildly acrid [peppery]^, (Petersen), bitter (Marr)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single, gregarious, or cespitose [in tufts] on rotting wood (coniferous or hardwood), (Petersen), on hardwood under Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.8-10 x 3.7-4.8 microns, average 8.06 x 4.20 microns, elongate-ovoid to elliptic, adaxially flattened, roughened in profile, ornamentation of very obscure, low warts or ridges, moderately cyanophilic, hardly anastomosing, and ill-defined, without droplets or with one acyanophilic droplet, wall up to 0.3 microns thick, moderately cyanophilic^; "hymenium usually unilateral, with sterile areas restricted to narrow lines decurrent from axils"; hyphae of basal mat and rhizomorphic strands of 3 types: generative (2.2-4.5 microns, generally thin-walled, colorless, densely interwoven, conspicuously clamped, inflated clamp connections up to 11 microns broad, thin-walled to slightly thick-walled - wall up to 0.4 microns thick, unornamented), skeletal (1.6-2.6 microns wide, usually less than 2.2 microns wide, straight, thick-walled up to 0.7 microns, colorless, aseptate, arising from clamp connection but ending blindly), and very rare gloeoplerous hyphae (3.4-4.5 microns wide, "thin-walled, with foamy, coscinoidal contents strongly cyanophilous"), (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'of the same color'
SIMILAR  Ramaria tsugina has branch tips and/or axils or base greenish, as well as different stem morphology and spore size, (Exeter(3)). Ramaria stricta var. stricta has yellow to yellow-green branch tips (instead of creamy tan to beige), is more common in the western part of North America than R. concolor, and fruits on coniferous wood (but sometimes on hardwood).
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Marr(1) (as R. stricta var. concolor), Exeter(3)*
38
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria concolor  (Corner) R.H. Petersen  forma marrii  R.H. Petersen^  Bibliotheca Mycologica 43: 62. 1975
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria concolor forma marrii differs from R. concolor forma concolor in that branches are open, lax, curved ascending (as opposed to erect, often crowded, and neither lax nor open). Note also the occurrence of cystidioid elements in the type.^ RANGE Forma marrii has been found in WA, ID, and CA, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  conforming to reactions of Ramaria concolor forma concolor
FRUITING BODY  up to 4.5cm wide, up to 6cm high, stem, repeatedly branched, branches lax, curved-ascending^, open to somewhat divaricate, mostly dichotomous, axils open to lunate, tips delicate, digitate, (Petersen)
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  branches red-tan, axils colored as surrounding branches, tips cream tan to pale tan^, (Castellano), branches "cinnamon buff", tips "cream buff" to "light ochraceous buff", (Petersen)
STEM  up to 1.5cm long, red-tan, bruising brown, arising from a white mycelial mat and white rhizomorphic strands^, (Castellano), up to 1.5cm long, "cinnamon buff", bruising to "sayal brown", arising from white mycelial mat and white rhizomorphic strands, (Petersen) 
ODOR  not pronounced, weakly of anise^, (Petersen)
TASTE  bitter, weakly astringent, not acrid [not peppery]^, (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on rotting deciduous wood^, (Petersen), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the surface of the ground [illustrated on wood], associated with Abies spp. (true fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  "microscopic characters and spores conforming to species description" [of Ramaria concolor forma concolor]^; cystidioid structures in hymenium hyphal, 1.5 microns wide, projecting from hymenial surface up to 40 microns, thin-walled, gnarled, often once-branched, leptocystidial, (Petersen), spores 7.8-10 x 3.7-4.8 microns, elongate, ovoid to elliptic, ornamentation of obscure, low warts or ridges, thin-walled, moderately cyanophilic, apiculus prominent, eccentric, and truncate, (Castellano, likely reiterating data from description of R. concolor forma concolor); [presumably with clamp connections, as this is characteristic of subgenus Lentoramaria]
NAME ORIGIN  after Dr. Currie D. Marr, author of the definitive publication on the genus in Washington
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Castellano(1)*
39
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria conjunctipes  (Coker) Corner  var. sparsiramosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 67. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria conjunctipes var. sparsiramosa forms a dense bundle of stringy white stems giving rise sparsely to branches that are salmon-pink to salmon orange or pale tan, with yellow tips. Spores are obscurely roughened, and clamp connections are absent.^ RANGE It has been found in WA and CA, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  up to 4cm wide and 6cm high, subspherical to broadly obovate in outline, stem fasciculate (bundled) with up to 10 individuals^, branches of individual fruitbodies in 2-4 ranks, up to 0.3cm wide in lower part, 0.2cm or less in upper part, round in cross-section, axils narrowly rounded, internode ration diminishing rather abruptly apically when mature, tips minutely double-dichotomous when young, minutely digitate by maturity, (Castellano), fruitbody cluster 10cm x 3-5cm, primary axes very slender, mostly 0.2-0.3cm wide, resembling a fascicle of white strings, branching very sparse, mostly 2-3 ranks near the tips, (Marr)
FLESH  in branches solid to locally hollow, somewhat stringy; white^, (Castellano)
BRANCH COLOR  "fleshy pallid salmon to salmon colored to pale tan"; tips pale yellow^, (Castellano - unclear whether 'fleshy' refers to consistency or is part of color), pale salmon orange, salmon, pale ochraceous buff, tips pale yellow, (Exeter)
STEM  up to 0.4cm wide, slender, rooting somewhat, fasciculate [bundled] "with up to 10 individuals, loosely bound by superficial white tomentum"; stem white where protected^, (Castellano)
ODOR  negligible to mildly aromatic^ (Castellano)
TASTE  negligible to mildly fabaceous [bean-like]^ (Castellano)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  fruits in humus or soil and matures above ground, associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), November^, (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.2-9.7 x 4.7-5.8 microns, broadly ovate to broadly cylindric, obscurely roughened, small warts often indiscernible^; trama of branches of parallel thin-walled colorless hyphae up to 10 microns wide; clamp connections absent, (Castellano)
NAME ORIGIN  'sparsiramosa' means 'with sparse branches'
SIMILAR  Var. sparsiramosa differs from Ramaria conjunctipes var. tsugensis in its more densely fasciculate habit, slender form and smaller fruitbodies. In R. conjunctipes var. sparsiramosa the base is a dense fascicle of stringy white stems, branching is sparse in upper part, and fruitbodies are rarely taller than 10cm, whereas in R. conjunctipes var. tsugensis, the base is single to densely fasciculate, stems are not stringy, branching is dense in the upper part, and fruitbodies are up to 18cm tall. (Marr). "It is likely that R. fasciculata var. tsugensis does not differ significantly from R. fasciculata var. sparsiramosa and that further study will conclude that they are synonymous" (Castellano(1) with Latin names in italics).
SOURCES  Castellano(1)* (as R. fasciculata var. sparsiramosa), Marr(1), Exeter(1) (as Ramaria fasciculata var. sparsiramosa)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria conjunctipes  (Coker) Corner  var. tsugensis  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 65. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria conjunctipes var. tsugensis forms a dense bundle of white stems (rarely a single stem), giving rise to branches that are salmon-pink to salmon orange with a waxy translucent appearance, with yellow tips. Spores are finely ornamented with linearly lobed warts or sometimes smooth, and clamp connections are absent.^ RANGE It has been found in WA (Marr) and Desjardin(6) illustrates it from CA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem base inamyloid^ (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem context (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  3-7cm wide, 4.5-18cm high, shrubby, fasciculate (bundled), robust^, branching 3-6 times, dichotomously, branches elongated, lower internodes up to 3cm, slender, frequently less than 0.5cm wide, often hollow, compact and subparallel, axils narrowly U-shaped, mostly bifid near tips, tips subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  in branches fleshy-pliable or rubbery when fresh, colored as surface; similar to translucent plastic when dried^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  pale to light pinkish orange (salmon to peach) with a waxy translucent look, dull violet bruised spots sometimes occurring on branches; tips yellow^, (Scates-Barnhart), branches above ground 'salmon to peach', with waxy translucent quality, tips 'light to maize yellow', inconspicuous faint 'dull violet' bruised areas sometimes present, (Marr) 
STEM  base rarely single, usually a fascicle [bundle] of steeply to slightly bulbous stems that are covered with a white tomentum where buried; underground part of fresh fruitbody white^, (Marr)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Phillips)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October^, (Marr), September to October (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  about 'golden yellow'
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-10 x 4-6.5 microns, average 7.5 x 4.9 microns, ovate or short-elliptic, finely ornamented with linearly lobed, cyanophilic warts, some spores nearly smooth^; basidia 1-4-spored, 35-67 x 6-10 microns, clavate, clampless, occasionally 2 sterigmata connate [joined] at base; clamp connections absent, (Marr), basidia not clamped (Exeter)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'from Tsuga (hemlock)'
SIMILAR  Ramaria conjunctipes var. sparsiramosa has more densely fasciculate habit, slender form and smaller fruitbodies. In R. conjunctipes var. sparsiramosa the base is a dense fascicle of stringy white stems, branching is sparse in the upper part, and fruitbodies are rarely taller than 10cm, whereas in R. conjunctipes var. tsugensis, the base is single to densely fasciculate, stems are not stringy, branching is dense in the upper part, and fruitbodies are up to 18cm tall, (Marr). "It is likely that R. fasciculata var. tsugensis does not differ significantly from R. fasciculata var. sparsiramosa and that further study will conclude that they are synonymous" (Castellano(1) with Latin names are in italics). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Phillips(1)*, Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1) (under Ramaria fasciculata var. sparsiramosa), Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria coulterae  Scates^  Mycotaxon 33: 115. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  Elsie's stringy pinky
NOTES  Features of Ramaria coulterae include 1) small to medium size, 2) massive stem usually with a brownish fan-shaped area when cut longitudinally ("rusty-root"), 3) whitish stem and major branches, 4) branches pale yellow to very pale beige, darkening to buffy flesh-color, 5) crowded crested tips deep dull fleshy red to fleshy beige fading to fleshy pink then whitish, 6) stringy flesh in lower branches, 7) spring fruiting, 8) smooth or almost smooth spores, and 9) absent clamp connections.^ RANGE Collections were examined from ID, OR, and CA (Petersen(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ferric sulphate in water weakly positive (green), probably with branch sections; IKI on stem flesh negative, (Petersen), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm wide, up to 12cm high, broadly obpyriform [reverse pear-shaped] to somewhat circular in outline^, stem single, major branches 3 to several, very short when young, hardly round in cross-section, branches in 3-6 ranks, very short when young, internodes all short when young, axils acute when young, tips very crowded, cauliflower-like, and stiff when young, often expanding to knobby when old, at all ages with 4-6 crests on each tip, minutely pluridentate, (Petersen)
FLESH  in lower branches stringy, fibrous and ripping longitudinally, in upper ones very brittle and crisp, white; in stem watery but not slippery or gelatinous, off-white, usually with brownish fan-shaped area when cut longitudinally^, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches white to ivory-colored, branches 'pale yellow' (Kornerup(2) color) to very pale beige, darkening to buffy flesh-color when old, tips deep dull fleshy red to fleshy beige when young, quickly fading to fleshy pink in adolescence, when old colored as the major branches, at all ages with very tips brown to dark brown^, (Petersen), branches white to ivory in lower part, upwards pale yellow to pale beige, darkening to buffy flesh (pinkish buff), very slowly bruising dull bluish violet, tips deep dull fleshy red to fleshy beige, quickly fading to light vinaceous cinnamon (fleshy pink), when old tips colored as branches but very tips brown, outside abortive branches often curling downward when old, (Scates-Barnhart), off-white, pale yellow to pale pink-tan, darkening to pink-tan to tan-pink when old; tips pale red to flesh pink-tan when young, quickly fading to pink, when old colored as major branches, (Castellano)
STEM  up to 8cm long and up to 6cm wide, single, large to massive, deeply lined or channeled, smooth or with very blunt abortive processes or branchlets; white to off-white, slowly brunnescent to pallid purple-gray where handled or bruised; smooth, not pruinose or tomentose^, (Petersen)
ODOR  negligible^ (Petersen)
TASTE  mildly nutty^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  "probably edible and good", (Petersen)
HABITAT  on coniferous debris in coniferous forest, spring and early summer^, (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.3-12.6 x 2.9-4.0 microns, average 9.95 x 3.54 microns, narrowly elliptic to cylindric, smooth to obscurely undulate in profile, smooth or with a few ill-defined small low warts, uninucleate, homogeneous or with 1 or 2 droplets, the droplets dark yellow and refringent, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, hilar appendix small and gradual^; basidia 4-spored, 50-70 x 7-9 microns, clavate, clampless, minutely multiguttulate when mature; stem trama hyphae 4-13 microns wide, colorless, clampless, thick-walled, tightly interwoven, not agglutinated or adherent, ampulliform inflations up to 15 microns wide, aliiform, thick-walled, with extensive and coarse stalactitiform ornamentation, gloeoplerous hyphae occasional, 4-15 microns wide, thin-walled, yellow-refringent; tramal hyphae of upper branches 4-14 microns wide, colorless, clampless, thin-walled to thick-walled, parallel, free to adherent, ampulliform inflations and gloeoplerous hyphae not observed, (Petersen), true clamp connections absent but false clamp connections present (Scates-Barnhart)
NAME ORIGIN  in honor of Elsie Coulter, longtime collector and mycological enthusiast in northern Idaho and 20-year collecting companion of Kit Scates-Barnhart
SIMILAR  Ramaria celerivirescens may be similar, and also shows the brown patch of stem flesh, but the flesh turns instantly dark green in ferric sulphate in water, (Petersen). Ramaria velocimutans also shows brown patch of stem flesh but turns quickly blue green in ferric sulphate in water, (Scates-Barnhart). Ramaria flavobrunnescens var. aromatica always seems to show a yellow-brown diffuse patch of flesh at the stem base, but it fruits in the fall and its hyphae have clamp connections, (Petersen). "I know of two other similar taxa with brownish stipe flesh which do not show the instantaneous color change in ferric salts, but hyphae of both are clamped, neither has been described, and both are autumnal fruiters", (Petersen(3)). Ramaria rubripermanens has a positive iodine reaction on the stem flesh, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(2)*, Exeter(3)*, Kornerup(2)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria cyaneigranosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. cyaneigranosa^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 68. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  pink coral
NOTES  {See also Red Ramaria Table.} Ramaria cyaneigranosa is small to medium, with red to pink branches that have yellow tips or sometimes the tips almost the same color as the branches, and basidia have masses of cyanophilous granules. Var. cyaneigranosa has intensely reddish branches and yellow branch tips.^ RANGE It has been found at least in WA, (Marr). Unspecified varieties of R. cyaneigranosa have also been found in OR and CA, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh non-amyloid; ferric sulphate in water on stem flesh negative^, (Exeter(3))
FRUITING BODY  2-12cm wide, 4-12cm high, branching 3-5 times from the base^, lower nodes usually polychotomous, branches frequently connate, axils acute to U-shaped and branches slightly divergent, internodes often short, branches slender to somewhat flattened and wider, shortly forked, polydigitate or nodulose near tips, tips subacute to rounded, (Marr) 
FLESH  softly stringy; paler than outside^, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy-fibrous when fresh, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light red, about peach-pink, shell-pink, or shrimp-pink; tips like the branches or yellow at the very top^, (Scates-Barnhart), light red, tips colored as branches or minutely dotted with light yellow or reddish yellow, (Exeter(1))
STEM  0.5-3.5 x 0.4-3cm, single to subcompound, arising from slender taproot-like structure, stem or compound axes of stem frequently thick or slightly bulbous; underground part of fresh fruitbody white^, (Marr)
ODOR  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on the ground under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-15 x 4-6 microns, average 11.0 x 4.6 microns, with small lobed warts^; basidia with protoplasm granular and cyanophilous; clamp connections absent, (Exeter(1)), spores illustrated with fairly short, fairly thick, angled or sometimes forking lines; basidia 1-4-spored, 49-80 x 6-12 microns, clavate, clampless, protoplasm granular and cyanophilous; hymenium and subhymenium 60-70 microns thick; subhymenial hyphae 2-5 microns wide, interwoven, thin-walled, without clamp connections; context hyphae parallel to interwoven in the base, parallel in the branches, crystalloid masses occurring in the context of the stem, hyphae noninflated in the stem, 3-10 microns wide, slightly to highly inflated in the branches, walls smooth, cyanophilous, thin-walled, ampulliform inflations near septa 9-14 microns wide, walls of the swellings slightly ornamented, clamp connections absent, false clamp connections sometimes present, gleoplerous hyphae present, 2-4 microns except in localized bulbous regions where they may be up to 5-6 microns wide, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  likely referring to cyanophilous granules seen microscopically
SIMILAR  "The three varieties of R. cyaneigranosa are separated on fruiting body color, form, and spore length. Variety cyaneigranosa has the longest spores, the most intensely reddish branches, and yellow tips. Variety elongata has a slender spindly habit and reddish primordial tips fading during maturation but never yellow, and var. persicina is peach or salmon, not at all red, with minutely yellow-dotted tips." (Marr). Ramaria araiospora var. rubella is magenta red, (Marr). Ramaria araiospora var. araiospora may be difficult to separate on color, but 1) has finely ornamented spores 4 microns wide or less, 2) lacks basidia containing numerous cyanophilous granules, and 3) does not change color significantly on application of phenol or aniline, (Marr). Ramaria stuntzii is bright scarlet when young, and is easily distinguished by its robust habitat and amyloid flesh, (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(1), Exeter(3)*, Castellano(1)* (variety not specified)

LATIN NAMES  Ramaria cyaneigranosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. elongata  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 70. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  pink pencils
NOTES  {See also Red Ramaria Table.} Ramaria cyaneigranosa var. elongata is medium-sized and slender. Its branches are peach to pastel-red when young, fading to brownish salmon. The mature tips are colored like the branches and the young tips are more intensely reddish.^ RANGE It is found at least in WA, (Marr).

FRUITING BODY  1.5-3.5cm wide, 8-13cm high, branches parallel and ascending^, (Scates-Barnhart), very slender, component axes of base fusing into a small primary root-like process, small basal branch system with light red tips frequent, mature fruitbody branching up to 7 times, internodes elongated up to 4cm and branches parallel ascending, mostly dichotomous and axils turbinate, branches slender, 0.1-0.5cm diameter, mostly bifid near tips, tips subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  softly stringy, covered like surface or paler^, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy fibrous when fresh, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  peach to pastel-red when young, fading to brownish salmon, mature tips colored like branches, young tips more intensely reddish^, (Scates-Barnhart), pastel-red when young, fading to brownish salmon; tips light red, never yellow, maturing colored as branches, (Exeter(1))
STEM  base 1-4cm x 0.3-3cm, small, subcompound; white; covered by white basal tomentum^, (Marr)
ODOR  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock)^, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-10 x 4-5 microns, average 9.2 x 4.5 microns, small lobed warts, basidia with protoplasm granular and cyanophilous^; clamp connections absent, (Exeter(1)), spores illustrated with fairly short, fairly thick, angled or sometimes forking lines; hymenium and subhymenium combined about 80 microns thick; context hyphae: crystalloid masses occurring in stem context, hyphae slightly inflated, 4-15 microns wide, walls smooth to fluted, cyanophilic, thin to moderately thick, 0.25-2 microns, localized bulbous regions in the gleoplerous hyphae up to 23 microns wide; other structures similar to var. cyaneigranosa, (Marr), basidia not clamped (Exeter(3))
NAME ORIGIN  'elongata' means elongate, referring to slender form
SIMILAR  "The three varieties of R. cyaneigranosa are separated on fruiting body color, form, and spore length. Variety cyaneigranosa has the longest spores, the most intensely reddish branches, and yellow tips. Variety elongata has a slender spindly habit and reddish primordial tips fading during maturation but never yellow, and var. persicina is peach or salmon, not at all red, with minutely yellow-dotted tips." (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(1), Exeter(3)*

ENGLISH NAMES  peach fan
NOTES  {See also Red Ramaria Table.} Ramaria cyaneigranosa var. persicina is small and fan-like, with peach or salmon branches that have minutely yellow-dotted tips.^ RANGE It has been found at least in WA (Marr). There is a collection from BC at the University of British Columbia.

FLESH  fleshy when young, soft-stringy when old; colored like surface^, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy fibrous when fresh, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light peach-pink (salmon to peach); tips yellow, (Scates-Barnhart), salmon; tips minutely dotted with yellow, (Exeter(1))

Variety cyaneigranosa has the longest spores, the most intensely reddish branches, and yellow tips. Variety elongata has a slender spindly habit and reddish primordial tips fading during maturation but never yellow, and var. persicina is peach or salmon, not at all red, with minutely yellow-dotted tips." (Marr).

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria cystidiophora  (Kauffman) Corner  var. anisata  R.H. Petersen^  Mycologia 80(2): 223. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata differs from other varieties of R. cystidiophora by salmon and yellow color pattern (branches pallid salmon, tips light clear yellow when young but fading slightly by maturity), and a strong spicy-anise odor^ The description is derived from Petersen(2) except where otherwise noted. RANGE  It has been found in Mendocino County in CA (Petersen), and Benton and Lane Counties in OR (Exeter).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  IKI gives purple-green reaction on stem flesh; ferric chloride in water positive reaction, probably with branch sections, (Peterson), 2 Oregon collections recorded as stem flesh non-amyloid and stem flesh negative to ferric sulphate in water, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 6cm across and up to 11cm high, obpyriform [reverse pear-shaped], major branches two to several^, ascending, often slightly grooved, branches in 3-5 ranks, more or less circular in cross-section, internodes diminishing gradually when mature, axils narrowly rounded to acute, tips irregularly cristate [crested] when young, extending somewhat and becoming dichotomous-divaricate by maturity
FLESH  in branches stringy, white internally but colored like surface near the surface; in stem off-white, moist but not slippery
BRANCH COLOR  pallid salmon, opaque, often flushed ruddy when old, tips light clear yellow when young, fading slightly by maturity
STEM  up to 5cm long and 1.6cm wide, single (often falsely fasciculate), slender, narrowing gradually downward, sometimes with a few abortive branchlets, rooting, often geniculate [knee-shaped] or gnarled below ground level; hoary white, weakly watery-brunnescent [turning brown] when handled
ODOR  strong, spicy, vaguely of anise
TASTE  negligible
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  coniferous forests^ (Exeter)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7.6-8.6 x 3.2-3.9 microns, average 8.20 x 3.66 microns, cylindric to narrowly elliptic, roughened in profile, with scattered small flat warts, sometimes arranged in oblique rows, contents of dense amorphous dark yellow inclusions, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus indistinct, thick^; basidia 4-spored, 55-65 x 7-8 microns, narrowly clavate, clamped, contents multigranular and/or multiguttulate, especially proximally, moderately cyanophilic; stem flesh of the following three types: 1) generative hyphae 4-16 microns wide, inflated especially in inner stem, colorless, clamped, thick-walled (up to 0.5 microns), more or less parallel, free, ampulliform clamps up to 20 microns wide, asymmetrical, irregularly shaped to aliiform, not unusually thick-walled, with delicate, local stalactitiform ornamentation, 2) gloeoplerous hyphae 1.5-3.5 microns wide, meandering, yellow-refringent, more common in inner stem, and 3) superficial turf hyphae resembling dendrohyphidia, arising from a clamp connection, more or less perpendicular to stem surface, aseptate, subrefringent, subdiverticulate (with sharp spines or narrow branches up to 11 microns long arising randomly), ending in an acerose tip
NAME ORIGIN  'anisata' refers to anise odor
SIMILAR  Ramaria conjunctipes var. tsugensis has much broader spores and lacks clamp connections, (Petersen). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cystidiophora var. citronella, Ramaria cystidiophora var. cystidiophora, and Ramaria cystidiophora var. fabiolens.
SOURCES  Petersen(2), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria cystidiophora  (Kauffman) Corner  var. citronella  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 73. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  lemon fuzzy-foot
NOTES  Ramaria cystidiophora is characterized by clear bright yellow branches when fresh, the whitish base bearing pronounced white basal tomentum and often somewhat bundled, and microscopic characters including finely warted narrow spores and acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae. Var. citronella differs from var. cystidiophora by its pungently sweet odor, resembling Citrus blossoms, and by its larger spores and non-gelatinizing hyphae.^ RANGE Ramaria cystidiophora var. citronella is found in WA (Marr). There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia and collections from WA, OR, and ID at the University of Washington.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  2-12cm wide, 5-17cm high, medium size, usually taller than broad, base single or semifused from several stems, branches slender in many tiers, tips +/- pointed^, (Scates-Barnhart), 2-12cm wide, 5-17(22)cm high, mostly medium size, usually taller than broad, base single or compound, the compound base consisting of several partly connate axes, small basal branch systems often present, branching up to 7 times from the base, mostly dichotomous, mature internodes usually elongated, up to 3.5cm long, branches slender, about 1.5cm maximum width, mostly 0.2-0.8cm wide, axils subacute or narrowly U-shaped and branches slight to moderately divergent, forked or cristate [crested] near tips, tips subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-stringy; yellowish white^, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  tips bright yellow, branches lighter yellow^, (Scates-Barnhart), branches 'light yellow' (Kornerup(2) color), tips colored the same or more intensely yellow, (Marr)
STEM  base 1-8cm x 0.5-4.5cm, single or compound, underground part of fruitbody white, base and lower branches brunnescent, about 'caramel brown' (Kornerup(2) color), or sometimes with faint reddish tinge; base covered with a well defined white tomentum^, (Marr)
ODOR  like lemon blossoms^, (Scates-Barnhart), pungently sweet odor, resembling Citrus blossoms, (Marr)
TASTE  slightly bitter^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  probably edible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), September to November^, (Marr), in deep humus under Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), and Tsuga heterophylla, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'apricot-yellow'^ (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-13 x 3.5-5 microns, average 10.3 x 4.2 microns, subcylindric with slight dorsal convexity and a suprahilar depression, finely ornamented with cyanophilous warts (illustrated as linear, thin, and sometimes forking once)^; basidia 2-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 53-75 x 7-11 microns, clavate, clamped; acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae interweaving throughout the peripheral stem context, slender, contorted, bearing lateral spine-like branches, thin-walled, and containing a densely cyanophilic granular mass, (Marr), hyphae with clamp connections (Exeter)
NAME ORIGIN  refers to lemon odor
SIMILAR  Ramaria cystidiophora var. fabiolens has gelatinizing hyphae and a bean-like odor, (Marr). Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans stains reddish brown and has longer spores (average length 11.8 versus 10.3 microns), (Marr). Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata differs from other varieties of R. cystidiophora by its salmon and yellow color pattern (branches pallid salmon, tips light clear yellow when young but fading slightly by maturity), and a strong spicy-anise odor, the spores being 7.6-8.6 x 3.2-3.9 microns, averaging 8.2 x 3.7 microns, (Petersen(2)). Ramaria flavigelatinosa var. fragrans has a similar odor, but lacks clamp connections and lacks acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the base, (Marr). Ramaria flavobrunnescens var. aromatica lacks acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the base, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cystidiophora var. cystidiophora.
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*, Kornerup(2), Petersen(2) (discussing Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria cystidiophora  (Kauffman) Corner  var. cystidiophora^  Ann. Bot. Mem. 1: 573. 1950  
ENGLISH NAMES  fuzzy-foot, fuzzy-footed coral
NOTES  Ramaria cystidiophora is clear bright yellow when fresh, the whitish base bearing pronounced white basal tomentum and often somewhat bundled, and microscopic characters include finely warted narrow spores and acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae.^ RANGE The type was found in WA (Marr).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh non-amyloid; ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh^, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  10-12cm high, mostly of medium size^, primary branches about 0.3-0.4 wide, circular or nearly so in cross-section, internodes successively shorter upwards, axils obtuse to U-shaped, final branches finely divided, tips acute or subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  cartilaginous, moderately fragile when fresh^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light to buffy yellow, tips light yellow^, (Scates-Barnhart), "clear bright yellow when in prime condition", "buff-yellow" to "warm buff", final branches and tips "citron yellow", (Marr, colors from Ridgway)
STEM  long, 5-8cm x 1-1.2cm, or subfasciculate [somewhat bundled], immersed, ascending or lying down, thick just below the first node, narrowing downward; underground part yellowish white to pale yellow, white at the extreme base, (Marr), base white and covered by white tomentum, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  very distinctive - similar to anise^, (Marr), sweet anise-like (Exeter)
TASTE  mild^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  in deep humus under dense stands of Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock)^, (Marr), under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), fall, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-9 x 3-4 microns, average 8 x 3.6 microns, subcylindric, ornamented with fine warts^; basidia 4-spored, 51 x 7 microns, clavate, basally clamped, basidia and basidioles occasionally protruding beyond the hymenial layer 5-15 microns; contextual hyphae of stem gelatinized with embedded crystalloid deposits, peripheral regions of stem context contain acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae (1.5-2 microns wide, thin-walled, with cyanophilic granular contents, freely branching, multidirectional, bearing lateral spurs 2.5-6 microns long), (Marr), hyphae clamped, (Scates-Barnhart), the following description of stem hyphae in R. cystidiophora var. anisata gives additional information on the stem hyphae in this species: stem flesh of the following three types: 1) generative hyphae 4-16 microns wide, inflated especially in inner stem, colorless, clamped, thick-walled (up to 0.5 microns), more or less parallel, free, ampulliform clamp connections up to 20 microns wide, asymmetrical, irregularly shaped to aliiform, not unusually thick-walled, with delicate, local stalactitiform ornamentation, 2) gloeoplerous hyphae 1.5-3.5 microns wide, meandering, yellow-refringent, more common in inner stem, and 3) superficial turf hyphae resembling dendrohyphidia, arising from a clamp connection, more or less perpendicular to stem surface, aseptate, subrefringent, subdiverticulate (with sharp spines or narrow branches up to 11 microns long arising randomly), ending in an acerose tip, (Petersen(2)) 
NAME ORIGIN  means 'bearing cystidia'
SIMILAR  Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans has 'reddish brown' staining or bruising reaction and longer spores. Ramaria cystidiophora var. fabiolens has a string-bean-like odor, very broad fruitbody, and larger spores, (Marr). Ramaria cystidiophora var. citronella has a pungently sweet odor resembling Citrus blossoms, larger spores, and non-gelatinizing hyphae, (Marr). Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata has 1) a strongly spicy, somewhat anise-like odor, 2) branches that are pallid salmon, often flushed ruddy when old, with the tips light clear yellow when young, fading slightly by maturity, and 3) spores that measure 7.6-8.6 x 3.2-3.9 microns, with the average 8.2 x 3.7 microns, (Petersen(2)). Ramaria flavobrunnescens lacks the acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae, (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Petersen(2) (discussing Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria cystidiophora  (Kauffman) Corner  var. fabiolens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 75. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  beany fuzzy-foot
NOTES  Ramaria cystidiophora var. fabiolens differs from R. cystidiophora var. cystidiophora in having a string-bean-like odor, a very broad fruitbody, and larger spores.^ RANGE It has been found at least in WA (Marr). There is a collection from BC at the Pacific Forestry Centre.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  7-15cm wide, 9-18cm high, mostly of medium size, broad, single or fasciculate [bundled]^, frequently several axes from a connate basal mass, small abortive or primordial basal branch systems commonly present; branching up to 10 times from the base, nodes polychotomous or dichotomous, axils turbinate or acute and branches divaricate, internodes lengthening up to 5cm, usually less, branches generally slender, lower ones up to 1.5cm wide, upper branches mostly 0.1-0.5cm wide, forked or pluridigitate near tips, tips rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  cartilaginous-gelatinous in branches, base firmly gelatinous when fresh; branch flesh colored as surface, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light yellow with bright sunflower yellow tips, although this fades somewhat in older specimens^, (Phillips), 'light yellow', tips colored the same in older specimens or more intensely yellow in younger ones, about 'maize or sunflower yellow', (Marr, colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)) 
STEM  3-7cm x 1.5-4cm, single or fasciculate [bundled], frequently several axes arising from connate basal mass; base covered with more or less distinct white tomentum^, (Marr)
ODOR  like string-beans^ (Marr)
TASTE  slightly bitter or not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Phillips)
HABITAT  under Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), Taxus brevifolia (Western Yew), or Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October and November^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 3.5-5 microns, average 9.7 x 4.2 microns, subcylindric to elongate-elliptic with slight suprahilar depression, ornamented with small irregularly shaped cyanophilic warts^; basidia 1-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 45-70 x 7-10 microns, clavate, clamped; most of the contextual hyphae in the stem compactly interwoven and gelatinous, acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae interweaving in all directions throughout the peripheral layers of the stem, 1.5-2 microns wide, contorted, bearing lateral peg-like branches about 2 microns long, thin-walled, and containing a dense cyanophilic granular mass, (Marr), hyphae with clamp connections (Exeter)
NAME ORIGIN  'fabiolens' means 'with bean-like odor'
SIMILAR  Ramaria cystidiophora var. citronella has a pungently sweet odor and non-gelatinizing hyphae, (Marr). Ramaria cystidiophora var. anisata differs from other varieties of R. cystidiophora by 1) a salmon and yellow color pattern (branches pallid salmon, tips light clear yellow when young but fading slightly by maturity), 2) a strong spicy-anise odor, and 3) spores measuring 7.6-8.6 x 3.2-3.9 microns, with the average 8.2 x 3.7 microns, (Petersen(2)). R. flavigelatinosa var. flavigelatinosa is similar in odor, general appearance, and consistency, but lacks clamp connections, and lacks acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the base, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cystidiophora var. cystidiophora and Ramaria gelatinosa var. oregonensis.

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria distinctissima  R.H. Petersen & M. Zang  var. americana  R.H. Petersen^  Mycologia 80(2): 225. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria distinctissima var. americana include 1) medium size, with outline ovate, 2) branches pinkish flesh-colored to salmon, 3) tips bright orange when young and fresh, fading when old to the same color as the upper branches, 4) yellow to golden yellow lower branches and upper stem, 5) base white and tomentose, 6) long spores that have scattered warts and meandering ridges, and 7) clamp connections.^ The description is derived from Petersen(2) except where noted. RANGE Collections were examined from BC and ID (Petersen(2)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ferric chloride in water positive reaction, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh, stem flesh inamyloid, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 9cm wide and 14cm high, ovate in outline, major branches 2-5, more or less circular in outline^, branches in 3-6 ranks, round in cross-section, ascending, often somewhat divergent, internodes diminishing gradually when mature, axils narrowly rounded to acute, rounded in upper part, tips irregularly branched to crested when young, short-digitate at maturity
FLESH  under spore-bearing surface pastel orange, paler to white inwards; in stem solid, white, moist but not slippery
BRANCH COLOR  pinkish flesh-colored to salmon, tips bright orange when young and fresh, fading when old to the same color as the upper branches, (Petersen), flesh-pink to salmon, tips bright orange when young and fresh, fading to same color as upper branches when old, lower branches bright yellow, (Exeter)
STEM  up to 4cm x 2.5cm, narrowing downward to bluntly acute or obconic base, without abortive branchlets; white in lower part, above that becoming bright yellow to golden yellow, fading when old to flat yellow; tomentose at base, smooth above, (Petersen), included by Petersen in key to species exhibiting yellow color band on upper stem, (Exeter)
ODOR  negligible
TASTE  negligible
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  under mixed conifers, with species of Thuja and Tsuga predominant
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12.6-16.3 x 4.8-6.3(7.0) microns, average 13.7 x 5.3 microns, elliptic to subboletoid, often flattened adaxially, conspicuously roughened in profile, scattered prominent cyanophilous warts and short meandering ridges, 1 to several droplets, the droplets amorphous, dark yellow, and semi-refringent, spore wall up to 0.3 microns thick, hilar appendix not very prominent^; basidia (3-)4-spored, 60-70 x 10-13 microns, broadly clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous when young, with scattered refringent guttules at maturity; clamp connections often on tramal hyphae of upper branches, and usually not on tramal hyphae of stem
NAME ORIGIN  means 'very distinct'
SIMILAR  Ramaria largentii also shows spores as long, but is much paler, (Petersen who comments that R. largentii could conceivably represent faded or ill-colored specimens of R. distinctissima var. americana).
SOURCES  Petersen(2), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria flavigelatinosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. flavigelatinosa^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 81. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria flavigelatinosa var. flavigelatinosa are 1) small to medium height, 2) compound or somewhat compound base that is white at least in lower part, 3) yellow branches with tips colored the same or slightly brighter, 4) branch flesh the same color as the surface, 5) sometimes minute areas of lower part of fruitbody bruising dull violet, 6) flesh firm-gelatinous when fresh, 7) bean-like odor, 8) spores ornamented with cyanophilic warts of irregular shapes, and 9) absent clamp connections.^ Fruitbodies of Ramaria flavigelatinosa are yellow, with var. carnisalmonea and var. megalospora showing a blush of salmon on the branches, coming from the salmon branch flesh, (Petersen(12)). Spores are larger in var. megalospora, and odor is sweet in var. fragrans. RANGE Ramaria flavigelatinosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. flavigelatinosa has been found at least in WA and CA, (Marr). There are collections with variety unspecified from OR at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  3-24cm wide, 5-14cm high, small to medium height, sometimes very broad, stem compound or somewhat compound, lower branches often connate^, polychotomous, upper branches usually free, mostly dichotomous, internodes variously elongated up to 4cm, mostly 0.1-1cm wide, axils often turbinate and branches divaricate, pluridigitate or more commonly forked near tips, tips subacute to rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  firm-gelatinous when fresh, drying hard; in branches colored as surface; in stem translucent white^, (Marr), firmly gelatinous, like gelatin or cartilage; of branches white, of base translucent white, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  tips bright yellow, branches lighter yellow, lower parts of fruitbody occasionally with dull violet bruised spots^, (Scates-Barnhart); 'light yellow to maize yellow', tips colored the same or slightly brighter, about 'sunflower yellow', occasionally small to minute areas of the fruitbody bruising 'dull violet', (Marr)
STEM  general outline conic, basal mass 1.5-5.5cm x 1-6cm, base "compound or subcompound, consisting of several to numerous connate axes"; white at least in the lower parts; new primordial branch systems frequent at base^, (Marr), white, becoming yellow further up, (Phillips), 1.5-5cm x 1-6cm, usually a cluster of +/- fused stem-branches, base white, lower parts of fruitbody occasionally with dull violet bruised spots; base lacking white tomentum, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  fabaceous [bean-like]^ (Marr), of raw green beans (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  not known (Phillips)
HABITAT  terrestrial, under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (October-November)^, (Marr), September-November (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  between 'maize yellow' and 'apricot yellow'^, (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 3.5-4.5 microns, average 9.6 x 4.1 microns, subcylindric, distinctly ornamented with cyanophilic warts of irregular shapes^; basidia 2-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 41-60 x 6.5-10 microns, clavate, clampless, frequently containing cyanophilic globules; clamp connections absent, false clamp connections sometimes present, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'yellow-gelatinous'
SIMILAR  Ramaria cystidiophora var. fabiolens is similar in odor, general appearance, and consistency, but has clamp connections and has acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the stem base, (Marr).

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria flavigelatinosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. megalospora  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 84. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria flavigelatinosa var. megalospora differs from R. flavigelatinosa var. flavigelatinosa in having larger spores.^ Fruitbodies of Ramaria flavigelatinosa are yellow, with var. carnisalmonea and var. megalospora showing a blush of salmon on the branches, coming from the salmon branch flesh; although not appearing gelatinous when fresh, flesh of upper branches is gelatinous under the microscope: unlike R. gelatinosa in which tramal hyphal walls are disintegrated as part of the gelatinization process, hyphal walls remain intact in R. flavigelatinosa var. megalospora, while mucoid or gelatinous material is deposited outside or between the hyphae walls. RANGE Var. megalospora has been found at least in WA (Marr), and eastern North America (Phillips), including specifically NS (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid, ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh^ (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  within the range described for var. flavigelatinosa^, (Marr), up to 6cm wide, up to 10cm high, "2 or 3 major branches growing nearly erect from the base and dividing into numerous ranks of erect smaller branches with sharply finger-shaped tips that become more rounded" when old, (Phillips), up to 6cm wide, up to 10cm high, broadly fusiform to fan-shaped in outline, stems in bundles, major branches 2 or 3, nearly erect, more or less round in cross-section, branches in 5-7 ranks, erect, round in cross-section, axils rounded, internodes long, ratio diminishing gradually when mature, tips crowded, sharp-digitate when young, minutely thumb-shaped when old, (Petersen), 3-24cm wide, 5-14cm high, small to medium, often broad, lower branches sometimes fused, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  cartilaginous when fresh^, (Marr), "solid, firmly gelatinous to soapy, drying hard and cartilaginous; white with small somewhat translucent spots", (Phillips), in stem solid, firm-gelatinous to soapy in consistency; white, mottled with small subtranslucent spots, drying hard, cartilaginous; in branches salmon, when fresh not appearing gelatinous, (Petersen), in base cartilaginous with some +/- gelatinous streaks, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  slightly more orangy yellow, close to 'melon yellow', otherwise similar to var. flavigelatinosa^, (Marr), "branches yellow lower down, above pallid salmon color, becoming more orange-salmon, with bright yellow tips when young, fading to buff-yellow", (Phillips), major branches downward colored as stem (yellow when young), upward colored as branches which are pallid salmon colored to orange-salmon, tips bright yellow when young, fading to pallid yellow, (Petersen), orangy yellow to pallid salmon or orange-salmon, tips bright yellow when young but fading to pallid yellow or color of branches, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  ^base 1.5cm x 1cm, expanding upward into a dense cluster of irregularly shaped stems; "white underground, light yellow higher up where exposed", (Phillips), small, in fascicles up to 1.5cm x 1cm, irregular in shape, generally narrowing downward, often (but not always) with abortive branchlets; white when protected, upward where exposed light yellow when young mellowing when old to "pale pinkish cinnamon", (Petersen), base usually consisting of many fused stems, generally conic, 1.5-5.5cm x 1-6cm, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  within the range described for var. flavigelatinosa^, (Marr), mildly fabaceous [bean-like] (Petersen), mildly bean-like to not distinctive (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  within the range described for var. flavigelatinosa^, (Marr), none or faintly fabaceous [bean-like] (Petersen), mild (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Phillips, Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), September and October^, (Marr), August-September, coniferous woods in Pacific Northwest and deciduous woods in eastern North America, (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  clay color^ (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-15 x 4-6 microns, average 12 x 4.5 microns, subcylindric^; basidia 50-75 x 8-12 microns; context hyphae: vesiculation near septa less frequent than in var. flavigelatinosa, gelatinous matrix not as abundant, (Marr), 9.7-12.6 x 4.3-5.4 microns, average 10.89 x 4.82 microns, elliptic, flattened adaxially, with suprahilar bulge, distinctly roughened in profile, ornamented with coarse warts and nonoriented, sometimes complex ridges, contents with 1 or 2 dark, refringent droplets, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus gradual, not prominent; clamp connections absent, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with large spores'
SIMILAR  Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans similar in form and color and spore size, but has clamp connections and has acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the base, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria flavigelatinosa var. carnisalmonea.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Petersen(12), Phillips(1)*, Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)
