FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria flavobrunnescens  (G.F. Atk.) Corner  var. aromatica  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 85. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria flavobrunnescens var. aromatica include medium size, single stem, clear yellow branches, becoming brownish yellow when old, the tips a deeper yellow when young, branches appearing stiff and somewhat tufted at the nodes, fleshy-fibrous cartilaginous consistency when fresh, usually a pronounced sweetish odor, spores with small obscure warts, and clamp connections.^ Ramaria flavobrunnescens var. aromatica differs from R. flavobrunnescens var. flavobrunnescens (infrequent in eastern North America) in having larger spores, fragrant odor, and subcartilaginous consistency. The type variety, incidentally, has branch color suffused with fleshy tints in juvenile and adolescent specimens, (Petersen(12)). Var. aromatica is common in the Pacific Northwest (Scates-Barnhart). RANGE Var. aromatica has been found at least in WA (Marr). There is a collection from BC at the Pacific Forestry Centre, and there are collections from WA, OR, and CA (variety unspecified) at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid^ (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  3-18cm wide, 4.5-18cm high, frequently fan-shaped in outline^, stem usually single, branching up to 6 times from the stem, branches straight, diverging slightly from polychotomous to dichotomous nodes, axils acute to subacute, internodes lengthening up to 3.5cm, lower branches up to 1.5cm wide, upper branches usually thick, 0.2-0.8cm wide, polydigitate to polynodulose near tips, tips subacute to blunt, (Marr), upper branches +/- thick, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous, cartilaginous when fresh; white^, (Marr), "seems always to show a yellow-brown diffuse patch of flesh at the stipe base", (Petersen(3))
BRANCH COLOR  'light yellow' with deeper yellow tips when young, but when old, tips colored as branches and all areas becoming brownish yellow^, (Marr), "tips white when very young, then tips and branches light to bright yellow during growing stages, aging brownish yellow", (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  0.5-4.5(7)cm x 0.5-4(8)cm, usually single, cylindric to broadly conic, short abortive or primordial branches at base; buried part of fresh fruiting body white to 'pale yellow'^, (Marr), 1-6cm x 1-7cm, tapering or equal, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  sweet, slightly resembling gardenia, curry, or sweet pastries^, (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  yes (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), well represented in the fall flora of western Washington and may occur in the spring in less abundance^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'apricot yellow'
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-12 x 3-5 microns, average 10.4 x 4.0 microns, subcylindric, with small obscure warts^; basidia 1-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 40-85 x 7-10 microns, clavate, clamped; clamp connections present, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  'flavobrunnescens' means 'yellow becoming brown'; 'aromatica' means 'aromatic, fragrant, spicy'
SIMILAR  Ramaria coulterae usually shows a brown patch in the base of the stem, but it fruits in spring and hyphae do not have clamp connections, (Petersen(3)). Ramaria rasilispora has spores that are entirely smooth to obscurely (finely) warted, (Marr). Ramaria cystidiophora has acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the stem, (Marr). Ramaria flavigelatinosa lacks clamp connections, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria cystidiophora var. citronella, Ramaria flavigelatinosa var. fragrans, Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora, and Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in single quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Petersen(12), Petersen(3) (in the discussion of Ramaria coulterae), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria formosa  (Pers. per Fr.) Quel.^  Fl. Myc. 466. 1888;  Clavaria formosa  Pers. per Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  yellow-tipped coral
NOTES  Diagnostic features of Ramaria formosa include 1) salmon branches and yellow tips, 2) stout fasciculate brunnescent stems, 3) soft, friable dried flesh, 4) spore size, and 5) clamp connections, (Petersen).^ Another set of diagnostic features are 1) large fruitbody with pinkish branches and yellowish tips, 2) any part discoloring brown with handling, 3) either stem or primary branches thick - 2-4cm wide, 4) dried specimens very chalky-friable, 5) basidia and sterigmata filled with cyanophilic granular protoplasm, 6) prominently ornamented apiculate spores averaging about 10.4 x 5.4 microns, 7) clamped hyphae, and 8) positive macrochemical reactions with alpha-napthol, guaiac, guaiacol, phenol, and aniline, (Marr). RANGE Ramaria formosa is found from coast to coast in temperate climates of North America, including NS, NC, TN, and is common in Europe, (Petersen). It is uncommon in western WA, and collections were examined from WA, OR, NC, (Marr). Desjardin(6) illustrate it from CA. There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  branch sections green in ferric sulphate in water (Petersen(12)), stem flesh inamyloid, ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 9cm wide and up to 15cm high, broadly obovate in outline, usually fasciculate in groups of 2-5, major branches 2-4^, ascending but not erect, more or less round in cross-section, branches in 4-6 ranks, round in cross-section, axils rounded, internode ratio diminishing gradually when mature, tips cusped when young, elongating to minutely digitate by maturity, but remaining crowded, (Petersen(12)), medium or large size, 7-14cm wide, 7-20cm high, generally broad, (Marr)
FLESH  in branches, solid, pastel rosy orange under spore-bearing surface; in stem, solid, soft, moist but not slippery, white^, (Petersen(12)), fleshy-fibrous, somewhat spongy at base when fresh, drying brittle and very chalky-friable; colored as surface or even more intensely, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches dull salmon tan, branches "salmon color" when fresh, slowly duller ("apricot buff") with spore deposit; tips dull creamy ocher when fresh, fading to concolorous with branches when old^, (Petersen(12)), 'peach or light red', tips distinctly 'light yellow' when young, subconcolorous with [nearly colored as] branches when old, any part brunnescent with handling, turning about 'grayish orange' to 'cinnamon brown', (Marr)
STEM  up to 5cm long and 4cm wide, large, rounded to bluntly tapered, involving little substrate with no abortive branchlets; white where protected, weakly to moderately watery brunnescent on bruising or handling; smooth^, (Petersen(12)), single, massive, e.g. 3cm long and 5cm wide, subfasciculate or fasciculate [nearly bundled or bundled]; underground part white or brownish white; covered by a thin white tomentum; often with small whitish abortive or primordial branches, (Marr)
ODOR  indistinct, earthy^, (Petersen(12)), not distinctive (Marr)
TASTE  faintly fabaceous [bean-like], (Petersen(12)), not distinctive (Marr)
EDIBILITY  poisonous (McKnight(1))
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir) or (?) Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October to December^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10.7-15.5 x 5.6-6.7 microns, average 12.2 x 5.92 microns, elliptic to narrowly ovate, roughened in profile; ornamentation of complex meandering ridges and a few isolated warts, contents homogeneous or with 1 or 2 dark inclusions, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus prominent, gradual^; basidia 4-spored, 60-75 x 8-12 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous to minutely granular, strongly cyanophilic, sterigmata stout; "Clamp connections are especially difficult to observe on tramal hyphae in this species. It is essential, therefore, to examine young basidia, for in all other regards, R. formosa and R. neoformosa are nearly indistinguishable." (Petersen(12): in quotation the Latin names are in italics), spores 9-12 x 4.5-6 microns, average 10.4 x 5.4 microns, elliptic, ornamented with large, irregularly lobed, strongly cyanophilic warts, apiculus usually prominent up to 1 x 1.5 microns; basidia 1-4-spored, 40-82 x 8-13 microns, clavate, basally clamped, contents granular, cyanophilic, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "handsome"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Ramaria celerivirescens has similar colors, but does not discolor with handling and lacks clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria neoformosa R.H. Petersen differs mainly in lacking clamp connections, (Petersen(12)). Ramaria leptoformosa is similar in form and color but slimmer, with smaller average spore width (4.1 microns), and lacking cyanogranular protoplasm, (Marr). Ramaria rubricarnata has amyloid-reacting stem flesh, a single stout stem, and basidia without the cyanophilic granular protoplasm typical of R. formosa, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria largentii and Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata.
SOURCES  Petersen(7), Petersen(12), Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Exeter(3)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria fumosiavellanea  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 91. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria fumosiavellanea include 1) medium size, 2) branches that are brownish violet-gray with violaceous-tinted tips, 3) stem that is small, off-white, and either single or two joined, 4) red reaction of violet pigmented surfaces to 20% KOH, 5) spores that are elongate-elliptic either with fine linearly lobed cyanophilic warts or nearly smooth, and 6) absent clamp connections.^ Ramaria fumosiavellanea is rare, and may be conspecific with R. spinulosa: Petersen(3) (1988) says "Our examination of the type specimens of R. fumosiavellanea Marr and Stuntz (SUCO: Marr no. 730) shows it to be conspecific with R. spinulosa." (presumably var. spinulosa), but Petersen(2), published the same year (earlier in the year according to Exeter(3)), says "There is a small complex of taxa with brown, tan-brown, or violaceous brown branches and apices, clampless hyphae, and wide spores. In it are R. spinulosa (Pers.: Fr.) Quel. (brown flesh, spore Lm = 10.5 microns, Europe and eastern North America), R. spinulosa var. diminutiva (brown flesh, spores Lm = 8.5 microns, California), and R. fumosiavellanea Marr & D.E. Stuntz (off-white flesh, spore Lm = 10.3 microns, Pacific Northwest). The stipe surface in R. spinulosa is also brown or orange-brown, while that of R. fumosiavellanea is off-white." The description below is derived from Marr(1) except where noted. RANGE The type was found in WA. 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid; violet pigmented surface areas of either fresh or dried specimens quickly turning red ('Venetian red') on direct application of 20% KOH, (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  11.5cm wide, 11cm high, medium size, fan-shaped in outline, stem single or two connate [joined], branching up to 6 times from stem^, branches somewhat flattened and longitudinally sulcate [grooved] immediately below the polychotomous nodes, "axils acute or subacute, branches divaricate at least in the lower nodes", lowest internodes elongate and about 2cm wide, upper branches usually less than 1cm wide, surface rugose [wrinkled], polydigitate near tips, tips multinodulose or rounded
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh, drying brittle and chalky-friable; in branches translucent brownish white, in base white
BRANCH COLOR  'violet-gray' also with a brownish component, tips and a short distance downward paler than 'grayish orange' with a violaceous tint
STEM  either single, or a fascicle [bundle] or two connate [joined] stems, tapered, small, 1cm x 1.5cm; about 'grayish orange'
ODOR  faintly sweet
TASTE  not distinctive
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  in sandy soil
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-13.5 x 4.5-7 microns, average 10.4 x 5.4 microns, elongate-elliptic, ornamented with fine, linearly lobed, cyanophilic warts, some nearly smooth, distinct apiculus, mostly 1 x 1 micron^; basidia 4-spored, 75-90 x 8.5-10 microns, clavate, without clamp connections, sterigmata 6-9 microns long, straight, mostly erect; hymenium and subhymenium combined 115-125 microns thick, subhymenial hyphae constituting an interwoven layer 35-40 microns wide, cells 3-4 microns wide, thin-walled, without clamp connections; context hyphae subparallel to interwoven in stem, parallel in branches, cells frequently inflating, 12-19 microns wide, wall smooth or commonly fluted, cyanophilic, thin-walled or slightly thickened, 0.25-1.5 microns thick, cellular inclusions often cyanophilic globules, crystalloid masses embedded in the stem context, ampulliform inflations near septa common, 12-17 microns wide, walls of the swellings distinctively ornamented in the stem, moderately or slightly ornamented in the branches, clamp connections absent; gloeoplerous hyphae not observed
NAME ORIGIN  means 'smoky avellaneous' in color
SIMILAR  Ramaria spinulosa var. diminutiva (Mendocino Co. in California) also has brown to violaceous brown branches and tips, wide spores, and absent clamp connections, but stem flesh and surface is brown, and average spore length is 8.5 microns, (Petersen(2)).
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Petersen(2), Petersen(3), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. gelatiniaurantia^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 93. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  orange jelly-belly
NOTES  Features of Ramaria gelatiniaurantia var. gelatiniaurantia include 1) medium to large size, 2) a base consisting of many fused stems, the underground part of the base white, with a band of yellow just above ground level, 3) bright orange branches and tips, 4) gelatinous flesh especially in the base, 5) bean-like odor, 6) spores ornamented with small lobed cyanophilic warts, and 7) absent clamp connections.^ Marr(1) says R. gelatiniaurantia is non-bruising or bruising dull violet, and gives a violet bruising reaction for var. violeitingens, but does not specify for var. gelatiniaurantia. The place of this taxon has been called into question by recent investigators: some collections identified as R. gelatinaurantia var. gelatinaurantia may fit varieties of R. flavigelatinosa, (M. Beug, pers. comm.). RANGE The type was found in WA, (Marr). Some variety (varieties) of R. gelatiniaurantia have been found also in OR and CA, (Castellano). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh of stem inamyloid, no significant change within 30 minutes in response to guaiac, (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  4-11cm wide, 6-22cm high, medium to large size, fasciculate [bundled]^, base compound, "branching up to 9 times from the base, mostly dichotomous, axils acute to turbinate and branches scarcely divergent", internodes elongating up to 4cm long, lower branches sometimes laterally fused, up to 2cm wide, upper branches slender, commonly 0.1-0.4cm wide, forked to finely divided near tips, tips mostly acute, (Marr)
FLESH  very gelatinous, especially in base; yellow in branch tips, base with marbled clear and whitish streaks^, (Scates-Barnhart), definitely gelatinous and especially in base when fresh; flesh of stem "marbled, translucent grayish white alternating with wavy opaque white areas", 'sunflower yellow' at least in the ultimate branches, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  tips and most of upper branches deep orange; fruitbody not bruising^, (Scates-Barnhart), most of exposed branches and especially tips 'deep orange', (Marr)
STEM  base consisting of many fused gelatinous stems; just above ground level a band of light to bright yellow, underground part of base white^, (Scates-Barnhart), base "compound, consisting of numerous, connate, gelatinous primary axes in various stages of development"; underground part of fruitbody white, immediately above ground 'light yellow' to 'sunflower yellow', (Marr)
ODOR  fabaceous [bean-like]^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  poisonous (McKnight(1))
HABITAT  type terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 3.5-5 microns, average 9.3 x 4.1 microns, subcylindric, ornamented with small lobed cyanophilic warts^, some false clamp connections present; basidia 4-spored, 70-82 x 8-11 microns, clavate, sterigmata 4-5 microns long, straight, not divergent; hymenium and subhymenium combined 75-110 microns thick; subhymenial hyphae 2-3 microns wide, interwoven, thin-walled; context hyphae interwoven in stem, parallel in the branches, hyphae mostly non-inflated, 3-5 microns wide in stem and 3-12 microns wide in the branches, walls smooth, surrounded by gelatinous matrix, scarcely cyanophilic, with thin walls, cyanophilic globular inclusions common, hyphae frequently vesicular near septa, 9-17 microns wide, walls of the swellings moderately thick up to 2 microns, distinctly ornamented in the stem, less ornamented in the branches; gloeoplerous hyphae rare, mostly slender, 2-3.5 microns wide; clamp connections absent, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'gelatinous - orange'
SIMILAR  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia var. violeitingens differs in having yellow branch tips, gloeoplerous hyphae prominent in base, and reacting to guaiac within 30 minutes of application, (Marr). Ramaria sandaracina has clamp connections, (Castellano). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria sandaracina var. chondrobasis.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1)*, Ammirati(1)*, McKnight(1), Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. violeitingens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 94. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia var. violeitingens differs from R. gelatiniaurantia var. gelatiniaurantia in having yellow branch tips, gloeoplerous hyphae prominent in base, and reacting to guaiac within 30 minutes of application, (Marr).^ RANGE Ramaria gelatiniaurantia var. violeitingens has been found at least in WA, (Marr).

FRUITING BODY  medium size, 5-15cm wide, 12-17cm high, base compound or single^; branching up to 7 times from the base, occasionally polychotomous in lower nodes but mostly dichotomous in upper part, axils subacute to U-shaped, and branches slightly divaricate, internodes elongated up to 3cm or even longer, branches generally slender within the range of 0.1-0.5cm wide, forked to multifid near tips, tips mostly subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  subgelatinous and brittle when fresh; white in the base, 'yellow' in the branches^, (Marr), subgelatinous and brittle; yellow in the branches, white in the base, gelatinized content of base and branches about caramel brown, (Scates-Barnhart)

TASTE  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria gelatinosa  (Coker) Corner  var. oregonensis  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 95. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  gelatinous coral mushroom, gelatinous coral, Oregon gelatinous coral
NOTES  Ramaria gelatinosa var. oregonensis is the most gelatinous Ramaria reported from the Pacific Northwest. The base flesh shows translucent often gelatinous pockets or streaks when the base is cut lengthwise. Numerous branch systems emerge from a wrinkled compound basal mass that is colored white, light yellow, or light orange or combinations of these. The branches are mostly erect and nearly parallel, colored orange to orange-brown sometimes aging grayish violet. The tips are the same color as the branches or paler. Microscopic characters include spores coarsely ornamented with lobed cyanophilic warts, collapsing basidia with cyanophilic granules, and gelatinized hyphae in the stem (with crystalloid clusters) and to a lesser extent in the branches.^ RANGE It is found in WA and OR, (Marr). It is common in the fall in the Pacific Northwest and northern CA, (Arora). There are collections also for ID and AK at the University of Washington and there are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  usually of medium size, broad, 5-12cm wide, 8-15cm high, base compound, characteristically numerous branch systems arising at approximately the same level from the basal mass^, "maturing centrifugally, branching up to 7 times from the base, nodes commonly but not always dichotomous, axils subacute to U-shaped, branches nearly parallel, sometimes slightly flattened", internodes decreasing in length upwards, maximum length about 4cm, mostly 1cm or less wide, bifid or finely divided near tips, tips narrowly rounded, (Marr), 5-15cm wide, 5-15cm high, profusely branched from a fleshy base, branches mostly erect and smooth, (Arora)
FLESH  stiffly gelatinous; translucent, pale dull orange^, (Scates-Barnhart), gelatinous, the base resembling stiff agar when fresh, (drying hard and brittle); translucent, a shade lighter than 'grayish orange', (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light orange, but becoming darker or duller when old and sometimes developing a dingy violet cast, tips the same color as branches or paler^, (Scates-Barnhart), orange, orange-brown, aging grayish violet: at first 'light orange' developing darker shades when old such as 'grayish orange', 'Pompeian yellow', or 'agate brown', sometimes with a definite 'violet gray' cast, tips nearly the same color as branches or distinctly paler, (Marr), "orange to pinkish-orange to orange-buff, pinkish-brown, or occasionally yellow-orange", sometimes developing a faint purple-gray tinge when old, (Arora)
STEM  4-7cm x 3-8cm, compound, broad, "consisting of a wrinkled, gelatinous mass of fused axes, in longitudinal section the component parts delimited by convoluted hymenial surfaces, base surface covered with a thin white tomentum"; base of fresh fruitbody with areas of white, 'light yellow', and 'light orange', any one of these colors may predominate^, (Marr), lacking yellow bellyband, base variably white, light yellow or light orange, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  musty sweet^ (Marr), unpleasant (Miller)
TASTE  not distinctive (Marr), often bitter (Arora)^, mild (Scates-Barnhart, Miller)
EDIBILITY  poisonous to some people (causing diarrhea and digestive upset), (Arora)
HABITAT  widely scattered to gregarious in duff under conifers - Tsuga (hemlock), Abies (fir) etc.^, (Arora), terrestrial, under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), September and October, (Marr), on ground in old coniferous forests, often under Tsuga heterophylla, late summer and fall, (Scates-Barnhart), September to November (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr), golden yellow (Phillips), light buff to orange-buff (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 4.5-6 microns, average 8.9 x 5.1 microns, broadly ovoid to broadly cylindric, coarsely ornamented with lobed, cyanophilic warts, with prominent lateral apiculus up to 2 x 2 microns^; basidia mostly 4-spored, sometimes 2-spored, 43-65 x 5.5-11 microns, clavate, collapsing soon after spore release, cyanophilic granules especially abundant in collapsed basidia, basally clamped, sterigmata 5-7 microns; hymenium and subhymenium combined 80-100 microns thick; subhymenial hyphae 2-4 microns wide, interwoven, thin-walled; contextual hyphae of the stem "almost completely gelatinized, very little structure distinguishable, crystalloid clusters prominent in the gelatinous mass, hyphae of the branches less gelatinized, structural details best defined nearest the branch surface, parallel to subparallel", cells non-inflated or slightly so, 4-11 microns wide, walls smooth, often poorly delimited, those not highly gelatinized mostly thin-walled, faintly cyanophilic, cells of the branches frequently vesicular near septa, 9-16 microns wide, walls of the swellings smooth to moderately ornamented; clamp connections present, sometimes of the keyhole type; gloeoplerous hyphae rare, mostly 3-3.5 microns wide, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  'oregonensis' means 'from Oregon'
SIMILAR  Other distinctly gelatinous species in the Pacific Northwest are Ramaria flavigelatinosa, Ramaria gelatiniaurantia, Ramaria sandaracina var. chondrobasis, and Ramaria cystidiophora var. fabiolens: the first two lack clamp connections, and the last two have more finely ornamented, narrower spores, (Marr). Ramaria gelatinosa var. gelatinosa (eastern North America including North Carolina and Nova Scotia) is characterized by pallid salmon branches with yellow tips, wide, obviously roughened spores, and agglutinated, clamped tramal hyphae, (Petersen(12)). R. gelatinosa var. gelatinosa differs in color, according to Coker having creamy white branches when young that mature buff-pink and age to fleshy brown: similar features include spores morphology, hyphae with the same types of clamp connections, crystalloid deposits in the context, and basidia with cyanophilic protoplasm, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria testaceoflava.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in single quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Ammirati(1)*, Miller(14)*, Arora(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Petersen(12) (discussing Ramaria gelatinosa var. gelatinosa), Exeter(3)*
61
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria gracilis  (Pers. per Fr.) Quel.^  Fl. Myc. p.463. 1888;  Clavaria gracilis  Pers.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria gracilis include 1) delicate whitish branches that are pliable when fresh and have sharp tips, a whitish stem with copious rhizomorphs, musty to aromatic odor, ocher spore deposit, small spores with low scattered cyanophilic warts, and conspicuous skeletal hyphae with cyanophilic walls in the trama.^ RANGE "Appalachian Mountain chain (common at all elevations), across northern US and Canada, Rocky Mountains and Pacific coastal areas (rare), central and Northern Europe, and reported from USSR", collections examined from WA, OR, ID, also NS, ON, PQ, CA, CT, GA, MI, MN, NC, NJ, NY, SC, TN, VT, VA, WI, Denmark, France, Italy, Netherlands, Norway, Poland, Slovakia, Sweden, Switzerland, United Kingdom, (Petersen(11)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  spore-bearing surface deep slate green in ferric sulphate in water, spore-bearing surface unchanging in KOH, (Petersen), stem flesh inamyloid (Marr)
FRUITING BODY  "up to 8cm high, repeatedly branched, generally broadly fusiform in outline"^, major branches "several, slender, erect", secondary branches "slender, erect, usually lobed in cross-section", axils "very acute to narrowly rounded", "internodes diminishing in length gradually", tips very acute to acerose, from subcristate and flattened to prolonged and erect, (Petersen), very small, 2.5 x 2cm, single slender stem, branching about 5 times from the stem, "dichotomous to polychotomous, axils mostly acute and branches slightly divergent", lower internodes elongated to approximately 0.6cm, "branches with a slight thickish appearance", although with maximum width about 0.2cm, sometimes flattened at nodes and terminal nodes slightly flabellate [fan-shaped], bifid to cristate near tips, tips acute, (Marr)
FLESH  coriaceous [leathery] when fresh; white^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  off-white when young ("pale ochraceous salmon", "seashell pink") becoming pale ocher to pale ochraceous cinnamon later ("cartridge buff", "pinkish buff", "cream buff", "pale pinkish cinnamon"); tips white but sometimes darkening when old ("pale ochraceous buff")^, (Petersen), lower three fourths of fresh fruitbody 'pale orange', ultimate branches milk white, (Marr), tips milk white, branches white to cream, both darkening when old, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  up to 3cm long, up to 0.8cm wide, "erect, usually single but often connate with 1-2 other individuals", "soft and pliable at all ages, often buried in substrate and then usually lax"; "white to cream color when young and protected", becoming somewhat darker when old, and then colored as lower branches ("warm buff"); suede-like in texture; "basal rhizomorphic strands slender, delicate, well-defined, often anastomosing" branching up to several centimeters from fruitbody, "white, often becoming diffuse as small mycelial tangles or sheets"^, (Petersen), 0.3cm long and 0.2cm wide, single, slender, with a distinct felty white basal tomentum and rhizomorphic strands, (Marr), "base off-white when young, pale ocher, pale orange, to pale ochraceous cinnamon later", (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  distinct, musty to aromatic, but not definitely of anise, when dry faintly of fenugreek^, (Petersen)
TASTE  "very musty; agaricoid, strong"^, (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  not edible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  on conifer duff^ (usually pine), (Petersen), "growing from rotten wood on the ground (?), collected near Western Red Cedar and Douglas Fir", (Marr), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the surface of the ground, associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October and November (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  near "yellow ocher"^, (Petersen)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (5.2)5.5-7.1 x 3.3-4.4 microns, average 6.02 x 3.65 microns, broadly ovoid to broadly elliptic, somewhat flattened adaxially, roughened in profile, ornamentation of low, scattered, strongly cyanophilic warts and meandering ridges in a generally adaxial direction, contents homogeneous and without droplets or with one obscure droplet, the droplet appearing brownish under phase contrast, spore wall up to 0.5 microns thick, apiculus "eccentric, obconic, obscure, prominent"^; basidia 4-spored, 30-38 x 5.2-6 microns, narrowly clavate, clamped, colorless, sterigmata very delicate, up to 5.2 microns long; hyphae of rhizomorphic strands of 3 types: 1) generative, 2.2-4 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, conspicuously clamped, inflated areas not restricted to clamp connections, up to 14 microns wide, thin-walled to thick-walled (wall up to 1 micron thick), "delicately to coarsely ornamented over wide areas", 2) skeletalized generative, 3-4.5 microns wide, "thick-walled (wall often obscuring cell lumen), arising and ending in clamp connections or rarely ending blindly and then bluntly, highly refringent under phase contrast", and 3) skeletal, 1.5-2.2 microns wide, "thick-walled (wall up to 0.9 microns thick), arising from clamp connections, tapering gradually to an acute tip, ending blindly, more or less straight, flexible but not easily collapsed"; hyphae of branch trama of 3 types: 1) generative, as above, inflated clamp connections occasional to rare, 2) skeletalized as above, abundant to rare, and 3) skeletals as above, often thick (up to 8.5 microns wide) near origin, tapering gradually and ending blindly, (Petersen), spores 5-6.5 x 3.5-4 microns, average 5.3 x 3.5 microns, elliptic to oval, delicately ornamented with shallow lobed cyanophilic warts in a subspiral arrangement, with a prominent apiculus, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'slender'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rainierensis is stouter and taller, usually produces significantly larger spores, and has generative and skeletalized generative hyphae but not skeletal hyphae in the rhizomorphic strands, (Petersen). R. rainierensis has a bitter taste, and a faint odor of anise when mature, (Scates-Barnhart). Ramaria suecica has larger spores and has neither skeletalized generative hyphae nor skeletal hyphae in the rhizomorphic strands, (Petersen). Ramariopsis 'kunzei' and Clavulina coralloides can be somewhat similar, (Petersen). Lentaria pinicola has smooth spores and monomitic rhizomorphs.
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Marr(1), Castellano(1)*, Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*, Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria hilaris  R.H. Petersen & M. Zang  var. olympiana  R.H. Petersen^  Mycologia 80(2): 228. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria hilaris var. olympiana include small to medium size, color bright yellow in lower parts of branches and in tips, pallid salmon in the upper branches, gelatinous flesh, single stem off-white at base, faintly bean-like odor, spores with scattered, small, flat, occasionally lobed warts, and absent clamp connections.^ The description is derived from Petersen(2) except where stated. RANGE The type was found in WA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  IKI reaction negative; ferric chloride in water positive reaction, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), stem flesh non-amyloid (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 6cm wide and up to 10cm high, broadly fusiform to broadly obconic [conic narrowing downward] in outline^; branches ascending, erect to somewhat divergent, more or less round in cross-section; internodes diminishing gradually upward, axils rounded to minutely turbinate, tips minutely digitate when young, usually dichotomous, elongating somewhat by maturity
FLESH  in branches firm-gelatinous, brittle; progressively yellower upward, with no pinkish tints (salmon restricted to outermost tissues); in stem firm-gelatinous; translucent, more or less colorless, white outward
BRANCH COLOR  bright yellow in lower part, upward a lively pallid salmon; tips bright rich yellow, hardly fading in maturity
STEM  up to 1.7cm long and up to 1.3cm wide, single, tapering to a point; off-white at base, upward bright rich yellow; surface slippery although not moist; smooth; texture rubbery; without abortive branchlets, (Petersen), base white with a yellow bellyband, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  faintly bean-like
TASTE  negligible
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  not given but type fruiting in fall, (Petersen), on ground, associated with Abies (true fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Scates-Barnhart)^, fruits in October (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9.4-11.2 x 4.0-5.0 microns, average 10.1 x 4.4 microns, elliptic, usually flattened adaxially, obscurely roughened in profile, ornamentation of scattered, small, flat, occasionally lobed warts, contents dense, yellowish, usually with 1-2 more refringent areas but not with well defined inclusions, wall up to 0.2 microns thick^; basidia 4-spored, 57-68 x 8-9 microns, clavate, clampless, contents with scattered minute granules and small guttules; clamp connections absent, but hyphae of stem and branch trama have ampulliform inflations usually at septa
NAME ORIGIN  'hilaris' means 'cheerful, gay'; 'olympiana' probably refers to the Olympic Mountains in Washington
SIMILAR  Ramaria flavigelatinosa has gelatinous flesh but has a compound stem: var. carnisalmonea shows pinkish branches but has orange pigments in the trama that are not present in R. hilaris var. olympiana, (Petersen). Ramaria verlotensis "shows not only pink coloration with the yellow of the upper stipe and lower branches, but also a stout, cauliflower-like fruitbody habit", (Petersen). All other similar North American taxa with gelatinous flesh produce clamped basidia, (Petersen). Ramaria hilaris var. hilaris, found in China, has smaller spores (7.9-10.8 x 4.3-5.0 microns, with average length 9.7 microns), (Petersen). Ramaria gelatiniaurantia is somewhat similar but differs in color.
SOURCES  Petersen(2), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria incognita  R.H. Petersen^  Bibliotheca Mycologica  79: 145. 1981
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria incognita was synonymized with Phaeoclavulina argentea in Giachini(3) in 2011, but this synonymy was not reflected in the online Species Fungorum, accessed April 2, 2018, or MycoBank, accessed the same day. Features of Ramaria incognita include 1) relatively robust fruitbody, 2) upper stem and branches probably deep ocher to honey brown when fresh, drying deep dull orange brown, 3) the stem base remaining snow white, 4) no green colors seen on fruitbody, and 5) microscopically extensive unilateral hymenium, and relatively long spores with ornamentation of scattered slender rounded spines occasionally very long (up to 2 microns) and occasional short crests.^ The description is derived from Petersen(14). This species was described from dried specimens that did not have field notes accompanying them: fresh size, stature, color, odor, and taste cannot be described. RANGE Collections were examined from WA, ID, AZ, CA, CO, and NM, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  up to 3cm wide and up to 5cm high (dried), "repeatedly and rather densely branched, subarbuscular"^, major branches few to many, 0.2-0.6cm wide, "lobed in cross-section, not strict, lax-erect", minor branches "polychotomous to dichotomous, not round in cross-section", axils rounded to teardrop-shaped, tips minute, digitate to awl-shaped
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  probably deep ocher to honey brown when fresh, drying deep dull orange brown, tips sometimes whitish to argenteous (seldom), usually colored as the branches
STEM  may be distinct and then up to 2cm long and 0.8cm wide (dried), narrowing downward somewhat, and lobed or compressed in cross-section, to virtually missing, with the major branches arising from a common base at or below substrate level, stem base snow white when fresh (remaining so in drying), brunnescent on handling (remaining so in drying), "smooth upward, minutely felty or mealy below substrate level and usually involving substantial pure white, slender, discrete, branched rhizomorphs up to 3cm long (and surely longer in the natural environment)"
ODOR  weak of fenugreek when dried
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  not recorded
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8.6 x 3.3-4.5 microns, average 7.45 x 3.82 microns, elliptic to elongate - comma-shaped, with adaxial and abaxial surfaces often more or less parallel, ornamentation of scattered slender rounded spines up to 2 microns long (occasionally) and occasional short crests, contents granular, wall up to 0.7 microns thick^; basidia 35-45 x 5.5-8 microns, clavate, contents granular, (2)-4 sterigmata that are straight and slender; tramal hyphae of upper branches 2.8-11 microns wide, discrete, clamped; "hymenial surface with large sterile areas, usually decurrent from axils, but often producing sparse basidia in scattered clumps downward on adaxial branch surfaces"
NAME ORIGIN  means 'unknown', probably refers to the unknown description of fresh state
SIMILAR  Phaeoclavulina abietina has a characteristic blue-green bruising reaction, the color visible when dried, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(14), Exeter(3), Giachini(3)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria largentii  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 98. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  golden coral, Largent's coral
NOTES  Features of Ramaria largentii include 1) a massive base single or somewhat bundled, 2) bright orange branches with tips more intensely colored  than branches or similar in color, 3) slightly sweet odor, 4) clamp connections, and 5) the most striking feature of the species: relatively large spores with conspicuous irregularly shaped cyanophilic warts in a somewhat spiral pattern.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA, (Marr). It has been found at least in WA, OR, and CA, (Castellano), Rocky Mountains westward (McKnight), and Europe (Courtecuisse).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  7-14cm wide, 12-15cm high, medium size, broad, base single or subfasciculate [somewhat bundled]^, branching up to 9 times from the base, mostly polychotomous in the lower nodes and dichotomous in upper nodes, axils subacute to U-shaped, branches subparallel to moderately divaricate, internodes of mature specimens elongated, the lower ones up to 4cm long, branches slender, generally less than 1cm wide, bifid to multifid near the tips, tips rounded, (Marr), branches thick near base, slender in upper part, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; in stem white, in branches colored the same as surface near the surface, lighter toward the center^, (Marr), base flesh cartilaginous when young, becoming softly stringy, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  bright orange: tips brilliant orange, branches the same or paler^, (Scates-Barnhart), 'light orange', colored the same or more intensely colored near tips, (Marr), moderate to light orange-yellow or pale orange-yellow, upper part sometimes lighter, fruitbody does not stain when wounded, (McKnight), quite bright orange, tips darker, (Courtecuisse)
STEM  base 4cm x 5cm, single or somewhat fasciculate, cylindric or broadly conic, with basal tomentum, "small abortive branches frequently diverging from the upper base", base of fresh fruitbody white to 'pale yellow'
ODOR  slightly sweet^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  not recommended, easy to confuse with known poisonous species, (McKnight), presumably edible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, collected under lowland hemlocks in October^, (Marr), scattered or in groups, under conifers (McKnight), fruits in humus or soil and matures above the surface, associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pinus monticola (Western White Pine), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  pale orange-yellow^ (McKnight), 'golden yellow', (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-15 x 3.5-5 microns, average 13.4 x 4.5 microns, subcylindric, ornamented with conspicuous, irregularly shaped, cyanophilic warts in subspirals, "grossly ornamented"^; basidia mostly 4-spored, clavate, clamped; gloeoplerous hyphae common, 2.5-4 microns wide, or up to 9 microns wide in localized bulbous regions; clamp connections present, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Ramaria distinctissima var. americana also has long spores, but colors are brighter, with branches pinkish flesh-colored to salmon, tips bright orange when young and fresh, and lower branches and upper stem yellow to golden yellow, (Petersen(2) who comments that R. largentii could conceivably represent faded or ill-colored specimens of R. distinctissima var. americana). Ramaria longispora is similar in spore size and color, but is slender with a compound base, and lacks clamp connections, (Marr). R. longispora is difficult to distinguish on field characters, but is more slender and has a compound stem, and spores are slightly shorter and less distinctly ornamented, (McKnight). Other bright orange Ramarias with clamp connections have different spores, (Marr). Ramaria formosa has more pinkish branches with yellow tips, (McKnight). Ramaria gelatiniaurantia has marbled flesh in stem: translucent grayish white, alternating with waxy opaque white areas, (McKnight). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria sandaracina var. chondrobasis.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Castellano(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Scates-Barnhart(1), Petersen(2) (discussing R. distinctissima var. americana), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria leptoformosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 100. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  slim formosa
NOTES  Features of Ramaria leptoformosa include 1) medium size 2) fairly slender fruitbody with slender branches, 3) stem single or more commonly compound and white tinged with yellow or orange, 4) branches light orange to light red with tips deep yellow to orange, 5) spores finely ornamented with linearly lobed cyanophilic warts, and 6) clamp connections.^ RANGE Ramaria leptoformosa has been found at least in WA (Marr). There are collections from OR at Oregon State University. There is a collection from BC  at the University of British Columbia (illustrated on Mushroom Observer) and determined by O Ceska.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  usually medium size, taller than broad, 2-10cm wide, 4.5-17cm high, base single or more commonly compound, branching up to 7 times from the base^, commonly polychotomous in the lower nodes and dichotomous in upper part, "axils acute to u-shaped and branches slightly divergent", mature internodes usually elongated, up to 4cm long, branches slender, seldom over 1cm wide, commonly 0.2-0.5cm wide, forked to finely divided near tips, tips subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; in branches colored as surface^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  tips deep yellow to orange, branches light orange to light red, sometimes yellow in lower part^, (Scates-Barnhart), 'light orange or light red', tips more yellowish 'sunflower or dark yellow' or 'chrome orange', (Marr)
STEM  base 0.5-5cm x 1-4cm, single or more commonly compound, the compound base consisting of 2-10 axes in various stages of connation [being joined], each component about 0.8-2cm wide, sometimes slightly bulbous, up to 9cm in length, "small basal abortive or primordial branch systems sometimes present"; underground part of fresh fruitbody 'yellowish or orange white', bruising or staining reactions slight or entirely absent^, (Marr), base white, tinged with yellow or orange, with or without a yellow "bellyband", (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), Taxus brevifolia (Western Yew), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October and November^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-13 x 3-5 microns, average 10.6 x 4.1 microns, subcylindric with a suprahilar depression and slight dorsal convexity, finely ornamented with linearly lobed, cyanophilic warts^; basidia 1-4-spored (mostly 4-spored), 41-80 x 8-12 microns, clavate, clamped; hymenium about 70-80 microns thick, subhymenium 25 microns thick; subhymenial hyphae 2-4 microns wide, loosely interwoven, thin-walled, with clamp connections; context hyphae 2-14 microns, non-inflated or slightly inflated, moderately cyanophilous, thin-walled, walls smooth or fluted, ampulliform inflations near septa infrequent, 9-15 microns wide, walls of the swellings nearly smooth in the branches, moderately ornamented in the stem, clamp connections present, closed or open, the clamp cell sometimes enlarged up to 9 microns, gleoplerous hyphae present, 3-3.5 microns wide except in localized bulbous regions, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'thin [Ramaria] formosa'
SIMILAR  Ramaria formosa has a bruising reaction and less slender habit, average spore width is 5.4 microns, and it typically has cyanogranular protoplasm, (Marr). Ramaria amyloidea, Ramaria maculatipes, and Ramaria rubricarnata might possibly be mistaken for this species but are amyloid positive, (Marr). Ramaria sandaracina var. euosma might possibly be mistaken for this species, but has smaller spores and a subgelatinous consistency (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria longispora  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 101. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  formosetta
NOTES  Features of Ramaria longispora include 1) stem that is single or more commonly with two to six primary axes arising from a root-like structure, with white rhizomorphs, 2) underground part white, 3) yellow "bellyband" where branches begin, 4) branches light to deep orange, generally slender and sometimes hollow, 5) tips yellow when young, 6) spores averaging 13.5 microns long, with numerous distinct cyanophilic warts, and 7) absent clamp connections.^ RANGE Ramaria longispora has been found at least in WA (Marr). There are collections from OR and CA at Oregon State University and from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid, (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  2-9cm wide, 4-18cm high, small to medium size, slender, stem single or more commonly somewhat compound^, "consisting of 2-6 primary axes arising from a primary root-like structure; branching 3-6 times above the primary axes, mostly dichotomous, internodal elongation acropetal from the base, distinctly elongated at maturity", branches generally slender, 1-1.5cm wide in lower ones to mostly 0.2-0.5cm in upper part, sometimes hollow, axils mostly subacute and branches slightly divergent, bifid or finely divided near tips, tips subacute, (Marr) 
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; in branches colored as surface or paler in center^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light to deep orange, tips bright deep yellow when young, when old fading to match branches^, (Scates-Barnhart), 'light to deep orange', immature tips about 'saffron or chrome yellow' becoming colored as branches during maturation, (Marr)
STEM  3cm long and 1.5cm wide, single, slightly bulbous, or more commonly somewhat compound, consisting of 2-6 primary axes arising from a primary root-like structure; underground part of fresh fruiting body white, 'pale yellow to sunflower yellow' near emergence from substrate; arising from conspicuous white rhizomorphic strands^, (Marr), single and +/- bulbous, or semidivided with several branches arising from a basal root-like structure, a band of yellow where branches begin, conspicuous white "rootlets" sometimes present, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Phillips)
HABITAT  terrestrial, under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October^, (Marr), September-October, (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'apricot yellow'^, (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-18 x 4-6 microns, average 13.5 x 4.5 microns, subcylindric, sometimes with a slight suprahilar extension and dorsal convexity, ornamented with numerous distinct cyanophilic warts^; basidia clavate, 50-82 x 9-13 microns, without basal clamp connections, mostly 4-spored, sterigmata 4-7 microns long; hymenium and subhymenium combined 60-70 microns thick; subhymenial hyphae 3-4 microns wide, interwoven, thin-walled, without clamp connections; context hyphae subparallel near surface to distinctly interwoven elsewhere in the stem, parallel in the branches, hyphae slightly to highly inflated, 4-25 microns wide, walls smooth, thin, ampulliform inflations near septa, 10-13 microns wide, walls of the swellings moderately ornamented in the stem, smooth to slightly ornamented in the branches; gloeoplerous hyphae present, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with long spores'
SIMILAR  Ramaria largentii and Ramaria sandaracina are similar in color but hyphae have clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria gelatiniaurantia is similar in color but has gelatinous flesh and shorter spores, (Marr). Ramaria aurantiisiccescens is similar in color but has shorter spores with fine warts, (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Phillips(1)*, Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*
67
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria maculatipes  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 103. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  formosa stainer
NOTES  Features of Ramaria maculatipes include 1) peach branches with light yellow tips, 2) reddish brown stains or bruises on the single base and lower branches, 3) amyloid stem flesh, 4) nearly cylindric warts with fine cyanophilic warts with somewhat spiral pattern, and 5) clamp connections.^ RANGE The type is from WA (Marr). It has also been found in two CA locations, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh amyloid, the reaction requiring up to 45 minutes, (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  6cm wide, 10cm high, medium size^, single stem, branching up to 7 times from stem, "nodes frequently polychotomous, axils acute or turbinate and branches moderately divergent", internodes moderately elongated, the maximum length about 3cm occurring in the lower internodes, lower branches up to 2.5cm wide, upper branches mostly 0.2-0.6cm wide, polydigitate or polynodulose near tips, tips rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; colored as surface^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  branches light orangy-yellow, tips light yellow, lower branches staining dark reddish brown^, (Scates-Barnhart), branches 'peach', tips 'light yellow', lower branches staining or bruising 'oxblood red', (Marr)
STEM  base orangish white, staining dark reddish brown^, (Scates-Barnhart), 2-4cm x 1.5-2cm, single, tapering, underground part of fruitbody 'orange white', staining or bruising 'oxblood red', (Marr)
ODOR  slight, not distinctive^ (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  type collected under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), November^, (Marr), associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla, (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'grayish orange'^ (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-11 x 4-5 microns, average 10.2 x 4.3 microns, subcylindric with a slight suprahilar depression and dorsal convexity, ornamented with fine cyanophilic warts in subspirals^; basidia 4-spored, 57-80 x 8-9 microns, clavate, basally clamped, sterigmata 2-5 microns long, straight or slightly incurved, slightly divergent; hymenium about 80 microns thick and subhymenium 35 microns thick; subhymenial hyphae interwoven, 2-4 microns wide, clamp connections abundant, frequently of the keyhole type, thin-walled; "Contextual hyphae--a collapsing basal tomentum present, cells 2-5 microns diam, context of the stipe very compactly interwoven, parallel in the branches, cells non-inflated to slightly inflated, 4-13 microns diam, walls of the branches slightly fluted, thin-walled in the branches 0.25-1 micron, slightly thicker in the stipe, up to 1.5 microns, cyanophilous, vesiculation of cells near septa rare, if present; clamps abundant, either of the closed or open type"; "gleoplerous hyphae abundant in the stipe, rare in the branches, forming bulbous regions 8-20 microns diam, except for these localized regions the diam 4-6 microns", (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'spotty stem'
SIMILAR  Ramaria leptoformosa might possibly be mistaken for this species, but stem flesh not amyloid, (Marr). Ramaria rubribrunnescens has about the same color and staining reaction, but it has clampless hyphae, inamyloid stem flesh, and larger spores, (Marr). R. rubribrunnescens is also said to have an odor that is sweet when old, like anise, (Scates-Barnhart). R. rubribrunnescens has inamyloid flesh, lacks clamp connections, and has large striate spores, (Castellano). Ramaria amyloidea, Ramaria rasilispora, and Ramaria rubricarnata are three other species with clamped hyphae that have amyloid stem flesh: they differ either in color or in the results of a battery of macrochemical tests, (Marr). Ramaria rubiginosa, Ramaria vinosimaculans, and Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans are other taxa that characteristically develop reddish brown stains, (Marr). Ramaria synaptopoda also develops reddish brown stains. See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora, Ramaria rasilisporoides, and Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1)*, Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria magnipes  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. magnipes^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 105. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  big-base goldie
NOTES  Features of Ramaria magnipes var. magnipes include 1) large stem with several thick primary branches dividing into numerous compact cauliflower-like branch systems, 2) branches light yellow with bright yellow tips, changing to pinkish buff and when injured often changing to brick red, 3) weakly slowly amyloid stem flesh, 4) fruiting in spring and summer, 5) nearly smooth, cylindric spores, and 6) clamp connections.^ Ramaria magnipes var. albidior is described from the Sierra Nevada in California (Petersen(3)), where the fruitbodies develop within the humus layer, breaking the surface only in adolescence: consequently, branches are white to muted yellow while the tips are close to typical colors. RANGE Ramaria magnipes var. magnipes collections were examined from WA, OR, and ID, (Petersen(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh weakly amyloid, the reaction requiring about 1 1/2 hours^, (Marr), slowly weakly amyloid: "in the R. magnipes - R. rasilispora complex the reaction is gray-blue to blue with mottling of green and yellow shades"; ferric sulphate in water green, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), stem flesh amyloid (positive black and blue reaction "relatively slow and weak, sometimes requiring up to one hour"); ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 18cm wide and up to 15cm high, circular to depressed elliptic in outline, often only semi-emergent above substrate, stem large to massive^, major branches 2-4, up to 2.5cm thick, "short when young, lengthening significantly", more or less round in cross-section, branches in 3-5 ranks, hardly round in cross-section, usually crowded, "internodes lengthening acropetally, diminishing upward abruptly at maturity", axils narrowly rounded to acute; tips "cauliflower-like when young, crowded, somewhat terraced when young, cuspidate to molar-like and coarse by adolescence", remaining so when old, when old "usually inflating to irregularly pistillariform shapes" [presumably pistillariform the same as pistilliform - shaped like a pistil, swollen at the base, with a moderately long neck and slightly swollen tip], (Petersen), 14-24cm wide, 9-25cm high, (Marr)
FLESH  in stem "solid, white, homogeneous to mottled, occasionally in a fan-shaped pattern"; in branches white in lower part, very pale cream color upward, (Petersen), fleshy-fibrous when fresh; white (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light yellow to pastel greenish yellow, slowly mellowing to light pallid salmon or fleshy buff, when old pallid fleshy ocher to fleshy tan; tips white where protected, where exposed bright yellow to intense chartreuse yellow, mellowing when old to buff-colored, tips often changing to brick red when crushed, bruised or exposed to frost^, (Petersen), immature branches and tips 'butter or maize yellow', mature branches and tips 'light yellow' fading to brownish 'pale orange', (Marr), young branches and tips butter yellow becoming light yellow, then aging to brownish pale orange, often changing to brick red when bruised or exposed to frost, (Phillips)
STEM  up to 8cm long and 8cm wide, "large to massive, single, rounded at base, covered with sandy to gritty soil, smooth, here and there tomentose between substrate particles, sometimes with clusters of abortive branches and branchlets"; "white to off-white, often weakly brunnescent to neutral brown"; "abortive branch systems cauliflower-like, white where protected"^, (Petersen), single, steeply tapered to broadly conic, usually large, 7-14cm x 4-6cm, rooting, (Marr), base wide, measured below branches wider than tall, base surface with a soft white covering that is easily rubbed off, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  mild or somewhat unpleasant^ (Marr), mild (Miller)
TASTE  mild when fresh becoming slightly bitter with cooking^, (Marr), mild and pleasant (Miller)
EDIBILITY  yes, but acts as a strong laxative in some people, (Miller)
HABITAT  collected under an understory of Vine Maple, in a mixed coniferous forest of Amabilis Fir, Lowland Hemlock, and Douglas-fir; fruiting in June and July^, (Marr), May to August (Phillips), on the ground in mixed coniferous forests, usually with Abies (true fir), spring and summer, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  light brown^ (Miller)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9.4-13.3 x 3.2-5.0 microns, average 11.51 x 3.98 microns, cylindric to narrowly elliptic (and then flattened adaxially), smooth in outline, ornamentation none or a few slender meandering ridges, contents several droplets, the droplets amorphous, dark, non-refringent, apiculus small, gradual^; basidia 4-spored, 50-65 x 7-8 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous, (Petersen), spores 10-14 x 3-4.5 microns, average 11.8 x 3.7 microns, cylindric, entirely smooth to very finely warted, warts cyanophilic, obscure under 1000x, clamp connections present, gloeoplerous hyphae not observed, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with large stem'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rasilispora is less intensely yellow, with less massive stem and shorter spores, and is not bitter when cooked, (Marr).
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Exeter(3)*, Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Miller(14)*, Scates-Barnhart(1)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria marrii  Scates^  Mycotaxon 33: 123. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  brown baga
NOTES  Features of Ramaria marrii include small to medium size, branches that are cocoa-colored (light brown to flesh-tan), a single heavy whitish base that bruises brown, whitish stem flesh, fruiting in spring, spores with variable ornamentation, and lack of clamp connections.^ RANGE Ramaria marrii was found in ID. There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid; ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter), strongly positive reaction to ferric sulphate in water and to ferric chloride in water, probably with branch sections, (Petersen)
FRUITING BODY  up to 9cm wide, up to 15cm high, elliptic to broadly ovate in outline^, single stem, 2-4 major branches, ascending, hardly round in cross-section, branches in 3-5 ranks, round in cross-section, internodes long in lower part, diminishing abruptly at maturity, axils rounded to narrowly rounded, tips somewhat terraced when young, crowded, minutely digitate, (Petersen), usually +/- as wide as tall, 4-10cm wide, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  in stem solid, soft-spongy in large stems, off-white, often minutely marbled, may be brunnescent^, (Petersen), solid, soft-spongy in large stems, off-white, often minutely marbled, hardly bruising, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches pallid tan in lower part, otherwise as upper branches, upper branches light brown or flesh tan, often with avellaneous tints, remaining so throughout development, tips deep fleshy tan when young, fading somewhat by maturity^, (Petersen), cocoa-colored (pallid tan in lower part, upwards light brown or flesh-tan, often with pinkish buff tints, tips deep fleshy tan when young, fading somewhat at maturity), (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  up to 6cm long and up to 4cm wide, "single, broadly rounded below, without abortive branchlets"; "white to off-white, chocolate brown where pressed by substrate objects, bruising brown to watery brown", hardly changing when cut; smooth to minutely tomentose^, (Petersen), white to off-white, bruising chocolate to watery brown on exterior, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  negligible to slightly agaricoid^ (Petersen)
TASTE  mildly bitter to weakly astringent^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on soil and duff in mixed coniferous forests, including Pinus monticola (Western White Pine) and Abies grandis (Grand Fir)^, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.6-11.6 x 4.3-5.4 microns, average 9.85 x 4.74 microns, elliptic to narrowly ovate, flattened adaxially, obscurely roughened in profile, "ornamentation variable, from almost absent to narrow meandering ridges, to large discrete, low warts", with one droplet, the droplet refringent and yellowish, wall up to 0.3 microns thick, hilar appendix papillate^; basidia 4-spored, 45-60 x 8-10 microns, clavate, clampless, "contents multiguttulate when mature, the guttule yellowish, refringent"; hymenium thickening, subhymenium rudimentary; trama hyphae of upper branches parallel to subparallel, tightly packed, free to locally adherent, 4-12 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, without clamp connections; stem trama hyphae 6-14 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled (up to 1.2 microns), tortuous, gnarled, tightly interwoven, without clamp connections, ampulliform inflations up to 16 microns wide, aliiform, thick-walled (up to 1.2 microns), hardly ornamented, gleoplerous hyphae not observed, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  in honor of Dr. Currie D. Marr, author of the definitive publication on the genus in Washington and friend of K. Scates-Barnhart and R. Petersen
SIMILAR  Ramaria spinulosa has similar color, brunnescent bruising reaction, similar reaction to iron salts, similar spore dimensions, and absent clamp connections, but differs in that 1) it is more slender with modest clean stem, whereas R. marrii is bulkier with generally heavy stem, 2) stem flesh is consistently tan to tan-brown, 3) dried fruitbodies are chalky-friable (whereas those of R. marrii dry firm-friable to hard), 4) spores have one to several droplets that are dark and non-refringent, whereas those of R. marrii have one droplet that is strongly refringent and yellowish, and 5) R. spinulosa fruits in fall, favoring the coastal regions from California through Washington, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria purpurissima  R.H. Petersen & Scates^  Sydowia Band 40: 211. 1987
ENGLISH NAMES  purple prince
NOTES  Features of Ramaria purpurissima include 1) intense violaceous to purple branches and tips, sometimes becoming ochraceous purple but not turning brown with maturity, 2) medium size but bulky and with massive white stem that occasionally has brunnescent areas, 3) red reaction with KOH on purple areas, 4) spores with complex low warts and ridges, and 5) strongly skeletalized generative stem trama hyphae.^ Petersen notes that spores are sparser on the branches than in most Ramaria species, which may be part of the reason for the retention of purple color. RANGE Ramaria purpurissima was found in northern ID (Petersen). A collection from OR labeled as this species is at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  KOH gives red reaction on purple parts (Petersen), stem flesh non-amyloid; ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  5-15cm wide, 8-10cm high, medium to small^, (Scates-Barnhart), up to 15cm wide, and 14cm high, nearly circular to very broadly obovate in outline, major branches 2-4, arising together, up to 3cm thick, more or less round in cross-section, branches in 3-6 ranks, rather crowded and abruptly arising from major branches, round in cross-section, crowded when young, short (up to 2cm long) when mature, axils rounded, internodes short throughout, tips cusped when young, dichotomous to double-dichotomous at maturity, minutely pointed, (Petersen)
FLESH  solid, hard; white, (Scates-Barnhart), in stem, solid, white, violaceous around larval tunnels, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  branches and tips wholly amethyst lilac, sometimes ochraceous purple when old but not turning brown^, (Scates-Barnhart), major branches dull violaceous to dull purplish, branches intensely violaceous to purple, tips intensely purple, (Petersen)
STEM  single, thick, massive, base white, occasionally with brunnescent areas^, (Scates-Barnhart), up to 9cm long and 5cm wide, "single, massive, rounded below", narrowing gradually downward, including very little substrate; "with fine white tomentum in creases, otherwise smooth, off-white, occasionally with brunnescent areas appearing as substrate smudges", (Petersen)
ODOR  slight^ (Scates-Barnhart), negligible (Petersen)
TASTE  somewhat bitter^ (Scates-Barnhart), negligible (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  much too beautiful and rare to eat, (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  under mixed conifers - Abies (fir), Tsuga (hemlock), Pseudotsuga (Douglas-fir)^, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-11.2 x 4.7-5.4 microns, average 10.3 x 5.0 microns, elliptic with adaxial bulge, conspicuously roughened in profile, complex low warts and short ridges, often in transverse orientation, hilar appendix prominent, broad, and with moderate throat, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, one to several droplets, the droplets discrete, refringent, and yellow-ocher^; basidia 4-spored, 70-80 x 8-10 microns, clavate, clamped, homogeneous when young, minutely granular to multiguttulate when mature; tramal hyphae of stem 3-11 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled (wall up to 2 microns thick), rigid, clamped, free interwoven, gloeoplerous hyphae not seen in stem, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'very purple, the most purple'
SIMILAR  Ramaria versatilis has less bulky stature and less massive stem, color is paler apically and changes to cinnamon with spore production, and stem tramal hyphae are thin-walled rather than heavily skeletalized, (Petersen). R. versatilis has branches that become browner with maturity (upper base and lower branches retaining a purple band), whereas R. purpurissima may be ochraceous purple when old, but does not turn brown, (Scates-Barnhart). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria violaceibrunnea.
SOURCES  Petersen(10), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*
