FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rainierensis  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 145. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rainierensis has 1) cream to buff branches with tips somewhat paler, 2) a single stem arising from a basal mycelial mat and rhizomorphic strands, 3) flesh that is leathery when fresh, 4) a faint odor of anise when mature, 5) a bitter taste, 6) spores with scattered cyanophilic warts and small ridges, 7) clamp connections, and 8) rhizomorphic strands with generative, skeletalized generative, and skeletal hyphae.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA and ID, (Petersen). It is also found in northern CA (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid^ (Marr)
FRUITING BODY  0.4-7cm wide, 2-8cm high, with a stem, major branches 2-4^, slender, flattened or channeled in cross-section, erect to somewhat spreading, axils subacute to narrowly rounded, flattened, usually sterile and decurrent, internodes diminishing gradually, tips minute, acute, (Petersen), young specimens sparsely branched, about 3 ranks, but branching up to 6 times from the base in older specimens, mostly dichotomous, axils subacute to narrowly U-shaped, branches slightly divergent, slender, 0.2-0.8cm wide, slightly flattened especially at nodes, internodes varying from 0.2-2.5cm long, shortly forked, cristate or rarely single near tips, tips acute, (Marr)
FLESH  coriaceous [leathery] when fresh, (Marr), in branches colored as surface, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches 'yellowish white' becoming 'grayish orange' when mature, tips somewhat paler than branches; "hymenium usually unilateral, inferior, sharply delimited in color and texture when dry", (Petersen, colors from Kornerup(2))
STEM  0.5-3.5cm x 0.2-1cm, lobed in cross-section, erect, smooth in upper part but often covered with basal mat in lower part; arising from rhizomorphic strands and basal mycelial mat on humus, rhizomorphic strands "white, slender, ill-defined, often minutely webbed, not extensive", basal mat "white to off-white, not extensive, cottony in lacunae of substrate, appressed, separable on substrate surface, sterile"^, (Petersen)
ODOR  faint of anise when mature^ (Petersen)
TASTE  bitter^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  growing among ferns near Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), (Marr), gregarious but not cespitose, September to October, (Petersen), fruits in humus or soil and matures above surface, December and March; associated with Abies spp. (fir), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Castellano)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'apricot yellow'^ (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.1-11.1 x 4.4-5.9 microns, average 9.51 x 5.03 microns, broadly cylindric to broadly elliptic, slightly flattened adaxially, roughened in profile, cyanophilic ornamentation of scattered, significantly raised, warts and small ridges, contents homogeneous, wall "up to 0.6 microns thick in median area, thinner distally", apiculus "eccentric, abrupt, broad, prominent"^; basidia 4-spored, 40-45 x 7.8 microns, narrowly clavate to nearly equal, clamped, contents more or less homogeneous; hyphae of rhizomorphic strands and basal mat of three types: 1) generative, 3-4.5 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, often encrusted with crystalline material, with conspicuous clamp connections, "inflated clamps up to 13 microns broad, somewhat thickened (wall up to 1.2 microns thick), coarsely and widely ornamented", 2) skeletalized generative hyphal segments 3-4.5 microns wide, refringent under phase contrast, apparently thick-walled (wall usually obscuring lumen), "relatively rigid to lax and crumpled in mounts", and 3) skeletal hyphae up to 9 microns wide at origin, "thick-walled (wall up to 3 microns thick, usually obscuring lumen), rare, irregularly shaped, tapering somewhat, refringent under phase contrast"; hyphae of upper branch trama generative, 3.0-9.6 microns wide, parallel, rather straight, with clamp connections, "inflated clamps up to 11 microns broad, thin-walled, unornamented", (Petersen), spores 7-10 x 4.5-6 microns, average 8.5 x 5 microns, elliptic with prominent lateral apiculus that is commonly 1 x 1.5 microns, ornamented with distinct lobed cyanophilic warts arranged in subspirals, (Marr), skeletal hyphae in rhizomorphs nearly acyanophilic, (Castellano)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'from Rainier [National Park]', where type was found
SIMILAR  Ramaria gracilis has smaller spores, and different hyphal construction, (Petersen). Ramaria suecica is less slender and has no skeletalized generative hyphae in the rhizomorphic strands, and the rhizomorphs have inflated clamp connections that are unornamented, whereas in R. rainierensis the inflated clamp connections of the rhizomorphic strands are conspicuously ornamented (1250x) and skeletalized generative and skeletal hyphae are both present in the rhizomorphic strands - spores of R. suecica are also slightly narrower, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Marr(1), Castellano(1), Exeter(3)*, Kornerup(2)
72
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rasilispora  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. rasilispora^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 107. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora has 1) pale ochraceous yellow to fleshy buff branches, 2) branch tips that are light yellow to light greenish yellow sometimes blushing to pinkish or brownish, 3) a distinct white stem, 4) spring fruiting, 5) cylindric spores that are smooth or with a few slender ridges, and 6) clamp connections.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA, OR, ID, and CA, and it was commonly collected, (Petersen). There are collections (variety unspecified) from BC at the Pacific Forestry Centre.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh weakly amyloid, the reaction requiring about 1 1/2 hours, (Marr), amyloid reaction: "in the R. magnipes - R. rasilispora complex the reaction is gray-blue to blue with mottling of green and yellow shades"; ferric sulphate in water green reaction, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), stem flesh amyloid (positive reaction slow and weak, taking about one hour); ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 22cm wide, and up to 15cm high, circular to depressed-elliptic in outline, "usually emergent from substrate but often semiemergent", stem single^, major branches 2-4, ascending but not erect, more or less round in cross-section, branches in 4-6 ranks, "crowded when young, lengthening to rather open by maturity", more or less round in cross-section, internodes diminishing gradually at maturity, axils rounded, often splitting, tips crowded when young, "dentate to irregularly coarse at all ages", (Petersen), 6-15cm wide, 5-15cm high, large, broad, "branching up to 7 times from the base, nodes polychotomous or dichotomous, axils acute or turbinate, branches straight, moderately divergent and thickish", primary branches very stout, 2-4cm wide, upper ones mostly 0.2-1.5cm wide, internodes lengthening up to 5cm when mature, polydigitate near tips, tips obtuse to rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  in stem solid, white, usually mottled with watery spots; in branches, homogeneous, white^, (Petersen), fleshy-fibrous or punky-fibrous in the stem when fresh; white, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  pale ochraceous yellow to fleshy buff, tips light yellow to pastel chartreuse yellow, often blushing to "onion skin pink" where exposed to cold, dry air^, (Petersen), lower branches off-white where underground, young branches 'light orange' to 'apricot yellow' maturing pale 'grayish orange', tips colored the same or turning 'dark brown' when old or with drying, (Marr)
STEM  up to 7cm long and up to 4cm wide, "usually smaller, single, obtusely rounded at base, usually wider than high"; white, not brunnescent; "minutely tomentose between substrate particles"; "commonly with abortive branchlets in the form of blunt or tapering stumps"^, (Petersen), 3-6cm x 2.5-5.5cm, single, conic, stout; off-white where underground, (Marr)
ODOR  very weakly aromatic^, (Petersen), mild to distinctively fungoid, or somewhat unpleasant, (Marr)
TASTE  negligible to mildly bitter^, (Petersen), not distinctive (Marr)
EDIBILITY  eaten routinely by many people (Petersen)
HABITAT  fruiting on soil and duff in mixed coniferous forests, including Pinus monticola (Western White Pine), Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), and Abies grandis (Grand Fir), May and June^, (Petersen), terrestrial, in coniferous woods, spring and summer, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.3-11.5 x 3.6-4.3 microns, average 9.95 x 3.87 microns, cylindric, occasionally subboletoid (swollen adaxially), smooth in outline (at 1000x), ornamentation none or a very few slender meandering ridges, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, content 1 to several droplets, the droplets amorphous, dark, non-refringent^; basidia 4-spored, 50-60 x 7-8 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous, sterigmata slender, sublyriform [somewhat lyre-like]; clamp connections present, (Petersen), spores 8-11 x 3-4 microns, average 9.5 x 3.4 microns, cylindric, entirely smooth to very finely warted, warts cyanophilic, obscure under 1000x magnification, basidia clamped, clamp connections abundant; gloeoplerous hyphae rare, 3-5.5 microns wide, or up to 12 microns wide in localized bulbous regions, (Marr), spores 8-11.5 x 3-4.5 microns, (Trudell)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with smooth spores'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rasilispora var. scatesiana has paler fruitbodies, it is colored cream to pale yellow, and branch tips are less bright and less deep yellow, (Petersen). R. rasilispora var. scatesiana "differs in being somewhat paler colored, and occasionally occurring in fall, although it too is primarily a spring-fruiter", (Trudell). Ramaria magnipes is more intensely yellow, with a more massive stem, longer spores, and bitterness when cooked, (Marr). R. magnipes has longer spores but cannot be distinguished from R. rasilispora by stem size or color - the base surface is covered by a soft white coating that is easily rubbed off, (M. Beug, pers. comm.). The only sure way to differentiate dried specimens of R. magnipes is by spore length, but R. magnipes is more intensely colored when fresh and the stem surface is more easily brunnescent, dried specimens seem to be stocky and stout, with larger stems and shorter branching patterns, and they dry darker and dingier, with harder, darker stem flesh, (whereas those of R. rasilispora are cream, with white flesh even when dry), (Petersen). Ramaria flavobrunnescens var. aromatica has small obscure warts, (Marr). Ramaria amyloidea, Ramaria maculatipes, and Ramaria rubricarnata are three other species with clamped hyphae that have amyloid stem flesh, (Marr).
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Trudell(4)*, Exeter(3)*, AroraPocket*
73
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rasilispora  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. scatesiana  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 108. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  Kit's creamy coral
NOTES  Ramaria rasilispora var. scatesiana is differentiated from Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora by the former's paler color, cream to yellow^ (Marr, p.107). RANGE Collections were examined from WA, ID, and CA, (Petersen(3)). It possibly occurs in Finland and Poland (Petersen(13)). There is a Paul Kroeger collection from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  IKI negative to weakly positive on stem flesh; ferric sulphate in water green reaction, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), non-amyloid to weakly positive on stem flesh; ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 8cm wide, up to 24cm high, circular to obovate in outline, major branches 2-4^, ascending, hardly round in cross-section, branches in 4-6 ranks, somewhat crowded when young, open when mature, more or less round in cross-section, internodes diminishing upward gradually when mature, "axils rounded to obtusely angled", tips crass [thick] at all ages, molar-like when young, expanding by maturity to bluntly cauliflower-like to obtusely angled, (Petersen(3)), 6-20cm wide, 5-20cm high, medium to large, broad, cauliflower-like when young, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  in stem solid, homogeneous, white; in branches, somewhat stringy; white^, (Petersen(3)), fleshy to softly stringy; white, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  pale to light ochraceous cream, sometimes with a hint of pink when old, tips clear yellow to pastel greenish yellow when young, mellowing to buff-colored, when old often with a hint of pink^, (Petersen(3)), cream to yellow, 'yellowish white' to 'light yellow', (Marr), young branches and tips cream to light yellow to pastel greenish, maturing pale dull orange or buff, sometimes aging pinkish or turning pinkish red when frosted or exposed to cold dry air, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  up to 6cm long and up to 4cm wide, "usually smaller, single, rounded at base"; white to off-white, "not brunnescent or very weakly so upward on handling"; smooth in upper part, often mycelioid at base, not tomentose; often with blunt abortive protuberances^, (Petersen(3)), base single, narrowing downward, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  negligible to weakly fragrant^ (Petersen(3)), none or fungoid, (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  negligible to weakly musty or krauty [sour]^ (Petersen(3)), mild, (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  yes (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  on the ground in mixed conifer woods, with Abies (true fir), mainly spring, but also summer and fall^, (Scates-Barnhart), May, June, August, (Petersen(3))
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9.4-11.9 x 3.2-4.3 microns, average 10.62 x 3.73 microns, cylindric to subboletoid, almost smooth in outline, ornamentation absent or a few slender, meandering ridges, contents "univacuolate to guttulate, with guttules dark, amorphous, non-refringent", wall up to 0.3 microns thick, apiculus gradual^; basidia 60-70 x 7-8 microns, sterigmata spindly, more or less straight; hyphae of stem trama and upper branch trama usually (but not invariably) with clamp connections, ampulliform inflations and gloeoplerous hyphae not observed, (Petersen(3)), spores 8.3-11 x 3-4 microns, average length 10.6 microns, (Petersen(13))
NAME ORIGIN  'scatesiana' after Kit Scates-Barnhart "and other members of the Idaho Mycological Society who so generously contributed specimens, notes, and colored slides of ramarias occurring in their 1970 spring flora"^, (Petersen(3))
SIMILAR  Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora and Ramaria magnipes are deeper in color, and seems to appear only in spring, whereas R. rasilispora var. scatesiana occurs both in spring and fall - for other differences from R. magnipes, see Ramaria rasilispora var. rasilispora, (Petersen(3)). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria thiersii.
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Marr(1), Petersen(13), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rasilisporoides  R.L. Exeter^  Ramaria of the Pacific Northwestern United States p78. 2006
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria rasilisporoides include 1) large size, 2) yellow colors that are changeable - white where protected, yellow where exposed, pale orange when old, 3) compact cauliflower appearance when young, 4) elongating branches and tips that become knobby when mature, 5) deep rooting, 6) amyloid stem base, 7) short, finely warted spores, and 8) clamped basidia, "An entirely yellow medium to large coral ... with a deeply rooting stipe and many abortive branchlets. Terrestrial in coastal coniferous forests in autumn." (Exeter(3))^. RANGE The type was found in OR. Distribution includes the Oregon Coastal Range.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ferric sulfate + water (FSW) applied to the stem context negative; reaction of the stem tissues to Melzer's reagent amyloid
FRUITING BODY  10-28cm x 7-10cm, cauliflower-like when young, with large to massive stem
FLESH  in branches "pale yellow, white yellow lacking any orange or salmon colorations"; in stem "fleshy fibrous, hard and firm, cartilaginous, grainy mottled, white"
BRANCH COLOR  lower branches white to very pale yellow, "upper branches pale yellow, yellow-cream, pale yellow to canary yellow, maturing pale caramel colored or 'creamed coffee' color to a brownish orange", tips white where protected, pale to citron yellow, the most intense coloration of the fruitbody, maturing colored as the upper branches
STEM  5-11cm x 3-4cm, large to massive, "single to compound, often deeply rooting with recurved abortive branchlets common high on the stipe; white (somewhat watery brunnescent where handled) where protected, abortive branchlets pale yellow where exposed"
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  coniferous forests - Pseudotsuga (Douglas-fir), Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar), in fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  7-10(11) x 3-4.5 microns, narrowly cylindric, "smooth to mostly finely warted or with broken ridges arrayed linearly toward the hilar appendage"^; basidia 4-spored, 37-77 x 4-7 microns, clavate, bases with clamp connections; hyphae of context 3-6 microns wide, with clamp connections
NAME ORIGIN  means 'like (Ramaria) rasilispora'
SIMILAR  Only five other Ramaria species have a positive amyloid reaction of the stem tissues and possess basidia with clamp connections: Ramaria amyloidea, Ramaria maculatipes, Ramaria magnipes, Ramaria rasilispora, and Ramaria rubricarnata: R. amyloidea is salmon-colored with a stem context "that has a distinctive brown band ('rusty root') and reacts blue-green to FSW", R. maculatipes is salmon-colored and has a stem with wine-colored stains, R. magnipes and R. rasilispora both have smooth spores (under oil immersion) and fruit primarily in spring, and R. rubricarnata is mostly salmon-colored and has longer and wider spores (about 10-11.4 x 4.3 microns), (Exeter(3)).
SOURCES  Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria raveneliana  (Coker apud R.H. Petersen) R.H. Petersen^  Sydowia 35: 195. 1982;  Ramaria conjunctipes (Coker) Corner  var. raveneliana  Marr & D.E. Stuntz;  Clavaria raveneliana  Coker apud R.H. Petersen
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria raveneliana include 1) small size, 2) bundled base, a few hollow branches to each stem, 3) flesh colored branches, their sharp tips clear yellow, 4) growth on the ground under hemlock and Rhododendron, 5) spores with cyanophilic ornamentation of small warts or raised patches, and 6) absent clamp connections. RANGE It was described from NC, Marr(1) implies its presence in WA, and Exeter(3) gives a distribution in the Pacific Northwest.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid, ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 4cm high, obovoid to fusiform in shape, sparingly branched, branches few to each stem, many in the fascicle, "usually hollow, brittle, slender", axils acute, tips acute, not prolonged; fruitbodies of the type of C. raveneliana are consistently hollow, both in stem and branch parts, (Petersen), typically 4cm wide and 4cm high, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  stringy when fresh, chalky-friable when dry^, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  "flesh color", tips pale clear yellow, near "pale orange yellow" to "pale ochraceous salmon"^, (Petersen)
STEM  fasciculate [bundled], stems up to 0.4cm wide, stuffed to hollow; off-white to very pale watery pink in lower part^, (Petersen)
ODOR  none^ (Petersen)
TASTE  none^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  on poor soil under Tsuga (hemlock) and Rhododendron^, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.9-11.2 x 5.0-6.1(6.3) microns, average 9.94 x 5.52 microns, "broadly cylindric to elliptic, flattened adaxially, very obscurely roughened in profile", ornamentation of small, scattered, strongly cyanophilic warts or raised patches, contents homogeneous to obscurely guttulate, weakly cyanophilic, apiculus "abrupt, almost perpendicular, leaving no throat"^; basidia 4-spored, 37-45 x 7-9 microns, "clavate, thin-walled, easily collapsed, without clamp connections"; hyphae of stem trama 3-17 microns wide, colorless, thin-walled, without clamp connections, often inflated irregularly, lysing in medullary part of stem, irregularly interwoven elsewhere, gloeoplerous hyphae not observed; hyphae of upper branch trama 2-5 microns wide, thin-walled, colorless, generally parallel, without clamp connections, adherent to agglutinated, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  probably after mycologist H.W. Ravenel
SIMILAR  Marr & Stuntz distinguish this taxon from varieties of Ramaria conjunctipes on the basis of hollow stems and branches and fruitbodies 4cm tall or less, (Marr). R. conjunctipes has saffron branches (not "flesh color"), stem and branches are not hollow, and spores are narrower, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(22) (colors in quotation marks from Ridgway), Scates-Barnhart(1), Marr(1) (as R. conjunctipes var. raveneliana), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubella  (Schaeff. per Krombh.) R.H. Petersen  forma blanda  R.H. Petersen^  Bibliotheca Mycologica 43: 89. 1975
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rubella var. rubella has rhizomorphic strands turning bright mauve pink in 10% KOH, with hymenium amphigenous, or if not, then sterile areas restricted to narrow lines decurrent from axils, but R. rubella var. blanda has rhizomorphic strands unchanging or yellowish in 10% KOH, and the hymenium is clearly unilateral, especially in dried specimens; spores are also slightly shorter, (Petersen)^. The description is derived from Petersen(11) except where indicated. RANGE Collections were examined from BC, WA, CA, and TN - it is a rare taxon, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  rhizomorphs unchanging or yellowish in 10% KOH, spore-bearing surface smoky green in ferric sulphate in water
FRUITING BODY  up to 5.5cm wide, up to 8cm high, fusiform [vertical spindle-shaped] to nearly spherical in general outline, stem very short^, branches up to 0.4cm thick," more or less strict to open and spreading, often flattened, especially at axils, and then branching somewhat antler-like", internodes diminishing gradually, "axils narrowly to broadly rounded, usually sterile, the sterile area decurrent by a line", spore-bearing area "occasionally amphigenous to predominantly unilateral, smooth", sterile surface rugulose [finely wrinkled], apparently somewhat paler than spore-bearing surface; tips "delicate and erect to open and rounded-dichotomous"
FLESH  in branches tough, whitish
BRANCH COLOR  "pinkish buff", "fawn color", "pale pinkish cinnamon", "light vinaceous cinnamon", to "avellaneous", tips white to pale cream, (Petersen), pink-tan, pale pink-cinnamon, pale vinaceous cinnamon, to avellaneous; tips white to pale cream, (Castellano)
STEM  "very short, almost branched from base, up to 1cm thick, flattened to lobed in cross-section, arising from copious basal mycelium", white at base, upward colored as branches; basal mycelium in small sheets and slender (up to 0.1cm thick), discrete rhizomorphic strands, or a combination of these, white or whitish, separable from substrate and branching through it, extensive, unchanging or yellowish in 10% KOH
ODOR  not distinct
TASTE  acrid [peppery]
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on coniferous wood (Picea) and hardwood (Alnus)
SPORE DEPOSIT  "cinnamon buff" (Petersen), red-tan (Castellano)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.3-8.1 x 4.4-5.9 microns, average 7.14 x 4.92 microns, broadly ovoid to broadly elliptic, slightly flattened adaxially, roughened in profile, cyanophilic ornamentation of scattered prominent warts or short meandering ridges, wall up to 0.5 microns thick, contents apparently homogeneous or with one droplet, the droplet refringent under phase contrast, apiculus "prominent, eccentric, over 1 micron long, often with hump at upper base, tapering distally"^; basidia 4-spored, 45-50 x 7.4-8.9 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous; hyphae of the rhizomorphic strands of 2 types: 1) generative, 1.5-4.5 microns wide, thin-walled, tightly packed, colorless, with conspicuous clamp connections, "inflated clamps up to 15 microns broad, somewhat thick-walled, (wall up to 1.2 microns thick), abundant, unornamented", and 2) skeletalized generative, 2.2-3.8 microns wide, "thick-walled, refringent under phase contrast, arising and ending in clamp connections, usually over 1000 microns long, straight"; hyphae of fruitbody base trama skeletalized generative, 3.0-9.6 microns wide, "thick-walled (wall up to 2.6 microns thick or obscuring lumen)", with clamp connections, gnarled, interwoven; hyphae of upper branch trama generative, "from thin-walled to thick-walled (wall up to 4 microns thick, or obscuring lumen)", with clamp connections, generally parallel
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria rubella forma rubella.

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubella  (Schaeff. per Krombh.) R.H. Petersen  forma rubella  R.H. Petersen^  Biblthca Mycol. 43: 89. 1975;  == Clavaria rubella  Schaeffer;  = Ramaria acris  (Peck) Corner  Trans. Br. Mycol. Soc. 44: 235. 1961;  = Clavaria acris  Peck
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rubella forma rubella has 1) dull violaceous rose (ruddy) fruitbodies with short or absent stem, 2) upper branches more brightly colored, tips with the last half millimeter white at first, 3) deep red-purple reaction of spore-bearing surface to KOH, 4) white rhizomorphs that turn light mauve pink in 10% KOH, 5) short spores with cyanophilic ornamentation of low scattered small warts to delicate ridges, and 6) rhizomorphic strands with generative hyphae and skeletalized generative hyphae.^ RANGE Collections were examined from BC, WA, OR, ID, NB, NS, ON, PQ, CA, MA, MI, NC, NH, NY, TN, VT, Austria, Germany, and Switzerland, and it is also reported from Japan, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  spore-bearing surface deep red-purple in KOH, spore-bearing surface dark slate green in ferric sulphate in water, rhizomorphic strands change to bright mauve pink in 10% KOH, (Petersen), stem flesh inamyloid, (Marr)
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm high, stem single to multiple, branches "numerous, erect to somewhat lax, usually subparallel, generally forming a fusiform mature fruitbody but often of irregular height"^, lobed in lower part when sectioned, round to flattened upward, internodes usually gradually diminishing, axils rounded to narrowly rounded, tips delicate, acute, spore-bearing area amphigenous to often unilateral, "sterile areas usually as narrow lines decurrent from axils, minutely rugulose to wooly", not significantly different in color from spore-bearing surface, (Petersen), 0.5-7cm wide, 3-10cm high, small, sometimes broad, stem lacking, branching from substrate up to 8 times, some nodes trichotomous to polychotomous but most dichotomous, axils widely subacute to U-shaped, branches open, subparallel, or divaricate, internodes up to 2cm long, mostly 0.5-1cm long, branches somewhat flattened especially at nodes, slender, mostly 0.1-0.4cm wide, young terminal branches flabellate-cristate, single or bifid near tips when mature, tips acute, (Marr)
FLESH  rubbery-coriaceous [rubbery-leathery] when fresh; paler than surface^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  dull reddish (ruddy to ruddy purplish) except tips white when young, (branches pale red when young, aging grayish red)^, (Scates-Barnhart), dull violaceous rose ("Japan rose", "onion skin pink", when young, "avellaneous", "buff pink", "light vinaceous cinnamon", "vinaceous cinnamon", when mature, fading to "fawn color", "pinkish cinnamon", "cinnamon" when old), bruising to "fawn color" or somewhat vinescent when handled, upper branches more brightly colored ("russet", "vinaceous russet"), tips abruptly (apical 0.05cm) white, colored as branches when old, (Petersen), basal branches of fresh fruitbodies white in tomentum covered areas, otherwise 'pale red' to 'grayish orange', most branches 'pale red' in young fruitbodies or 'grayish red' in older ones, tips white, slowly maturing concolorous, bruised areas slightly translucent but not markedly changing color, (Marr), violaceous pink-ocher, white at the tips, (Courtecuisse)
STEM  "single to multiple, slender to stout, lobed in cross-section, often branched from base, arising from and often covered by a whitish mycelial felt" that spreads over the adjacent substrate up to 1cm in radius; whitish in lower part to "deep Quaker gray" or "hair brown" upward, when old "benzo brown" to "fuscous", "subtended by a network of delicate, occasional, whitish rhizomorphic strands which change to bright mauve pink in 10% KOH"^, (Petersen), colored similarly to branches except where covered by whitish cottony fuzz, (Scates-Barnhart), stem lacking, "conspicuous velvety tomentum spreading into a surface mycelial mat surrounding basal branches, white rhizomorphic strands prominent, some conspicuously long, elastic", (Marr)
ODOR  none or faintly of anise^ (Petersen), not distinctive to musty sweet, (Scates-Barnhart), not distinctive or faintly musty-sweet, (Marr)
TASTE  very slowly acrid [peppery] to strongly acrid^ (Petersen), mild to usually acrid (biting), (Scates-Barnhart), not distinctive, (Marr)
EDIBILITY  uncertain (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  on wood, August to November^, (Petersen), growing from decayed conifer wood, collected in a mixed Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir) and Thuja plicata (Western Red-cedar) forest, September to November, (Marr), on decaying conifer wood or hardwood, fall, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.3-9.5 x 4.1-5.5 microns, average 7.5 x 4.29 microns, broadly cylindric to slightly ovoid, roughened in profile, strongly cyanophilic ornamentation of low, scattered, small warts to delicate ridges not covering significant wall areas, contents no droplets to several minute droplets, the droplets non-refringent and brownish under phase contrast, wall up to 0.3 microns thick, weakly cyanophilic, apiculus "prominent, eccentric, prolonged, not perpendicular"^, basidia 4-spored, 35-60 x 7-7.5 microns, clavate, clamped; hyphae of rhizomorphic strands of 2 types: 1) thin-walled generative hyphae, 2-5 microns wide, colorless, conspicuously clamped, and 2) skeletalized generative hyphae, 3-4 microns wide, thick-walled (wall often completely obscuring lumen), "arising from clamped septum of generative hypha and almost invariably ending in the same manner (although rarely ending blindly)", acyanophilic, colorless, inflated clamp connections abundant, up to 35 microns wide, somewhat thick-walled (wall up to 1 micron thick), not ornamented; hyphae of upper branch trama 2.2-7.5 microns wide, colorless, thick-walled to extremely thick-walled (wall obscuring lumen), long-celled, with conspicuous clamp connections, subparallel, commonly anastomosing, acyanophilic, inflated clamp connections not observed, (Petersen), spores 5.5-8 x 4-5.5 microns, average 6.4 x 4.6 microns, elliptic, ornamented with numerous small but distinct papillate cyanophilic warts, prominent lateral apiculus present, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'red'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubella forma blanda has 1) rhizomorphic strands that are unchanging or yellowish in 10% KOH, and 2) hymenium that is clearly unilateral, especially in dried specimens, whereas R. rubella forma rubella has rhizomorphic strands turning bright mauve pink in 10% KOH, hymenium amphigenous, or if not, then sterile areas restricted to narrow lines decurrent from axils, and 3) the spores of forma blanda are also slightly shorter, (Petersen). Ramaria apiculata var. brunnea 1) is tan or brown (not ruddy or ruddy purple), 2) rhizomorphic strands turn weak brown in 10% KOH, (not bright pinkish mauve), and 3) it has spores that are elliptic rather than broadly ovoid, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(11), Scates-Barnhart(1), Marr(1) (as Ramaria acris) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Corner(2) (as Clavaria acris), Corner(3) (as Ramaria acris?), Exeter(3)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubiginosa  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 109. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  yellow winey-base
NOTES  Features of Ramaria rubiginosa include 1) medium size, 2) thick, single or somewhat compound stem, 3) branches that are light yellow with bright yellow tips, 4) any part of the fruitbody but especially the base bruising or staining 'reddish brown', 5) spores finely ornamented with lobed anastomosing warts, and 6) absent clamp connections.^ It is possible that this is the same species as Ramaria sanguinea (Pers.) Quel., in which case Ramaria rubiginosa would be reduced to a synonym of that species (Petersen(12)). The average spore length divided by the average spore width is 2.14: there is apparently an unnamed species that is similar but with average spore length divided by average spore width = 2.50, (M. Beug, pers. comm.). RANGE Ramaria rubiginosa is found at least in WA (Marr). There are collections from OR at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid or very faintly amyloid, (Marr), while the hymenial surface of all Ramarias turn blue-green in FSW, the reaction of the hymenial surface of Ramaria rubiginosa was unusually fast and intense, however the reaction of the stem context is actually only slowly positive to FSW: the strong reaction as recorded in Marr(1) is an error (M. Beug, pers. comm.)
FRUITING BODY  4-17cm wide, 5-18cm high, single or somewhat compound stem^, branch systems dividing 3-7 times in a cruciate dichotomous manner, upper branches compressed when young, even in mature fruitbodies internodes longest in lower branches, primary branches 1-3cm wide, upper branches mostly slender, 0.1-1cm wide, "axils acute or turbinate and branches slight to moderately divergent", cusped to finely divided near tips, tips subacute to rounded, (Marr), lower branches thick; branches upper branches with short intervals between forks, (Scates-Barnhart)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; white^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  light yellow with bright yellow tips, any part staining +/- winy (reddish brown)^, (Scates-Barnhart), lower branches 'yellowish white' to 'light yellow', terminal branches colored the same or more intensely yellow when young, about 'light yellow' to 'sunflower yellow', any part bruising or staining 'reddish brown', (Marr)
STEM  single, thick, 2-8cm x 2-6cm, "or subcompound, with two to several thick axes arising from a small primary root-like structure"; base of fresh fruitbody 'yellowish white'; any part of the fruitbody but especially the base bruising or staining 'reddish brown'^, (Marr), base thick, any part of fruitbody but especially the base staining +/- winy (reddish brown), (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  not distinctive or faintly sweet^, (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  probably edible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October and November^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'grayish yellow'^ (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-11 x 3.5-6 microns, average 9.4 x 4.4 microns, cylindric, finely ornamented with lobed, anastomosing warts, these faintly more cyanophilic than the general wall^; basidia mostly 4-spored, 42-85 x 7-11 microns, clavate, clampless, inclusions cyanophilic; clamp connections absent, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'rusty'
SIMILAR  Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans has a less stout fruitbody, less conspicuous stains, clamped hyphae, and acantho-dendroid gloeoplerous hyphae in the stem, (Marr). Pacific Northwest species reacting with ferric sulphate 10% aqueous solution in the stem base include Ramaria testaceoflava (clamped basidia, average spore length 11.8 microns), Ramaria amyloidea (clamped basidia, average spore length 8.9 microns, stem flesh amyloid), Ramaria velocimutans (clamped basidia, average spore length 9.0 microns, stem flesh inamyloid), R. rubiginosa (clampless basidia, no rusty root), Ramaria celerivirescens (clampless basidia, rusty root, normal terminal branches), and Ramaria claviramulata (clampless basidia, rusty root, chubby terminal branches, branches turn reddish brown with 20% KOH), (Exeter(2)). Ramaria vinosimaculans, Ramaria maculatipes, and Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans are other taxa that characteristically develop reddish brown stains, (Marr). Ramaria synaptopoda also has a yellow fruitbody and reddish brown stains on the lower part of the fruitbody, but has a pronounced fasciculate [bundled] habit. Ramaria rubribrunnescens also develops reddish brown stains.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*, Exeter(2), Petersen(12) (discussing Ramaria sanguinea), Siegel(2)*
79
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubribrunnescens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 111. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  sweet formosa stainer
NOTES  Features of Ramaria rubribrunnescens include 1) medium size, 2) a single stem that becomes somewhat bundled when older, 3) branches that are pastel red with light yellow tips, the general color becoming a shade browner than light orange when old, 4) the base white to orangish white when fresh, 5) lower branches and base staining dark reddish brown, 6) odor that is sweet when old (resembling anise), 7) spores smooth or with very fine ornamentation, and 8) absent clamp connections.^ RANGE Ramaria rubribrunnescens has been found at least in WA (Marr). There is one collection from OR at Oregon State University. There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem context inamyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative with stem flesh, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  5.5-11cm wide, 7-16cm high, medium size, stem single but becoming a subfascicle [somewhat bundled] when older^, branching up to 9 times from stem, "mostly dichotomous at least in the upper nodes, axils subacute or narrowly u-shaped, branches slightly divergent", internodes elongated when mature, branches generally slender, mostly 0.1-0.5cm wide, bifid to finely divided near tips, tips acute to rounded, (Marr)
FLESH  fleshy-fibrous when fresh; colored as the surface^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  when immature 'pastel red' with 'light yellow' tips, when old the general color a shade browner than 'light orange', lower branches staining 'oxblood red'^, (Marr), tips light yellow, immature branches pastel red, aging a shade browner than light orange, lower branches staining dark reddish brown, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  single, often slender and tapering, e.g. 1cm long and 0.7cm wide, basal abortive or primordial branches common when young, when older the stem "a subfascicle of several to numerous slender primary branches"; base of fresh fruiting body white to 'orange white', base and lower branches staining 'oxblood red', when old the staining extensive in the lower parts^, (Marr), base white to orangish white, base and lower branches staining dark reddish brown, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  sweet when old, resembling anise^, (Marr)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Marr)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October and November^, (Marr)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'grayish yellow'^ (Marr)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-14 x 3.5-5 microns, average 12.3 x 4.4 microns, subcylindric "with slight suprahilar depression and dorsal convexity", ornamentation very fine, some spores smooth or nearly so, entire spore wall cyanophilic^; basidia 2-4-spored, 41-67 x 8-11 microns, clavate, clampless, contents strongly cyanophilic; clamp connections absent, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'becoming red-brown'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubiginosa, Ramaria vinosimaculans, Ramaria cystidiophora var. maculans, and Ramaria maculatipes all characteristically develop reddish brown stains, but R. rubribrunnescens is distinguished by long spores (12.3 microns average length) and the absence of clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria synaptopoda also develops reddish brown stains.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Castellano(1)*, Exeter(3)*
80
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubricarnata  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. pallida  R.H. Petersen & Scates^  Mycotaxon 33: 133. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rubricarnata var. pallida differs from the type variety in 1) having muted colors of branches and branch flesh, larger stems, and longer major and secondary branches, 2) fruiting only known in spring (type variety fruiting in both spring and fall), and 3) having average spore length aver 11 microns.^ RANGE Collections were examined from ID and CA (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh weakly amyloid; ferric sulphate in water green reaction, probably with branch sections^, (Petersen), stem flesh weakly amyloid, ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm wide, up to 13cm high, broadly obovate to nearly circular in outline, stem large^, major branches 3-5, hardly round in cross-section, branches in 4-6 ranks, divergent to flaring, internodes diminishing abruptly when mature, axils round, tips blunt and cuspidate when young, by maturity blunt and divergent, (Petersen)
FLESH  in stem solid, stringy, sometimes minutely streaked, white; in branches streaked off-white and "pinkish buff"^, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches off-white ("pale pinkish salmon"), branches buff-colored to pallid buffy yellow with a hint of salmon ("light ochraceous buff", "pinkish buff", "cream color", lighter than "light ochraceous salmon"), tips dull greenish yellow ("amber yellow", "mustard yellow") when young, by maturity light yellow ("buff yellow")^, (Petersen), buff to pallid buffy yellow, with a hint of salmon, tips greenish yellow when young, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  up to 5cm long and 7cm wide, broadly tapering to a rounded base; white, weakly or not brunnescent; smooth to pruinose where protected; usually with some recurved abortive white stumps but not branchlets^, (Petersen)
ODOR  negligible to mildly fabaceous [bean-like]^ (Petersen)
TASTE  negligible to mildly fabaceous^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  spring^ (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9.7-14.4 x 4.0-4.7 microns, average 11.44 x 4.27 microns, elliptic to subcylindric, obscurely roughened in profile, ornamentation of small, low warts and scattered meandering ridges, contents yellowish, homogeneous to or with 1 to several obscure droplets, the droplets dark ocher, refringent, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus not prominent, with moderate throat^; basidia 4-spored, 65-72 x 9-11 microns, clavate, clamped, contents densely multiguttulate at maturity, yellow, sterigmata delicate, lyriform [lyre-like]; clamp connections present, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  'pallida' means 'pale'
SIMILAR  Ramaria thiersii 1) has inamyloid stem flesh, 2) "branch colors usually do not include any salmon tint", 3) branch flesh is pure white or tinted salmon, 4) "all parts show conspicuous brunnescence where handled or rubbed", and  5) the stem appears large but usually is cleft deeply by the major branches, (Petersen). Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata is differentiated from the other varieties as follows: var. rubricarnata has lower branches stocky and colored pallid salmon buff to occasionally yellow, upper branches stocky and pallid salmon buff to occasionally yellow, flesh of branches bright salmon, flesh of dried stem yellow, fruiting in fall and sometimes spring, and average spore length about 10 microns, var. pallida has lower branches elongate and cream colored, upper branches elongate and cream-colored, flesh of branches pallid pinkish, flesh of dried stem white, fruiting in spring, and average spore length over 11 microns, and var. verna has lower branches stocky and yellow, upper branches stocky and salmon-colored, flesh of branches bright salmon, flesh of the dried stem white, fruiting in spring, and average spore length over 11 microns, (Petersen).
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubricarnata  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. rubricarnata^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 109. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata has 1) a large to massive off-white stem, weakly bruising reddish brown, 2) branches that are pale salmon, salmon buff, or occasionally yellow, 3) branch tips that are light to bright yellow but darken when old, 4) flesh of the branches bright salmon, 5) dried flesh of stem yellow, 6) fruiting in fall or sometimes spring, and 7) spores with cyanophilic ornamentation of low small warts and short meandering ridges in a somewhat spiral pattern.^ All varieties of Ramaria rubricarnata share the general color of branches and tips and the general color of the branch flesh, as well as amyloidity of stem flesh and presence of clamp connections. Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata is differentiated from the other varieties as follows: var. rubricarnata has lower branches stocky and colored pallid salmon buff to occasionally yellow, upper branches stocky and pallid salmon buff to occasionally yellow, the flesh of the branches bright salmon, the flesh of the dried stem yellow, fruiting in fall and sometimes spring, and average spore length about 10 microns. Var. pallida has its lower branches elongate and cream-colored, the upper branches elongate and cream-colored, the flesh of the branches pallid pinkish, the flesh of the dried stem white, fruiting in spring, and average spore length over 11 microns, and var. verna has the lower branches stocky and yellow, the upper branches stocky and salmon-colored, the flesh of the branches bright salmon, the flesh of the dried stem white, fruiting in spring, and average spore length over 11 microns, (Petersen). RANGE Collections of Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata were examined from ID, (Petersen). A collection of this species (variety unspecified) from OR is at Oregon State University. Another collection of this species (variety unspecified) from BC is at the University of British Columbia. 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh weakly amyloid; ferric sulphate in water green reaction, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), stem flesh amyloid (Marr), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 9cm wide, 11cm high, usually smaller, obpyriform [reverse pear-shaped] to nearly circular in outline^, large to massive stem, major branches 2-4, short, hardly round in cross-section, branches in 4-6 ranks, short, hardly round in cross-section, compact, internodes all short, diminishing gradually upward to abruptly when mature, axils acute to rounded, tips crowded, pluridentate at all ages, (Petersen), 3-12cm wide, 5-13cm high, small to medium size, (Marr)
FLESH  in stem solid, homogeneous to minutely mottled, white, moist but not slippery or gelatinous, drying yellow ("buff yellow") over extensive areas; in branches pastel salmon to salmon ("light ochraceous salmon", "capucine buff")^, (Petersen), when fresh cartilaginous-fibrous, brittle, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  major branches pale pastel salmon, branches pallid salmon buff ("light ochraceous salmon", "pinkish buff", "pale ochraceous salmon", paler than "light ochraceous salmon"), to occasionally yellow ("buff yellow", "apricot yellow", "light orange yellow"); tips light yellow to yellow ("buff yellow", "apricot yellow", "baryta yellow", "light cadmium"), darkening when old^, (Petersen), in young specimens rich maize yellow, reflecting a light orange to pinkish color in the lower branches due to the underlying salmon-colored flesh, (Marr)
STEM  up to 6cm long and up to 5cm wide, large to massive, single; 'off-white'; "minutely pruinose in some areas, the pruina easily rubbed off and then weakly brunnescent"; with common clusters of cauliflower-like abortive branchlets that are white where protected^, (Petersen), 2.5-5 x 1-6cm, broadly conic to subcylindric, with a white basal tomentum, (Marr), base white, weakly bruising reddish brown, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  negligible (Petersen), slightly sweet (Marr)
TASTE  negligible^ (Petersen), not distinctive (Marr)
EDIBILITY  uncertain (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) or Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), October to November, (Marr), June as well as fall^, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'golden yellow'^ (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8.6-11.2 x 4.0-4.7 microns, average 9.99-4.31 microns, elliptic, obscurely roughened in profile, ornamentation of low small warts and short meandering ridges, contents dense, oily, often with 1-several amorphous, dark droplets, occasionally with a single, dark, well-defined, subrefringent droplet, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus gradual, not prominent^; basidia 4-spored, 70-80 x 8-9 microns, clavate, clamped, contents with many droplets when mature, sterigmata spindly, sublyriform [somewhat lyre-like]; clamp connections present, (Petersen), spores 9-12 x 3.5-5 microns, average 10.4 x 4 microns, elliptic, ornamented with distinct but small cyanophilic warts in subspirals; basidia 2-4-spored, mostly 4-spored, 40-75 x 8-11 microns, clavate, basally clamped, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Ramaria leptoformosa might possibly be mistaken for this species, but the stem flesh is not amyloid, (Marr). Ramaria flavigelatinosa var. carnisalmonea is similar in color, but lacks clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria flavobrunnescens var. aromatica differs in form, odor, and spore morphology, (Marr). Ramaria formosa has a different stem form, stem flesh that is not amyloid, and basidia with cyanophilic granular protoplasm, (Marr). Ramaria amyloidea, Ramaria rasilispora, and Ramaria maculatipes are three other species with clamped hyphae that have amyloid stem flesh, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria rubricarnata var. pallida and Ramaria rubricarnata var. verna.
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*, Kornerup(2)

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubricarnata  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. verna  R.H. Petersen & Scates^  Mycotaxon 33: 135. 1988
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Ramaria rubricarnata var. verna differs from the type variety in 1) having the lower branches stocky and yellow, 2) having the flesh of the dried stem white, 3) fruiting in spring only, and 4) having spores with average spore length over 11 microns.^ RANGE Collections were examined from ID and CA, (Petersen).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh weakly amyloid; ferric sulphate in water green reaction, probably with branch sections, (Petersen), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 7cm wide and 9cm high, obpyriform [reverse pear-shaped] to nearly circular in outline, stem large^, major branches 2-4, hardly round in cross-section, short, branches in 3-5 ranks, round in cross-section, "internodes all short, diminishing upward gradually in adolescent specimens", "axils acute to narrowly rounded", (Petersen) 
FLESH  in stem, solid, homogeneous, white, drying white; in branches, usually brighter salmon than branch surface or salmon-orange ("light salmon orange", "orange pink", "salmon buff", "mikado orange")^, (Petersen)
BRANCH COLOR  lower branches light yellow ("baryta yellow", "maize yellow"), upper branches salmon-orange to light salmon ("light ochraceous salmon", "light ochraceous buff", "ochraceous salmon", "salmon color", "capucine orange"), vinescent ("light vinaceous fawn") around soil particles; tips yellow to rich yellow ("buff yellow", "light orange yellow", "apricot yellow", "light cadmium"), quickly fading to the color of the branches^, (Petersen), salmon orange to light salmon in upper part, light yellow at ground level (yellow "belly-band"), (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  up to 4cm long and up to 3cm wide, single, large; white, except upper stem colored light yellow like lower branches; "minutely pruinose in some areas, the pruina easily rubbed off, and there very weakly watery brunnescent"; with common clusters of cauliflower-like abortive branchlets that are white where protected^, (Petersen)
ODOR  negligible to weakly fabaceous [bean-like]^ (Petersen)
TASTE  negligible^ (Petersen)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  spring^ (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10.4-12.2 x 4.0-5.0 microns, average 11.09 x 4.35 microns, elliptic, delicately but distinctly roughened in profile, ornamentation of very delicate, small, low warts and/or short meandering ridges, contents with 1-3 well-defined, subrefringent droplets, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus gradual, not prominent^; basidia 4-spored, 70-80 x 8-9 microns, clavate, clamped, contents multiguttulate at maturity, strongly cyanophilic, sterigmata spindly, sublyriform [somewhat lyre-like]; clamp connections present, (Petersen)
NAME ORIGIN  'verna' means 'pertaining to spring'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubricarnata var. rubricarnata is differentiated from the other varieties as follows: var. rubricarnata has lower branches stocky and colored pallid salmon buff to occasionally yellow, upper branches stocky and pallid salmon buff to occasionally yellow, the flesh of the branches bright salmon, the flesh of the dried stem yellow, fruiting in fall and sometimes spring, and average spore length about 10 microns, var. pallida has lower branches elongate and cream-colored, upper branches elongate and cream-colored, the flesh of the branches pallid pinkish, the flesh of the dried stem white, fruiting in spring, and average spore length over 11 microns, and var. verna has lower branches stocky and yellow, upper branches stocky and salmon-colored, the flesh of the branches bright salmon, the flesh of the dried stem white, fruiting in spring, and average spore length over 11 microns, (Petersen).

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubrievanescens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 41. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  pale pinky
NOTES  Features of Ramaria rubrievanescens include 1) white to cream color or pinkish beige except for primordial branch tips that are pink, the pink color disappearing with maturation or within a day of picking, 2) massive white stem discoloring yellowish and bruising maroon, 3) fall fruiting, 4) cyanophilic ornamentation of conspicuous striae [stripes], and 5) clamp connections.^ Note that some Ramaria rubrievanescens specimens including the spring ones were later classified by Petersen(3) as Ramaria rubripermanens, (see SIMILAR section for Petersen's criteria). RANGE Collections were examined from WA, (Marr, September, October). Distribution includes WA, Oregon, California, and eastern North America, (Castellano, who includes spring collections). There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh amyloid, but "reaction may be slow (?)"^, (Marr), stem flesh weakly amyloid, (Scates-Barnhart), stem flesh strongly dextrinoid (see note under Ramaria rubripermanens for details on the color of the reaction in this group), (Petersen(3)), IKI positive slowly, green-purple, branch sections in ferric sulphate in water green but reaction not intense, (Petersen(12)), ferric sulphate in water on stem context negative, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  6.5-8.5cm wide, 7-8cm high, nearly as wide as tall, massive stem^, branch systems crowded, vertically compressed on the stem and curving inwards, about 1-4cm long, branching 2-5 times, lower branches usually very short and wide, 2-4cm wide, connation [joining] frequent in lower part, upper branches mostly 0.1-0.4cm wide, axils U-shaped, slightly divergent, bifurcate to pluridigitate near tips, tips obtuse, rounded, or decidedly blunt, (Marr), 7-22cm wide, 7-22cm high, often remaining cauliflower-like for a long time, (Scates-Barnhart), up to 6cm wide and 9cm high, obpyriform in outline, major branches 3 or 4, stout, ascending but not erect, more or less round in cross-section, branches in 4-6 ranks, round in cross-section, axils rounded to lunate, internodes long in lower part, diminishing rather abruptly in upper part, tips minutely digitate when young, when old knobby, (Petersen(12))
FLESH  punky firm when fresh; white^, (Marr), dry, solid, soft; white, reddish under surface stains, (Petersen(12))
BRANCH COLOR  primordial branches flushed with 'shell pink', pink color lost with maturation and soon after collecting, mature branches 'yellowish white'; unless specimens are collected when immature, fruitbodies "are apt to be described as white or cream-colored"^, (Marr), peachy pink to pale salmon (light orange) or a shade browner, soon losing color, then seeming white overall, usually not bruising but occasionally turning winy in damaged areas, (Scates-Barnhart), fleshy beige, tips buff pink to pallid rose when young, (Petersen(12))
STEM  3.5-9cm x 2-4.5cm, single, cylindric or conic, massive; milk-white discoloring yellowish, bruising 'brownish violet'; with occasional small abortive or primordial branches^, (Marr), single, proportionately massive, usually 1/2 to 2/3 of total height but not so broad as tall, usually cylindric with rounded base, (Scates-Barnhart), "single, stout to massive, rounded below"; "off-white where protected, with limited stains to maroon"; smooth; apparently without aborted branchlets, (Petersen(12))
ODOR  faintly sweet (Marr), negligible (Petersen(12))
TASTE  slightly similar to nuts (Marr), faintly bitter, acrid [peppery] on tongue, (Petersen(12))
EDIBILITY  yes, choice, (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, in forest of Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), September and October, (Marr), on ground in conifer forests, spring or fall, (Scates-Barnhart), fruits in humus or soil and matures above ground, associated with Pinaceae spp., June, September, October, (Castellano), but Petersen(3) reclassifies spring specimens (see SIMILAR)
SPORE DEPOSIT  'light yellow'^, (Marr), "light salmon orange", (Petersen(12))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-13 x 4-5.5 microns, average 11.7 x 4.9 microns, mummy-shaped, ventral surface curving sharply to the apiculus, cyanophilic ornamentation of conspicuous striae^; basidia mostly 4-spored, occasionally 2-spored, 55-87 x 8-11 microns, clavate, clamped, containing strongly cyanophilic globules; clamp connections present, (Marr), spores 10-13 x 3.5-5.5 microns, average 11.77 x 4.41 microns, (Scates-Barnhart), spores 10.4-13.7 x 4.0-4.7 microns, average 11.77 x 4.41 microns, mummy-shaped, ornamentation of delicate closely spaced striae in an abaxial-distal to adaxial-proximal orientation, contents with one to several dark, lobed inclusions, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus gradual, with no throat; basidia 4-spored, 55-65 x 7-8 microns, clavate, clamped, contents homogeneous; clamp connections present, (Petersen(12))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'transiently red'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubripermanens 1) is rather variable in color (with tips varying from pallid or pale pink through shades of dull red to dull fleshy maroon), 2) does not stain on stem, and 3) fruits in spring and fall, while R. rubrievanescens 1) is always pink to pallid pink over tips at first, 2) always shows rufescent stem surface, and 3) fruits only in fall, (Petersen(3)). R. rubripermanens has branch tips that retain their color, whereas those of R. rubrievanescens fade rapidly, (Trudell). Ramaria botrytis var. botrytis may also have reddish terminal branches, stout form, and striate spores, but the spores are longer, 11-17 x 4-6 microns, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria velocimutans.
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors from Kornerup(2)), Petersen(12), Petersen(3), Scates-Barnhart(1), Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Castellano(1)*, Exeter(3)*
84
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria rubripermanens  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 43. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  perma pinky
NOTES  Features of Ramaria rubripermanens include 1) massive stem colored white to off-white and not staining, 2) major branches off-white to pale beige, branches white when young, ivory to fleshy cinnamon by maturity, 3) tips varying from pallid or pale pink through shades of dull red to dull fleshy maroon, 4) fruiting in spring and fall, 5) spores with cyanophilic ornamentation of striae [stripes], and 6) clamp connections.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA, OR, and ID, (Petersen). It also occurs in CA (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh amyloid, reaction slow, frequently requiring 30 minutes or more, (Marr), stem flesh strongly dextrinoid, (violet-brown to dark purple-brown, with speed and intensity variable) in bowl-shaped pattern when sectioned vertically - transverse streaks from bases of major branches to stem base, high in the periphery, progressing inwardly downward and across the entire diameter at the base; green reaction to ferric sulphate in water, probably branch sections, (Petersen), ferric sulphate in water negative on stem flesh (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm wide and 20cm high, spheropedunculate, circular to broadly elliptic in outline, stem moderate to massive^, major branches two to several, ascending but not erect, short and crowded when young, open when mature, branches in 3-6 ranks, hardly round in cross-section, crowded and very short when young, open, long and lax at maturity, axils rounded, often split when old, internodes lengthening acropetally, diminishing gradually at maturity, apices crowded, spinulose, expanding to pluridentate when mature; in juvenile stage, branches are so short and tips so stiff that the fruitbodies look spiny, this stage apparently persists for a considerable time because only very mature fruitbodies are found with elongated branches, (Petersen), 9-16cm wide, 9-13cm high, (Marr)
FLESH  in stem flesh firm and hard when young, sometimes watery but not slippery or gelatinous; white, (Petersen), punky-firm when fresh, in stem white, in branches 'light yellow' (Marr, color from Kornerup(2))
BRANCH COLOR  major branches off-white to pale beige, branches white when young, ivory to fleshy cinnamon by maturity, tips from pale pink or pallid pink to buffy red or blood red, especially where protected or when young, fading significantly over time to dull rosy pink, color persisting after picking^, (Petersen)
STEM  up to 12cm long and 9cm wide, moderate, large, or massive; white to off-white, not rubescent; with clusters of abortive branchlets high on the stem that are colored as the young tips at all ages^, (Petersen), 3-8cm long and 4cm wide, (Marr)
ODOR  very weakly fragrant, often strong of fenugreek when dried^, (Petersen), musty sweet (Marr)
TASTE  negligible when fresh or very weak of vegetables; when fried in the juvenile stage, a fine, mildly nutty flavor^ (Petersen), not distinctive, (Marr)
EDIBILITY  edible and choice, especially in juvenile stage, where branches are so short and tips so stiff that the fruitbodies look spiny, (Petersen)
HABITAT  terrestrial, collected under white pine, June, September, October, (Marr), May, June, (Petersen)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10.4-15.5 x 4.0-5.0 microns, sometimes up to 17 microns or more long, average 12.17 x 4.72 microns, elliptic to subboletoid, very subtly undulate in profile, cyanophilic ornamentation of prominent striae in a generally distal-abaxial to proximal adaxial direction, (often in two directions: distally longitudinal, proximally diagonal, a common arrangement in subgenus Ramaria which includes the stripe-spored species), contents one to several droplets, the droplets dark yellow, refringent, wall up to 0.2 microns thick, apiculus not prominent, subpapillate^; basidia 4-spored, 40-80 x 8-11 microns, clavate, clamped, contents with many minute droplets; clamp connections present, (Petersen), spores 8-13 x 3.5-4.5 microns, average 10.3 x 3.8 microns, subelliptic to mummy-shaped, ornamented with oblique to longitudinal striae, the striae distinctly more cyanophilic than the general wall; basidia mostly 4-spored, occasionally 2-spored, 31-62 x 7-11 microns, clavate, clamped, contents densely cyanophilic, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'permanently red'
SIMILAR  Ramaria rubrievanescens 1) is always pink to pallid pink over tips at first, 2) always shows rufescent stem surface, and 3) fruits only in fall, while R. rubripermanens 1) is rather variable in color (with tips varying from pallid or pale pink through shades of dull red to dull fleshy maroon), 2) does not stain on stem, and 3) fruits in spring and fall, (Petersen). R. rubrievanescens has branch tips that fade rapidly, whereas those of R. rubripermanens retain their color, (Trudell). Ramaria coulterae has a negative reaction of stem flesh with IKI (iodine), (Petersen(3)). R. coulterae is stringy and has a rusty root, (M. Beug, pers. comm.). Ramaria botrytis var. botrytis may also have reddish terminal branches, stout form, and striate spores, but the spores are much longer, 11-17 x 4-6 microns, (Marr). Ramaria botrytis var. botrytis fruits in fall, (Trudell). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria botrytis var. aurantiiramosa.
SOURCES  Petersen(3), Marr(1), Trudell(4)*, Castellano(1)*, Kornerup(2), Exeter(3)*
85
FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria sandaracina  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. chondrobasis  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 116. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  fused orange clump
NOTES  Ramaria sandaracina var. chondrobasis differs from var. sandaracina principally in being more gelatinous in consistency and having a larger, broader fruitbody without a distinct single or subfasciculate smooth base.^ RANGE Collections were examined from WA, (Marr). Desjardin(6) illustrates it from CA. There are collections of var. chondrobasis from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  similar to chemical reactions of var. sandaracina^, (Marr), flesh of stem inamyloid; flesh of stem negative with ferric sulphate in water, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  6-13cm wide, 8-15cm high, branching from a connate [joined together] subgelatinous basal mass^, commonly producing small light-colored primordial branches; fruitbody consists of numerous branches compactly arranged in a generally broad form, upper nodes often dichotomous, internodes of mature specimens elongated, maximum about 4cm long, axils acute and branches slightly divergent, mostly slender 0.1-0.7cm wide, mostly forked near tips, tips subacute, (Marr)
FLESH  subgelatinous, especially so in base^, (Marr), colored much like the surface; stem base subgelatinous, gelatinous streaks present when cut, (Scates-Barnhart)
BRANCH COLOR  colored as var. sandaracina^, (Marr), tips and upper branches salmon to deep orange, fruitbody occasionally with dull violet bruised areas, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  branching "from a connate, subgelatinous basal mass, commonly producing small, light-colored primordial branches"^, (Marr), base white to pale yellow; just above ground level a band of light to bright yellow, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  mild^ (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  none or slight bean-like^ (Scates-Barnhart)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  October and November^, (Marr), on ground under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 3.5-5 microns, average 8.3 x 4 microns, hyphae of the stem and sometimes in the branches definitely gelatinizing; other microscopic characters similar to var. sandaracina^; clamp connections present, (Marr)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia var. gelatiniaurantia is very similar in appearance but lacks clamp connections, (Marr). Ramaria largentii is also very similar in appearance, and has clamp connections, but has spores that are 11-15 x 3.5-5 microns, average 13.4 x 4.5 microns, subcylindric, and ornamented with conspicuous, irregularly shaped, cyanophilic warts in subspirals, (Marr). See also SIMILAR section of Ramaria gelatinosa var. oregonensis.
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria sandaracina  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. euosma  Marr & D.E. Stuntz^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 117. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  sweet orange clump
NOTES  Ramaria sandaracina var. euosma differs from var. sandaracina by having yellow branch tips, broader more profusely branched fruitbodies, sweet odor, and fewer and narrower gloeoplerous hyphae.^ RANGE The type is from WA.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  similar to reactions of var. sandaracina (apart from reacting to phenol)^ (Marr), flesh of stem inamyloid; flesh of stem negative with ferric sulphate in water, (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  9cm wide and 8cm high, broad, base compound^, basal branches up to 2cm wide, more slender and slightly flattened in upper part, mostly 0.1-0.5cm wide, polychotomous or dichotomous, axils narrowly U-shaped, internodes moderately elongated, about 2cm long or less, multifid near tips, tips acute, (Marr)
FLESH  when fresh subgelatinous and rubbery; translucent, subconcolorous [colored more or less as surface]^, (Marr)
BRANCH COLOR  lower branches basally 'light yellow', shading upwards into 'pale orange', branches bruising 'dull violet', tips 'light yellow'^, (Marr), tips light yellow, branches pale orange with a yellow band at junction with base, bruising dull violet, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  underground part of fresh fruiting body white, (Marr), yellow band at junction with base, base white, (Scates-Barnhart)
ODOR  sweet^ (Marr), sweetly fragrant, (Scates-Barnhart)
TASTE  not distinctive, (Marr)
EDIBILITY  probably edible, (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, growing under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock), October^, (Marr), on ground under Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir) and Tsuga heterophylla, (Scates-Barnhart)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 3.5-4 microns, average 8.6 x 3.8 microns, subcylindric with a slight subhilar depression and dorsal convexity, ornamented with small cyanophilic warts^; hymenium and subhymenium combined about 70 microns thick; hyphae of tomentum 3-3.5 microns wide; hyphae of context compactly interwoven in stem, parallel in the branches, cells non-inflated to highly inflated, 7-20 microns wide, walls smooth, gelatinizing in the stem, smooth to fluted in the branches, moderately cyanophilous, thin to moderately thick-walled, 0.25-1.5 microns, vesiculation of hyphal cells near septa rare, about 12 microns diameter, walls nearly smooth; clamp connections abundant, often of the closed type; gloeoplerous hyphae rare, slender, mostly 2.5-3 microns, (Marr), spores 6.5-9 x 3.5-4.5 microns, (Scates-Barnhart)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with agreeable odor'
SIMILAR  Ramaria leptoformosa might possibly be mistaken for this taxon, but it has larger spores and non-gelatinous consistency, (Marr). Ramaria gelatiniaurantia var. violeitingens is somewhat similar but lacks clamp connections, (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*

FAMILY  Gomphaceae, Order Gomphales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Ramaria sandaracina  Marr & D.E. Stuntz  var. sandaracina^  Biblioth. Mycol. 38: 114. 1973
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Ramaria sandaracina var. sandaracina include 1) single or somewhat bundled stem, 2) underground part of fruitbody whitish, brighter yellow near emergence from substrate, 3) deep orange to light orange branches and branch tips, sometimes with faint small spots of grayish violet bruising, 4) brittle fleshy-fibrous flesh sometimes with a small area in stem flesh slightly gelatinous, 5) slightly bean-like or mild odor, 6) relatively small spores with numerous cyanophilic papillate warts, and 7) clamp connections.^ The description is derived from Marr(1) except where noted. RANGE Collections were examined from WA and CA (Marr). There is a collection of var. sandaracina from BC at the University of British Columbia. There are collections from OR (variety unspecified) at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  stem flesh inamyloid, (Marr), stem flesh negative with ferric sulphate in water (Exeter)
FRUITING BODY  3-7cm wide, 5-10cm high, mostly small to medium size, slender, stem single or subfasciculate [somewhat bundled]^, branching up to 6 times from stem, nodes polychotomous or mostly dichotomous in upper part, axils acute and branches nearly parallel, internodes elongated especially in the middle and lower branches, 3.5cm about the maximum length, branches slender, most 0.2-1.5cm wide, forked to finely divided near the tips, tips acute or subacute
FLESH  brittle fleshy-fibrous, sometimes a small area in stem slightly gelatinous; colored more or less as surface
BRANCH COLOR  branches and tips 'deep orange', sometimes faint small bruised spots of grayish violet present; fruitbodies also referred to as deep orange to light orange, (Marr), branches and tips bright orange, a yellow area around upper base and lower branches, (Scates-Barnhart)
STEM  0.5-4cm x 0.5-2cm, single or subfasciculate, conic; underground part of fruitbody white to 'pale yellow', brighter yellow near emergence from substrate; stem surface smooth, lower regions covered with a thin white tomentum
ODOR  slightly fabaceous [bean-like] or not distinctive
TASTE  not distinctive
EDIBILITY  probably edible (Scates-Barnhart)
HABITAT  terrestrial, under Tsuga heterophylla (Western Hemlock) and Pseudotsuga menziesii (Douglas-fir), October and November
SPORE DEPOSIT  between 'grayish yellow and grayish orange'
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.5-9 x 3.5-4.5 microns, average 8.1 x 3.9 microns, subcylindric to elliptic, ornamented with numerous papillate, cyanophilic warts^; basidia 1-4-spored, 40-70 x 6-11 microns, clavate, basally clamped; hymenium about 60 microns thick and subhymenium 25 microns thick, subhymenial hyphae 2-4 microns wide, loosely interwoven, clamp connections frequently of the keyhole type, thin-walled; context hyphae - "outer parallel hyphae of the stipe collapsing into a densely stratified layer, context becoming more loosely organized inward, hyphae agglutinated in places, context subparallel in the branches, slightly to highly inflated, 4-18 microns diam, walls mostly smooth and glassy, sometimes slightly fluted, moderately cyanophilous, thin-walled, ampulliform inflations near septa 7-15 microns diam, walls nearly smooth in the branches, distinctly ornamented in the stipe; clamps abundant, often of the keyhole type, the clamp cell occasionally enlarged; gloeoplerous hyphae especially abundant in the stipe", localized bulbous regions enlarged up to 12 microns diam, otherwise diam 3-7 microns
NAME ORIGIN  means 'pertaining to realgar [a yellowish mineral]' or 'pertaining to beebread', from Latin 'sandaraca' derived from Greek 'sandarake'
SIMILAR  Ramaria gelatiniaurantia lacks clamp connections (Marr).
SOURCES  Marr(1) (colors in quotation marks from Kornerup(2)), Scates-Barnhart(1), Exeter(3)*
