FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora caryophyllea  Schaeff. ex Fries^  Syst. Myc. 1: 430. 1821
ENGLISH NAMES  funnel-shaped Thelephora, carnation groundwart
NOTES  Features include 1) funnel-shaped fruitbody with the upper part more horizontal, or in double rosettes, or with overlapping wedge-shaped lobes, 2) purplish brown to blackish brown or blackish purple color, 3) surface radially fibrillose to smooth, more or less zoned, with fringed margin irregularly torn or lobed, 4) spore-bearing surface underneath smooth or streaked, 5) stem absent or chestnut brown and usually central, 6) growth on soil in coniferous woods, and 7) elliptic angular-lobate spiny spores.^ RANGE Distribution includes WA, ID, NB, ON, PQ, AK, CA, CO, CT, DC, GA, IA, KY, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, NH, NY, OH, PA, and VT, (Ginns). It has been reported from BC (in Redhead(5)). There are collections from BC at the Pacific Forestry Centre and the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  small to medium, thin rosettes of variable shape: caps 1-5cm wide, simple and shallowly vase-shaped, or compound, with several overlapping concentric discs or spatula-shaped lobes; upper surface purplish brown to blackish brown or blackish purple, fading with drying and becoming somewhat zoned, "smooth or with radiating fibrils (streaks) or irregularly roughened"; margin irregularly torn or lobed; undersurface smooth or streaked^, (McKnight), 1.5-4cm high, with central stem, funnel-shaped, occasionally with lateral stem; cap 1-5cm wide, entire or more or less deeply lacerate into fan-shaped or spathulate, often imbricate [shingled] lobes, sometimes proliferating from the center and becoming imbricate or doubled; fuscous purple or purple brown, drying paler, more or less zoned; radially fibrillose or rugulose, or "merely inoderm", margin fimbriate [fringed]; spore-bearing surface inferior, decurrent, brownish or fuscous violaceous, paler to the margin, smooth, sometimes radially streaked, (Corner), up to 5cm across, often a double rosette shape, upper surface streaked, brownish radiating fibers, with smooth or lacerated margin, a bit zoned, lower surface zoned, light near margin, medium brown in the middle, purple brown near stem, slightly grooved, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), 1.5-5cm across, 1.5-5cm high, fuscous purple but drying wood brown; undersurface grayish olive to light yellowish olive, (Burt)
FLESH  coriaceous [leathery], thin; brown^, (Corner), thin; deep brown, (McKnight), 
BRANCH COLOR  
STEM  0.4-1.7 x 0.2-0.5cm, central, occasionally lateral, occasionally almost lacking; chestnut brown, drying pallid; smooth, matte or subtomentose^, (Corner), short to almost lacking, central or off-center; colored as cap, (McKnight), up to 3cm tall, (but sometimes absent), equal, cylindric, central; chestnut brown; a bit hairy and crusty, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), 1cm long, 0.2-0.3cm wide, (Burt)
ODOR  none^ (Corner)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (McKnight)
EDIBILITY  not edible (McKnight)
HABITAT  on sandy ground in coniferous woods, (Corner), single or in groups on soil in coniferous woods, summer and fall, (McKnight)
SPORE DEPOSIT  yellowish brown (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), purple-brown (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.5-8.5 x 5-7 microns, elliptic, angular, more or less lobate, echinulate [finely spiny] with spines up to 1 micron long, less often up to 1.5 microns long, rarely as blunt warts, umber purple, one to several droplets^; basidia 2-4-spored, 47-65 x 8-10 microns or 70-90 x 9-12 microns, sterigmata 7-8.5 microns long; cystidia none; hyphae 3-6.5(8) microns wide, with clamp connections, secondarily septate, developing firm, slightly thickened brown walls, hymenium generally cyanescent in KOH, the trama not, (Corner), spores 6.5-8.5 x 5-7 microns, angular-elliptic or lobed, with long spines, (McKnight)
NAME ORIGIN  from Greek meaning "with walnut-coloured ridges"^, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Thelephora regularis is paler in color, the cap is not characteristically fibrillose, and the stem is tomentose, (Corner). Similarly shaped forms of Thelephora terrestris are thicker and more coarse (McKnight).
SOURCES  Corner(4), McKnight(1)*, Burt(3), Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Redhead(5), Ginns(5), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora intybacea  Pers. ex Fr.^  Synop. Meth. Fung. 567. 1801
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Thelephora intybacea is similar to Thelephora terrestris but 1) the cap is pallid then rufous ferruginous then fuliginous, 2) the upper (inner) surface is felted, tomentose or scaly in narrow interrupted zones, and 3) the lower (outer) spore-bearing surface is papillate and wrinkled. Microscopic characters include angular-lobate spiny spores.^ RANGE Distribution includes WA, ID, ON, CT, DC, IA, MA, ME, MI, NC, NH, NY, OH, and VT, (Ginns), and  Europe, South Africa, and New Zealand, (Corner). There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  "As T. terrestris but the pileus (6cm radius) pallid, then rufous ferruginous, finally fuliginous, spongy-felted to floccose-tomentose, then inoderm or with slight adnate fibrillose squamules in narrow interrupted zones; hymenium papillate, rugulose; flesh firm, 1-3 mm. thick."^, (Corner), clusters often 5-8cm across, often central, but more often with the caps lateral and triangular, "sometimes the mass ascends small sticks and then extends out from this support in reflexed forms"; fruitbodies whitish then rufous-ferruginous, individual cap 2-3cm long, 2-4cm broad, 0.1cm thick, stems somewhat lateral and growing into one another, caps imbricate [shingled], fibrous, usually with the fibers matted and agglutinated into appressed and wholly adnate squamules [tightly attached fine scales], margin dilated and whitish-fimbriate [whitish-fringed] at first, at length becoming entire and colored like the rest of the cap; spore-bearing surface inferior, colored as the upper surface, papillose, (Burt)
FLESH  0.1-0.3cm thick, firm^, (Corner)
BRANCH COLOR  
STEM  somewhat lateral and growing into one another^ (Burt)
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  "Ground in pine woods, growing up from the layer of fallen leaves; saprobic on rotten woody material of all kinds; in mixed woods", (Ginns), generally cespitose; in pine woods, (Corner), August to October, (Burt)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-12 x 6-9 microns, angular lobate, echinulate (finely spiny) with scattered subacute spines 0.5(1) microns high, yellow brown, one droplet^; basidia 45-90 x 9-12 microns with 2-4 sterigmata 7-9 microns long; hyphae 2-7.5 microns wide, with clamp connections, becoming thick-walled; not cyanescent in KOH, (Corner)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Thelephora mollissima has a smooth spore-bearing surface (Corner). T. intybacea is distinguished from ferruginous specimens of Thelephora terrestris "by the thicker and entire margin of the pileus and by the absence of free squamules", (Burt). See also SIMILAR section of Thelephora americana.
SOURCES  Corner(4), Burt(3), Ginns(5)

The cap color is whitish becoming brownish with the cap surface subtomentose.^ The description is derived from Corner based on the Friesian interpretation of 1874. RANGE It has been identified by Jim Ginns from western Vancouver Island in BC (pers. comm.), using the concepts of Stalpers. It is also found in Europe.

MICROSCOPIC  "Spores and microscopic structure as in T. intybacea Fr."

FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora multipartita  Schwein.^  in Fries, Elench. fung. (Greifswald) 1: 166. 1828
ENGLISH NAMES  many-parted Thelephora
NOTES  Features of Thelephora multipartita include 1) small size, 2) tomentose sometimes branching stem that is central or lateral, 3) "cap" that is often deeply divided into branching shapes and triangular lobes with irregular margins, 4) color pinkish brown to grayish brown, paler toward margin, 5) smooth outer surface and nearly smooth inner surface, 6) fruiting on the ground under hardwoods, especially oak, or mixed woods, and 7) spores angular-lobate with spines.^ RANGE There is a 1973 Scott Redhead collection from BC at the University of British Columbia (as Thelephora regularis var. multipartita). There are collections at the University of Washington from AK and the Dominican Republic. MykoWeb displays it for CA. North American distribution is given by Ginns(5) as DC, IA, IL, NC, NJ, NY, OH, and PA. Collections were examined from IL, NJ, NY, OH, and PA, (Burt(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  up to 3cm wide, up to 5cm high, erect, coriaceous [leathery], narrowly fan-shaped or spathulate-partite, then clavarioid, branches obliquely erect from the stem, few or numerous, 0.3-1cm wide, spreading or becoming horizontal, often dividing several times into narrow spathulate branchlets, sometimes proliferating in the center, sometimes joined at the base to form a small funnel-shaped center, fuscous cinereous, drying ochraceous drab to cinnamon drab; upper side minutely pubescent, subtomentose, or subfibrillose; hymenium inferior, fawn or vinaceous drab, smooth^, (Corner), 2.5-7cm wide, 2.5-6cm high, variable, "typically forming cluster of funnel-shaped caps" that arise on stems from common base, caps "split deeply into irregular lobes; inner (upper) surface pinkish brown to grayish brown, paler toward the margin, nearly smooth; margin wavy, irregular, divided, whitish at maturity; outer (lower) surface pinkish brown to orange-brown, becoming grayish brown in age, smooth", (Bessette), 1-3cm across, (fruitbody 1.5-3.5cm high), leathery, funnel-shaped, "sometimes cleft more or less deeply and unequally into a few lobes, sometimes divided to the stem and spreading so as to appear dimidiate, very often deeply divided and subdivided into many narrow and spreading divisions more or less dilated and whitish at the apex"; upper inner surface fusco-cinereous, drying pallid, bald (minutely pubescent or fibrillose under a lens); lower outer spore-bearing surface fawn or vinaceous drab; even, bald, (Burt)
FLESH  thin, leathery; watery brown^, (Bessette)
BRANCH COLOR  
STEM  up to 2.5cm long and 0.1-0.3(0.4)cm wide, central or lateral^, (Corner), erect, nearly equal or widening downward, frequently branched; reddish brown, darkening when old; "coated with very tiny, soft hairs", (Bessette), 1-2cm x 0.1-0.3cm, "erect or incurved", equal or widening downward, "sometimes branched above; drying walnut-brown or pallid; villose", (Burt)
ODOR  none^ (Corner)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  inedible (Bessette)
HABITAT  "Soil under deciduous trees, especially oaks; on earth, upland rocky woods (mixed oak and pine), damp woods, in rich humus near base of oak"^, (Ginns), single, scattered, or in clusters "on soil in mixed woods and hardwoods, especially under oak", July to November, (Bessette)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark brown^, (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8.5(9) x 4.7-7 microns, angular, deeply lobate, irregular, echinulate rather densely with spines 0.5-1.5 microns long^, (Corner), spores 7-10 x 5-6 microns, "angularly oval to elliptic, warted, brownish", (Bessette), spores tuberculate (Burt)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with many parts'
SIMILAR  See also Thelephora regularis, of which Thelephora multipartita has at times been regarded as a variety. Burt was unable to find differences apart from the form "As yet, I know of no characters by which to assort and separate into their respective species specimens mixed together of typical T. regularis and those specimens of T. multipartita which have the pileus infundibuliform or merely cleft more or less deeply and unequally into a few lobes." "If a given collection consists wholly of specimens with pileus many-parted and subdivided into narrow divisions, or if it contains some such specimens in addition to others with more regular infundibuliform pileus, I refer the collection to T. multipartita". (Burt(3), with Latin names italicized).
SOURCES  Corner(4) (as Thelephora regularis var. multipartita), Bessette(2)* (as Thelephora regularis var. multipartita), Ginns(5) (as Thelephora regularis var. multipartita), Burt(3) (as Thelephora multipartita), Redhead(5) (as Thelephora regularis var. multipartita)

FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora palmata  Scop. ex Fr.^  Syst. Myc. 1: 432. 1821
ENGLISH NAMES  fetid false coral, stinking earthfan
NOTES  Features of Thelephora palmata include 1) multiple branching from a common base, 2) more or less erect, flattened, tough branches that are purplish brown to chocolate brown or darker, the tips whitish when actively growing, 3) short stem, 4) garlic to unpleasant-fetid odor, 5) growth under trees and along paths, and 6) elliptic-angular spiny spores.^ RANGE Distribution includes WA, PE, PQ, CA, CO, CT, DC, DE, IA, IL, MO, NC, NH, NJ, NY, OH, and PA, (Ginns), OR (Zeller), and BC (in Redhead). There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia. It is widespread and fairly common in North America, Europe, and Asia, (Trudell).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  trama is deeply cyanescent in KOH, the hymenium not cyanescent in KOH (Corner(4))
FRUITING BODY  up to 10cm or more wide, 2-10cm high, usually profusely branched from common base, flattened branches, tips also flattened (palm-like)^, (Arora), in tufts up to 5cm wide, up to 10cm high, clavarioid, cespitose, with flattened, palmatifid, multifid, or dichotomous branching, branches very numerous, dilating 0.4-1.2cm, wide in the lower axils, subfastigiate [somewhat bundled], often connate [joined by growth], becoming ligulate [strap-like] or cylindric 0.1-0.2cm wide; spore-bearing surface amphigenous, surface smooth, (Corner)
FLESH  tough, leathery^, (Arora), fibrous, tough, (Corner), tough, leathery; brownish, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
BRANCH COLOR  purplish brown to chocolate brown or darker, tips usually paler (whitish) when still actively growing^, (Arora), white when young, then fuscous purple, chocolate brown to blackish brown, often with a violet tinge, (Corner)
STEM  'present only as a common base or short "trunk" below the branches'^, (Arora), up to 1.5cm long and 0.4cm wide, irregular, soon dividing, (Corner), carrot-shaped (narrowing downward), sometimes very short but can be up to 6cm long and up to 3cm wide, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
ODOR  garlic-like, becoming fetid (unpleasant) when old^, (Arora), strong, fetid, particularly on drying, commonly without odor when fresh, (Corner), fetid, of rotting cabbage or garlic, disappears when drying, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  unknown (Phillips), inedible (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
HABITAT  moist ground in coniferous woods, also in grassy fields^, (Ginns), single or in groups on moist ground in woods and at their edges, often along woodland paths, late fall, winter, or spring, (Arora, for California), on the ground in coniferous woods, (Corner), August to November, later in California, (Phillips)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark reddish brown^, (Arora), purple-brown (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-11 x 7-8 microns, elliptic-angular, spiny^, (Arora), spores 8-12 x 7-9 microns, angular-lobate, echinulate [finely spiny] with spines 0.5-1.5 microns long, fuscous purple, 1-2 droplets; basidia 2-4-spored, 70-100 x 9-12 microns, sterigmata 7-12 microns long; hymenium amphigenous, often sterile on the upper side of oblique branches; cystidia none; hyphae 3-9 microns wide, with clamp connections, thin-walled in the branches, becoming thick-walled (up to 1 micron) in the stem, colorless or pale brown, often secondarily septate; trama deeply cyanescent in KOH, the hymenium not cyanescent, (Corner) 
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "palmfrond" or "broom", (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Thelephora terrestris is more frilly and flower-like and has very little odor (Trudell). Thelephora anthocephala 1) has a mild odor in the type variety (but may be foetid in var. americana), 2) its trama is not cyanescent in KOH, and 3) there are morphological features that are difficult to describe (see Bessette(2) p.425 and less directly Corner(4)). See also SIMILAR section of Thelephora caespitulans.
SOURCES  Corner(4), Arora(1)*, Phillips(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Burt(3), Redhead(5), Zeller(2), Ginns(5), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*, Bessette(2)

FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora penicillata  Fr.^  Syst. Mycol. 1: 434. 1821
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  T. penicillata var. penicillata grows as a flat crust on bare earth and leaves, twigs, grasses etc., and forms somewhat erect or decumbent, awl-shaped, strap-like, or spathulate branches. Although its form qualifies as somewhat coral-like, the only variety from the Pacific Northwest is var. byssoideofimbriata Bourd. & Galzin, (with one Idaho collection), which is wholly resupinate [flat on substrate], with the spore-bearing part smooth and chocolate-colored, and margin white, cottony, or in places spiculose.^ Forma subfimbriata has more pronounced branches than the type, forming clavarioid tufts up to 3cm high. RANGE North American distribution includes IA, MI, NJ, OH, and WI, (var. penicillata), ID (var. byssoideofimbriata), and PQ, IA, IL, IN, MA, NC, NJ, NY, PA, and SC, (forma subfimbriata), (Ginns). There is a collection from BC at the University of British Columbia. T. penicillata is also found in Europe, South Africa, and Australia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  resupinate-encrusting, up to 15cm wide, at first as a villous white membrane, forming suberect or decumbent, subulate [awl-shaped], ligulate [strap-like], or spathulate branches becoming cristate [crested] or penicillate [like a brush], up to 2.5cm by 0.1-0.7cm; hymenium covering whole surface or just underside of branches, fuscous purple, even or subrugulose [somewhat finely wrinkled]; margin ramoso-spiculose [branched with little spikes] or fimbriate [fringed], white; var. byssoideofimbriata wholly resupinate, more or less adnate [firmly attached], hymenium chocolate, smooth, margin white and byssoid (or in places spiculose)^, (Corner)
FLESH  0.1-0.15cm thick, floccose^, (Corner)
BRANCH COLOR  
STEM  
ODOR  
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  on bare earth and encrusting leaves, twigs, grasses etc., under hardwoods or conifers, (Corner), leaves, including partly decayed Fagus leaves in moist groves, (for var. penicillata), on Pinus contorta var. latifolia (for var. byssoideofimbriata), moist places, encrusting small twigs and moss; encrusting herbaceous plants; encrusting stems, (for f. subfimbriata), (Ginns), fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  purple-brown^ (Buczacki)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 5-7(8) microns, angular-lobate or scarcely lobate, echinulate with spines up to 1.5 microns long, often laxly, umber brown^; basidia 30-75 x 7-11 microns, cystidia none; hyphae 3-7.5 microns wide, in places up to 15 microns, with clamp connections, the walls 0.5-1.5 microns thick, secondarily septate, often aggregated into fibrils, pale or colorless; not cyanescent in KOH but the fibrils of the flesh turning dark brown, (Corner)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'penicillate' or 'like a small brush'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Corner(4), Ginns(5), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora regularis  Schwein.^  Schr. naturf. Ges. Leipzig 1: 105. 1822
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features of Thelephora regularis include 1) small size, 2) tomentose stem that is central or lateral, 3) "cap" that is funnel-shaped or triangular-lobed or fan-shaped, 4) color pinkish brown on upper inner surface, the young parts dark wine-red on bruising, 5) lower (spore-bearing) surface grayish brown to purplish brown, 6) growth on the ground, and 7) spores angular-lobate with spines.^ The status of Thelephora regularis var. regularis in the Pacific Northwest is unclear, because var. multipartita has been elevated to species level. RANGE Thelephora regularis was reported in Redhead for British Columbia without specifying the variety, but there is a 1973 Redhead collection at the University of British Columbia specified as var. multipartita. An M.C. Melburn collection from British Columbia, determined by L.K. Weresub, is deposited at the Pacific Forestry Centre (variety unspecified). There are R. Bandoni and O. Ceska collections from British Columbia at University of British Columbia (variety unspecified - good photographs of the latter collection are on www.mushroomobserver.org). North American distribution is given by Ginns(5) as ON, QC, AL, DE, IA, IL, KS, MA, ME, MO, NC, NH, PA, SC, and WI, (for var. regularis). Collections were examined from ON, AL, DE, IA, IL, KS, MA, ME, MO, NC, NH, PA, SC, and WI, (Burt(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  up to 2.5cm wide, 3cm high, with central or lateral stem, entire and funnel-shaped or divided into a few broadly wedge-shaped lobes 0.5-1.5cm wide, sometimes merely fan-shaped, the limb becoming horizontal; upper side of cap buffy flesh color, drying pale fawn ochraceous to alutaceous, young parts dark wine red on bruising, finely appressedly fibrillose toward the margin, matte or subtomentose in center, margin lacerate; spore-bearing surface inferior, grayish vinaceous or purplish fawn; smooth^, (Corner for var. regularis), 0.5-2.5cm across, (fruitbody 0.6-2.5cm high), leathery, funnel-shaped "or divided to the stem into triangular divisions" or fan-shaped; upper inner surface "drying pallid or tawny-olive, darker at center of the cup or at base of the divisions"; fibrillose, margin lacerate; lower outer (spore-bearing) surface usually hair-brown, sometimes pallid, (Burt)
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  
STEM  up to 1.5cm long and 0.1-0.15cm wide, subvillous [somewhat downy] to finely tomentose^, (Corner for var. regularis), 0.3-1.5cm x 0.1-0.15cm (Burt)
ODOR  none (Corner for both varieties)
TASTE  bitter, harsh (Corner for var. regularis)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or gregarious, in moss in wet places and in humus, (Corner for var. regularis), "in moss in wet places and in humus; damp mossy earth, damp sandy soil; soil among mosses in moist places", (Ginns(5) for var. regularis)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8 x 4.5-6.5 microns, angular-lobate, echinulate [finely spiny] with spines 0.5-1.5 microns long^; hyphae 2.5-4.5 microns wide, with clamp connections, the walls up to 1 micron thick, not cyanescent in KOH or the hymenium very slightly, (Corner for var. regularis), spores angular-tuberculate (Burt for var. regularis)
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Thelephora caryophyllea is darker, with a characteristically fibrillose cap, and lacks the tomentose stem or is at most subtomentose, (Corner). "Specimens of T. regularis which have the pileus infundibuliform and little cleft are suggestive of small specimens of T. caryophyllea but differ from the latter by the thicker pileus and paler coloration of T. multipartita [sic] which is wholly lacking in the rufescent coloration of T. caryophyllea." (Burt(3) with Latin names italicized). See also Thelephora multipartita which has at times been regarded as a variety of T. regularis. Burt was unable to find differences apart from the form "As yet, I know of no characters by which to assort and separate into their respective species specimens mixed together of typical T. regularis and those specimens of T. multipartita which have the pileus infundibuliform or merely cleft more or less deeply and unequally into a few lobes." "If a given collection consists wholly of specimens with pileus many-parted and subdivided into narrow divisions, or if it contains some such specimens in addition to others with more regular infundibuliform pileus, I refer the collection to T. multipartita". (Burt(3) with Latin names italicized).
SOURCES  Corner(4), Bessette(2)*, Burt(3), Redhead(5), Ginns(5)
113
FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora scissilis  Burt^  Ann. Missouri Bot. Gard. 1: 204. 1914
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include small size, and a slender, erect fruitbody, with slightly spreading brownish ash-gray branches arising from a chestnut base, the lower branches with appressed spicules.^ RANGE Distribution includes WA and MI (Ginns).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  0.2-0.6cm wide, 1.5-2cm high (India specimen referred here up to 5cm high), clavarioid, branches erect, longitudinally rugulose from the bases of many small appressed spicular processes, slightly fan-shaped on branching^, (Corner), spread of branches at top 0.2-0.6cm, fruitbody 1.5-2cm high, erect, clavariform, branched, coriaceous [leathery], "longitudinally ridged by the bases of numerous, small, appressed, acicular branches", the larger of which are at the top of the fruitbody and "spread slightly outward in fan-shaped manner"
FLESH  
BRANCH COLOR  avellaneo-cinereous [brownish ash-gray], with many small appressed spicules, finely longitudinally wrinkled from the bases of these, (in the type collection), fuscous-vinaceous with pallid white tips, (India and Borneo specimens referred here)^, (Corner), avellaneo-cinereous (Burt)
STEM  castaneous [chestnut], smooth or (in India and Borneo specimens referred here) with spicules^, (Corner), 0.7-1cm long and 0.1cm wide, bald, castaneous, (Burt)
ODOR  none in India and Borneo specimens referred here
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  gregarious on ground in woods^, (Corner), gregarious on ground, Washington, January, (Burt)
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-8 x 5-6 microns, angular, pale umbrinous^, basidia 4-spored, (type), spores 7-8.5 x 5.5-7 microns, angular-lobate, echinulate with spines 0.5-1.0 microns long, basidia 4-spored, cystidia none, hyphae 2-4 microns, clamped, with pale slightly thickened walls, not cyanescent in KOH, (India and Borneo specimens referred here); hymenium amphigenous, (Corner), spores 6-8 x 5-6 microns, angular, pale umbrinous under microscope, basidia 4-spored, colorless, (Burt)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'scissile' [separating into layers]
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Corner(4), Burt(3), Ginns(5)
114
FAMILY  Thelephoraceae, Order Thelephorales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Thelephora terrestris  Ehrh.: Fr.^  Syst. Myc. 1: 431. 1822;  Phylacteria terrestris  (Ehrh.) Pat.
ENGLISH NAMES  common fiber vase, earthfan, groundwart, fiberfan
NOTES  Features include 1) light to dark brown fruitbodies that are vase-shaped, funnel-shaped, or in rosettes, 2) the upperside with radiating silky fibrils or small scales, margin usually fringed or splitting and paler, 3) the underside wrinkled or papillate or smooth, 4) stem absent or short and lateral to central, 5) flesh thin and tough, 6) odor mild to earthy, 7) growth often in clusters on the ground, (sometimes shelf-like on plant stems), and 8) spores sparsely spiny.^ Thelephora terrestris causes smothering disease of coniferous seedlings in nature and in nurseries, but harms only slow growing seedlings and is of benefit in its role as a mycorrhiza former, (Ginns). RANGE Distribution in North America includes BC, WA, OR, ID, AB, MB, NF, ON, PQ, AK, AL, AZ, CA, CT, IA, IN, MA, ME, MI, MN, MT, NC, ND, NH, NJ, NY, OH, PA, SC, SD, and WY, (Ginns). It widely distributed as far south as central CA and GA, (Phillips). It is also found in Jamaica, Brazil, Uruguay, Europe, South Africa, China, Japan, Australia, and New Zealand, and is the commonest Thelephora of north temperate regions, (Corner).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  usually vase-shaped to fan-like, sometimes bracket-like or shelf-like on plant stems; cap 2-5cm wide or forming rosettes or clusters up to 12cm wide, margin usually fringed, splitting; brown to reddish brown or chocolate brown to grayish brown or fuscous, often darker when old, margin often paler or whitish, underside some shade of brown; dry, with radiating silky fibrils or small scales, underside smooth or wrinkled^, (Arora), fertile undersurface clay brown or pallid, smooth or irregularly wrinkled, (Phillips), underside smooth or with radiating wrinkles at first, becoming finely and unevenly warty, (McKnight), up to 5cm across and up to 7cm high, (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), "pileate, subsessile, imbricating, dimidiate or spuriously infundibuliform from concrescence", in masses up to 12cm wide, "often in imbricating rosettes, varying effuso-reflexed and resupinate", caps up to 6cm wide, more or less ascending, unilateral, soft; upper side colored ferruginous to dark brown, blackish when old, varying to purplish or fuscous rufescent; upper surface tomentose, fibrillose, strigosofibrillose, or fibrillosely scaly; margin white, smooth, even, then fimbriate and incised; underside (the spore-bearing surface) fuscous, pale fawn, brown chocolate or rufescent umber, finely and unevenly papillose, somewhat radially rugulose, rarely smooth, (Corner)
FLESH  very thin, tough^, (Arora), about 0.1cm thick, floccose-coriaceous, fibrous, colored as surface, (Corner)
BRANCH COLOR  
STEM  when present, lateral to central, short, thin, tough, colored like cap or paler^, (Arora), none or very short (Phillips)
ODOR  mild or earthy^ (Arora), none (Corner), like moldy earth (Lincoff(2))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (McKnight)
EDIBILITY  not edible (Phillips)
HABITAT  occasionally single but more often in confluent masses or clustered or in groups, growing in humus, sandy soil, decaying vegetable matter, on old stumps, climbing up herbaceous stems or tree seedlings, (Arora), year-round, mostly July to December, (Lincoff(2))^, July to September, (Phillips), on the ground in coniferous woods, often on roots, stumps and seedlings, rarely in deciduous woods, mainly fall but at all seasons, (Corner)
SPORE DEPOSIT  purplish brown^ (Arora), purplish to purplish brown (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-12 x 6-9 microns, elliptic-angular, warted (often minutely so)^, (Arora), spores 8-12 x 6-9 microns, angular-elliptic, sparsely spiny, (Phillips), 8-12 x 6-9 microns, angularly elliptic, more or less lobate, rather sparsely echinulate with spines 0.5 microns long, varying to nearly smooth, one to several droplets; fuscous purple, violaceous ferruginous, or purple umber; basidia 40-90 x 8-12 microns, 2-4 sterigmata 6-8.5 microns long; cystidia none; hyphae 3-10(12) microns wide, with clamp connections, with distinctly thickened brownish walls 0.5-1.5 microns thick, branched at a wide angle, fibrillose-fasciculate, not cyanescent in KOH but the hyphal fibrils of the flesh turning dark brown, (Corner)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "growing on the ground"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Thelephora palmata is more branched-coralloid, and has a strong unpleasant odor. Thelephora intybacea has thicker and entire cap margin and lacks free squamules, (Burt). See also SIMILAR section of Cotylidia diaphana and Thelephora caryophyllea.
SOURCES  Corner(4), Arora(1)* (for T. terrestris group), Trudell(4)*, Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, Burt(3), Ginns(5), McKnight(1)*, Bacon(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)* (as Thelephora terrestris group)
115
FAMILY  Tremellodendropsidaceae, Order Tremellodendropsidales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Tremellodendropsis tuberosa  (Grev.) D.A. Crawford^  Trans. & Proc. Roy. Soc. New Zealand 82: 619. 1954;  Merisma tuberosum  Grev.;  Thelephora tuberosa  (Grev.) Fr.;  Aphelaria tuberosa  (Grev.) Corner;  Stereum tuberosum  (Grev.) Massee;  Clavaria gigaspora  Cotton;  Polyozus contortus  (P. Karst.) P. Karst.;  Thelephora contorta  P. Karst.
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a whitish to brownish or grayish fruitbody with flattened, tough-leathery, more or less erect, pale-tipped branches, 2) a tough stem up to half the height of fruitbody, 3) growth on the ground, and 4) microscopic characters including basidia that are partially septate longitudinally at their tips.^ RANGE There are collections from BC at the University of British Columbia. It has been reported from WA (N. Siegel and D. Miller pers. comm.), and is fairly common along the Pacific Coast according to Trudell(4). It is fairly common in coastal CA according to Arora(1). There are also reports from NC, NY, PA, Bermuda, Finland, Spain, Sweden, United Kingdom, China, and Japan, (Corner(2)), and Bolivia, Brazil, and Borneo, (Corner(3)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
FRUITING BODY  0.5-4cm across, 2-7(10)cm high, "rather sparsely branched from a tough base" or sometimes scarcely branched, the branches usually somewhat flattened (especially the lower ones), erect (upright vertical), tough^, (Arora), 6.5cm high, with flattened branching (apparently in one plane, though actually, by twisting, in several planes), the lower branches palmately divided, the upper narrow and dichotomous ("branches 3-5-chotomous, or more below, dichotomous and slightly flattened distally, numerous and bushy or rather few and lax, often irregular"); hymenium apparently covering the whole fruitbody except the tips, (Corner(2))
FLESH  tough; white, not staining^, (Arora), tough, subcoriaceous [somewhat leathery], especially in stem, (Corner(2))
BRANCH COLOR  "whitish to buff, brownish, or grayish, sometimes with a purple or pinkish tinge (caused by a parasite?)", "tips often paler and brighter (whiter) when actively growing"^, (Arora), pale buff-straw, gray or drab tinged yellow, gray tinged brown, or pale ferruginous, tips white, (Corner(2))
STEM  well developed, usually a third to a half the height of the fruitbody; "colored like branches or paler, often with a coat of mycelial down"^, (Arora), up to 2cm long and 0.13-0.32cm wide, distinct or not, often divided from the base into the main palmate branches, (Corner(2))
ODOR  none^ (Corner(2))
TASTE  none^ (Corner(2))
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or in groups on ground in woods or clearings^, (Arora), single, gregarious, or cespitose [in tufts], on the ground in the forest, "or among grass, moss, or on bare soil in the open", (Corner(2)), late summer to fall (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  white^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-20 x 4.5-6.5 microns, elongate-elliptic or spindle-shaped, smooth^; many or all of the basidia "appearing partially septate (partitioned) longitudinally at their apices", (Arora), spores (12)14-20(24) x (4)5-7(9) microns, elongate elliptic, subfusoid or subcylindric, "blunt or subacute, attenuate to the oblique apiculus", smooth, "granular-vacuolate or minutely guttulate"; basidia according to Bourdot & Galzin dimorphic, 1) 50-90 x 8-10 microns, with 2-3-4, mostly 4, normal sterigmata 7.5-10 microns long, 2) 30-60 x 13-18 microns, sub-Tremellaceous, 2-4-lobed at apex with each lobe prolonged into a stout sterigma up to 18 x 3 microns and often septate about the middle, but Corner examined a collection with only one kind of basidia, 70-110 x 12.5-14 with 2-4 sterigmata 6-10 x 4-5 microns, the basidia "normally clavate until the emergence of the stout sterigmata caused them to become depressed at the apex and to appear shortly longitudinally (but incompletely) septate"; cystidia none; hymenium thickening to 200 microns, hyphae 2-5 microns wide, "cylindric, not inflated, walls slightly thickened, long-celled", clamped at every septum according to Corner for Clavaria gigaspora, occasionally clamped according to Bourdot & Galzin, or without clamp connections according to Coker, (Corner(2)), spores 12-20 x 5-9 microns, generally elliptic to somewhat spindle-shaped or almond-shaped, borne on basidia that are divided lengthwise, at least near their tips, (Trudell)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with tuber(s)'
SIMILAR  Lentaria byssiseda 'is a rather similar whitish to pinkish-tan species that often grows on wood, has less flattened branches, "normal" basidia, and white mycelial threads at the base of the stalk', (Arora, who discusses its occurrence in California). Tremellodendron species have not been confirmed from the Pacific Northwest - they can be similar but have longitudinally partitioned basidia as well as a typically subgelatinous hymenium with branching hyphidia, lacking cystidia and without clamp connections; the fruitbodies are erect, subfleshy to leathery, and occasionally club-shaped but usually branching freely, and they are extremely variable in habit of growth, color, and size, (Bodman).
SOURCES  Corner(2) (as Aphelaria tuberosa), Arora(1)*, Trudell(4)*, Corner(3), Bodman(1) (regarding Tremellodendron), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*
